# Introductions

Introductions


Ho, Pathfinders!


        When Paizo introduced the vigilante class in Ultimate Intrigue, a lot of players thought they were dreaming: here was a class that could seemingly be turned into anything: if you wanted a rogue, it could out-rogue the rogue with pools of precision damage dice, evasion, infiltration abilities, and all the skills you could shake a stick at; if you wanted a fighter, it could out-fighter the fighter with full BAB, better saves, more out-of-combat utility, and more potential bonus feats even than that superlatively feat-laden class. The vigilante eschewed dogmatic progressions of class features—some of which are stinkers in every class, let’s be honest—in favor of a la carte packages of feats or abilities, including many that conferred mechanical benefits not found in any other ability, feat, or item in the game. It seemed that whatever kind of build you wanted to play, the vigilante offered an option for it that gave you the essential abilities for playing it, all while giving players the opportunity to roleplay as their favorite defenders of justice. The class experienced such a vogue that a joke arose among the community: “How many builds does it take to create a competent Pathfinder PC?” “Just one, but there’s a vigilante archetype that does it better.” The best jokes have an element of truth, it would seem.
        As a person who’s always gravitated toward skillful classes that can accomplish more with guile and finesse than magical prowess or brute strength, the vigilante is an absolute playground of a class—one that I’ve enjoyed exploring time and again in one-shots, Pathfinder Society games, and other campaigns. So regardless of where your vigilante is going to fall on the alignment spectrum, let’s strap on those dark masks, pick a symbol that will strike fear in the hearts of evil-doers (or goody two-shoes—I don’t judge) everywhere, and take a deep dive into the dark side of heroism. Along the way, we’ll learn how to conceal our identities for fun and profit, infiltrate enemy organizations like it’s going out of style, and hopefully drag some baddies into the shadows, never to be seen again. Take your vengeance. Be your vengeance. There’s no better fit for it in Pathfinder.


With best regards for a good game,
Chris (Allerseelen)
________________

---

# VIG 100: The Art of Vendetta

VIG 100: The Art of Vendetta


One of the design choices that I made for my original guide to the Pathfinder inquisitor class and subsequently fell away from was to write a section comparing the class I’m reviewing to other, similar classes in order to figure out more about how it was mechanically similar to and different from those classes. After spending so much time reviewing classes and Pathfinder 1e content, I’ve also begun to develop a sense of...the spirit of a class that I’m reviewing, I guess you might say: a sense of what the class is strong at, what the class is weak at, how the design decisions that Paizo made on everything from armor and weapon proficiencies all the way down to the specific spells on the class’ spell list inform how the class is played. The Vigilante Roles section goes into some detail on this, of course, but that section is designed more as a primer on the mechanics of how to put together different builds, not as a philosophical treatise on what a class is. So starting with this guide (and perhaps going back to revise earlier guides at a later date) I’m going to reinstate this section as we ask the question: what are vigilantes?

---

# The Overview

The Overview


The somewhat cheeky answer to the above question is that vigilantes are simply rogues…as they should have been. And that’s partially true! Stalker vigilantes, especially those with the teisatsu archetype, combine the best elements of the rogue and ninja classes while benefiting from almost a decade of design experience that Paizo had acquired at that point. Freed from some of the traditional constraints of 3.5e roguery and given a buffet of optional class features in the form of social/vigilante talents, it turns out that the class is quite potent! Focusing solely on stalkers ignores avengers, however, who play much more like fighters or slayers than they do like rogues. Rather than point at any specific role or mechanical niche that the vigilante class fills, it’s perhaps most accurate to say that versatility is the theme of the vigilante: while every vigilante will be better at social interaction, infiltration, and espionage than your average Pathfinder class (thanks entirely to the strength of their social talents) their combat roles are many, varied, and unique as fingerprints. They’re neither as powerful as fighters nor as ridiculously flexible as brawlers, yet manage to compete favorably with both classes while retaining significantly more utility and skill outside of combat. Moreover, they have a number of archetypes that radically redefine what the class does, typically through the addition of spellcasting. The hunter, inquisitor, unchained summoner, witch, and wizard spell lists are all available among the vigilante archetypes, so there’s no need to think of it as a purely martial class if you don’t wish to.

---

# Dealing Damage

Dealing Damage


Thanks to their different vigilante specializations, the vigilante class can expect to deal damage in two different ways, depending on their path. Avenger vigilantes play, in essence, like any other full-BAB martial class. No matter whether you’ve built for a two-handed, Strength-based melee build, a sword and board build, a Two-Weapon Fighting build, a Dexterity-based Weapon Finesse build, a ranged build—no matter what happens, you’ll take all the requisite feats (or their equivalent vigilante talents) and just kinda smash. We’ll get into ways and means in VIG 151: Vigilante Roles. If you’re a stalker, well, you should prepare to deal damage in exactly the same way that rogues do—that is, through pools of precision damage dice. Precision damage is finicky in that not all enemies are susceptible to it, and even those who are require you to have debuffed or outmaneuvered them in some way before you get your sweet, sweet hidden strike damage. The list of eligible debuffs or maneuvers is long, but includes mundane flanking (or weirder flanking situations such as Gang Up, Press to the Wall, Improved Outflank, or Circling Mongoose), blinding (typically through the dirty trick combat maneuver, but possibly also through magical darkness or fog), invisibility (a teisatsu vigilante’s vanishing trick, for example), feinting (with the cunning feint vigilante talent), mundane Stealth (hide in plain sight), and some demoralization tactics (notably Shatter Defenses). Since hidden strike will comprise such a large percentage of your damage, it’s unwise to leave your eggs in one basket. Instead, cleave to that old maxim of investment and diversify, diversify, diversify! Make sure you can use at least two routes, preferably three, and tailor your routes to the campaign you’re in—demoralization and feinting builds will have a rough time against undead, for example, since they’re mostly mindless. Beyond those general (and perhaps unhelpful) guidelines of “Play a vigilante like a fighter!” or “Play a vigilante like a rogue!” the same principle holds true as in the section above: vigilantes can be almost anything you want them to be. Vigilante talents are so effective at clearing away prerequisites, granting you bundles of feats, and gifting you bonuses found nowhere else in Pathfinder that you can essentially build whatever you can dream. Except ranged builds. Those are…weirdly unsupported in this class. More on that later.

---

# Tanking Hits

Tanking Hits


        As a martial class, vigilantes don’t have a huge variety of avenues open to them to actively tank hits and stay on their feet under sustained fire. Moreover, even avenger vigilantes are a full-BAB d8 HD class, making them squishier than other full-BAB martials. What this means practically is that you’re going to have to rely mostly on passive bonuses from items or a select few vigilante talents (shield of blades, primarily) to buff your AC. Save buffs are again scant, but companion to the lonely or steely resolve can both work in a pinch, and dips such as scaled fist unchained monk 1 or paladin/antipaladin 2 can go a long way toward shoring up both saves and AC. Unfortunately, acquiring DR, spell resistance, miss chances through concealment, etc. are all out of the question unless you take a spellcasting archetype. As a mostly martial class, you either need Use Magic Device or support from a third party to leverage more exotic forms of defense.
        One element that I feel doesn’t receive enough attention for stalker vigilantes is that they begin the game proficient with medium armor, and can take the armor skin vigilante talent to eliminate both the speed penalty as well as the armor check penalties to every skill they might reasonably need to fulfill their role as a roguelike (i.e., Acrobatics, Escape Artist, and Stealth). Thus, while lightly armored, Dexterity-based stalkers are every bit as iconic as lightly armored, Dexterity-based rogues, you can also build a Strength-based stalker pretty easily, taking the Heavy Armor Proficiency feat (or heavy training vigilante talent) at later levels and wearing mithral full plate, which counts as medium armor for every purpose except whether you’re proficient with it. This improves damage output and makes you less reliant on your hidden strike dice than you would have been otherwise.

---

# Using Skills

Using Skills


        Vigilantes of all stripes, whether avenger or stalker, have huge advantages outside of combat. They receive 6 + Intelligence skill ranks per level, which places them on par with inquisitors, bards, investigators, and other skill monkeys in the the 1e universe. But even though other classes get plenty of ways to improve their skill checks (the inquisitor’s stern gaze, the investigator’s inspiration, etc.) or consolidate their skill ranks (the bard’s versatile performance), it’s not common for them to be introduced to completely different ways to use their skills. Not so for the vigilante, whose social talents are often entire mechanical subsystems on their own: sure, the renown talent line will get you some bonuses to Intimidate, but it also unlocks loyal minions, a Batcave of your very own, discounts at stores, and much more; Disguise may be a strength for the class, but it’s not until the many guises talent line enables you to become anyone at any time that you start to think, “Huh! Maybe there’s really something here.”
        As a heavily Charisma-based class, vigilantes excel in all the areas you would normally expect: Intimidate builds are ludicrously easy to build with frightening appearance, the renown social talent, Dazzling Display, and Signature Skill: Intimidate; likewise, Bluff and Diplomacy are predictable strengths. Once the skill familiarity social talent comes online at 9th level, it’s almost too easy to build for Use Magic Device, as the primary downside of that skill is that you need consistent skill check results to activate wands, scrolls, etc. in combat. With the ability to take 10, even when threatened or rushed, using spell trigger and spell completion items becomes as easy as drawing breath. There are some bonuses out there for Knowledge skills (ancestral enlightenment) and Craft or Profession for mundane item creation (double time, social grace, in vogue, etc.), but the vigilante’s apex of power is found—by a country mile—in Disguise. 
“Disguise?” I hear you ask. “Isn’t that almost always a suboptimal skill choice?” Yep. It sure is. But the keyword is almost, and vigilantes are most certainly the exception to the rule. Thanks in large part to the many guises social talent line, the hidden magic social talent, and your own innate seamless guise bonuses, vigilantes are better suited than just about any class in the game to perform deep-cover infiltrations of enemy organizations. Most classes that focus on infiltration (some inquisitor or bard archetypes, e.g.) require spells or spell-like abilities to inhabit their role fully. Although that may not be a problem in some circumstances, any well-provisioned organization is going to have spellcasters using divination magic to check for imposters, and should a stray greater dispel magic come your way, well, it could be all over but the crying. Vigilantes, on the other hand, accomplish their missions almost entirely non-magically: dual identity, seamless guise, many guises, everyman, any guise, hidden magic, etc. are all extraordinary abilities, meaning that no amount of magical scrutiny will reveal that you’re not who and what you say you are. Pair that with the vigilante’s ability to improve Disguise/Bluff scores and hit skill checks so consistently (thanks to skill familiarity), and it’s not likely that you’ll be found out unless you’re really in the wrong place at the wrong time or fumble your roleplay. The autonomy and longevity of the vigilante’s infiltration abilities are real strengths of the class—so use them!

---

# How’s the Action Economy?

How’s the Action Economy?


As streamlined as you would expect for a mostly martial/rogue-like class with few overt class features, thank you very much. There’s no pressing demand for swift or immediate actions, you don’t have any spells to cast, and everything beyond that point is standard fighter/rogue fare—get into full-attack range and go to work. The one glaring stumbling block in the vigilante’s action economy is their need to transition between identities when fights break out: social identities essentially can’t participate in combat without being recognized as more than the mild-mannered citizens they appear to be, but the transition between social and vigilante identities takes a full minute out of view of anyone else to preserve your secret. If you’re going the Iron Man route, where you don’t care about your social and vigilante identities being tied in the public eye, by all means, feel free to ignore any of the abilities that let you transition between them. If, however, you’d like to keep them separate, the quick change and immediate change vigilante talents should be considered mandatory unless an archetype (mounted fury, e.g.) prevents you from picking them up. There’s no good way to fuse the social and vigilante identities, so the best that can be done is to speed the transition between states.

---

# Anything You’d Change, Allerseelen?

Anything You’d Change, Allerseelen?


Really not! Apart from the inherent thematic silliness of playing both Clark Kent and Superman in the same character and watching no one ever put two and two together, the vigilante is an exceptionally mechanically solid class. The power and flexibility of social and vigilante talents feels highly similar to the design direction that Paizo eventually went in with 2e, and it feels fantastic to be able to put together whatever kind of fighter or rogue you want, complete with great social prowess, good saves, and a bunch of skill ranks. I do wish that vigilantes got more support for ranged combat styles, but that’s something you could potentially homebrew with your GM if you really wanted to.
________________

---

# VIG 101: Class Chassis

VIG 101: Class Chassis


Any deep understanding of a Pathfinder class has to be built on a rock-solid foundation of knowledge about what a class’ features do and don’t do. To that end, it’s become customary in all of my guides to go through the class features of each class with a fine-toothed comb, pointing out rule interactions, errata, and nuances of wording that might have large impacts on the class without being immediately obvious to novice players. Before that, though, we have to delve into what exactly the rubric is for the abilities, items, archetypes, etc. that will be reviewed in this guide.

---

# The Rating System

The Rating System


Throughout this guide, you’ll see feats, spells, class features, etc. rated using a color coding system that was first introduced by Treantmonk and since copied in many other guides. Everyone tends to use their own colors, though, so here’s what we’ll be working with:


Purple ratings are singular, the one ability at a given level or for a given playstyle that is cream of the crop. The apex, the zenith, the pinnacle: purple options are the royalty, the nobility. You will pick purple abilities, and damn the torpedoes.


Blue abilities are supercharged goodness: options that define the way a class is played, or that otherwise provide some exponential leap in effectiveness. Try your utmost to pick up these options.


Green abilities are almost universally useful, a good pick for nearly any build. If they’re not gamebreakingly powerful, they’re definitely holding up the line at a solid B+.


Yellow abilities are our C standard, neither good nor bad. A yellow rating can also denote situational abilities that might be useful to some playstyles but not others.


Red abilities are the traps. Stay away from these.

---

# The Chassis

The Chassis


d8 Hit Die: Vigilantes feel in many respects like spiritual successors to rogues, and as a mostly non-magical striker class, that means that they get a d8 HD. Interestingly, this design decision also extends to avenger vigilantes, making that particular vigilante specialization almost unique in the Pathfinder universe as a d8 HD class with full BAB. (The only other exception that I know of is the trappings of the warrior occultist.) Although a d8 is certainly better than a d6, vigilantes will not hold up under sustained fire as well as other d10 martial classes, so try to get your hits in and then fade back into a protected position whenever you can—being too brave will get you killed.


¾ BAB Progression or Full BAB Progression: Aha, I bet you didn’t see that coming! Or maybe you did, if you’re familiar with the vigilante class even slightly. Vigilantes choose one of two specializations when they start the game: avenger or stalker. Avenger vigilantes receive full BAB progression and their own set of in-your-face combat talents; stalker vigilantes remain a ¾-BAB class with a pool of precision damage dice, similar to rogues with sneak attack, and have their own set of sneaky-stabby talents. Many archetypes trade away vigilante specialization, however, in which case you count as neither an avenger nor a stalker. In these cases, you’ll have ¾ BAB and qualify neither for avenger nor for stalker talents. Archetypes usually have their own sets of talents that they want you to consider anyway.


Skills: Vigilantes receive a generous 6 + Intelligence modifier skill ranks per level, which is on par with most other dedicated skill monkeys in the game, including bards and investigators. Unless your GM is very lenient with point buy, however, you may need to dump Intelligence to accommodate higher Charisma, Wisdom, and Constitution scores; the average vigilante may end up with only 5 skill ranks per level. If your group hasn’t investigated the Background Skills ruleset, I highly recommend you do so, because most classes thrive when they’re able to build in more and more of the flavor that less-optimal skills afford. The skills available to you are detailed below—if it’s italicized, that means that it’s a class skill, conferring a permanent +3 bonus the first time you put a rank in it.


* Perception: Just because you’re a Charisma-based class, did you think you could get away without maximal investment in Perception? Hardly, mes amis, hardly. Without a doubt, Perception is the most important skill in Pathfinder, determining everything from your participation in surprise rounds to your ability to spot loot, traps, and secret doors. How are you going to become vengeance incarnate if you can’t even spot that thug creeping up on you in a dark alley? See, this is how Thomas Wayne died: failed Perception check. Look it up. It’s canon.
* Bluff, Diplomacy, Intimidate, Sense Motive: Which Pathfinder class is mechanically scariest? It’s probably down to either the inquisitor or the vigilante, but on balance (as much as I hate to break with my pro-inquisitor bias) I think the blue ribbon goes to the vigilante. Charisma-based, instead of Wisdom-based; packed with social talents that can improve your Intimidate score and class features that take advantage of scared enemies; designed narratively to strike fear into the hearts of ne’er-do-wells everywhere. Vigilantes will often be expected to be the party face, so gear up, buttercup.
* Disguise: Huh? Disguise rated blue? What’s that all about? Well, when you play a vigilante, you’re not playing one character, you’re technically playing two—and Disguise is how you sidestep a scenario in which those alter egos merge in the public eye. Sure, seamless guise gives you enormous bonuses to prevent anyone from identifying the mild-mannered billionaire Lance Hunt as Captain Amazing, but if you ever need to fight someone or use vigilante talents while in your social identity, you’re going to be incredibly happy that you invested in Disguise. Many guises and other infiltration playstyles will demand maximum investment in Disguise, as well. If you’re playing Iron Man and don’t particularly value the secrecy of your vigilante/social identities, you can dump it.
* Stealth: We all get that grim, silent defenders of justice and liberty are supposed to be stealthy, yes? I don’t need to hammer that point home? Good. Avengers can probably get away with little to no investment in Stealth, since their heavier armor will make them poor at it anyway without the armor skin vigilante talent, but stalkers must absolutely max out ranks here.
* Use Magic Device: Use Magic Device is widely regarded as one of the strongest, most versatile skills in the game because of it allows you to use wands, scrolls, etc. from different classes or even pretend to be a different race, class, alignment, etc. for the purposes of wondrous items. As a Charisma-based class, vigilantes are well-positioned to fool magic items into working for them through sheer force of personality, and with the skill familiarity social talent, they can even take 10 on UMD checks so that they never accidentally fail to activate an item.
* Acrobatics, Escape Artist: While avenger vigilantes can deal with opposed CMB checks and use heavy armor to tank movement-based attacks of opportunity a bit more comfortably, stalkers will absolutely need mechanisms for escaping grapples and avoiding stray hits. Acrobatics and Escape Artist are those mechanisms.
* Disable Device: Vigilantes are generally a rogue-like class, built for infiltration and covert operations. And you’re never going to pass Infiltration 101 if you can’t make it past an average padlock, are you? Gotta graduate from Vigilante College so your dead parents aren’t disappointed in you.
* Knowledge (Arcana, Dungeoneering, Nature, Local, Planes, Religion): Knowledge skills are never wasted skill ranks; vigilantes aren’t ideally suited to making them a primary focus, however—Intelligence is likely to be a dump stat, and your list of class skills would require serious effort to change around. The ancestral enlightenment and well-known expert talents (in conjunction with the aid another action) are probably a good middle ground to shoot for if you want to contribute to Knowledge skill checks.
* Climb, Fly, Swim: Some early ranks here may be merited in order to prevent you from plummeting to your death or drowning, but neither is relevant in the long term, especially if you’re in an archetype that confers some measure of spellcasting ability on you.
* Heal: Heal always has some benefits for any class, but with no Wisdom focus and no class skill, it would be a tough trek.
* Linguistics: The king of the background skills! Linguistics grants more languages as you pump more ranks into it, so it’s one of the few background skills that has immediate, demonstrable benefits to a character. Definitely use one of your two background skill ranks on Linguistics if you’re playing with that ruleset.
* Profession: Profession skills are probably among the better background skills you could have, since Paizo APs often go to some lengths to include Profession checks as alternatives to Knowledge or social skill checks. Not a priority if you’re not playing with background skills, though.
* Spellcraft: As much as I’d love for vigilantes to be great at Spellcraft, that would be a kinder world than the one we live in. With Intelligence as a likely dump stat and no class skill, vigilantes would be fighting an uphill battle to identify spells and the properties of magical items.
* Survival: Survival is more useful than a lot of people give it credit for. If you want to become better at tracking or are playing in a wilderness-heavy game, this is going to be one to consider more strongly.
* Appraise: Of no real mechanical use to vigilantes except as a one-rank background skill.
* Craft: Between the in vogue and double time social talents, vigilantes can actually become weirdly good at mundane crafting, but it’s almost never an optimal build choice.
* Handle Animal, Ride: You’ll know if you’re playing an archetype that needs these skills (mounted fury, e.g.). For most vigilantes, they’re wasted skill ranks.
* Knowledge (Engineering, Geography, History, Nobility): Very, very niche skill checks, and with no innate class skill support, you’re probably better off leaving these to someone else in the party. Engineering and Nobility can both get one rank if you’re playing with Background Skills—to proc ancestral enlightenment, if nothing else.
* Perform: Perhaps useful for a bard gestalt or variant multiclass, but not generally needed.
* Sleight of Hand: Not really necessary unless you plan to be planting evidence on someone.


Armor Proficiencies: Light armor, medium armor, and shields. Pretty good! Dedicated Strength builds will probably go with an avenger specialization, which then unlocks talents like heavy training and armor skin. Dips are also possible to nab heavy armor proficiency, but as a whole the vigilante class is pretty opposed to dips—anything you need, you can get out of a vigilante talent, and you only get those if you stick around in the class.


Weapon Proficiencies: Full simple and martial weapon proficiency certainly gives the vigilante an edge over the rogue! If you want to wield a greatsword, kukri, etc., you’ll appreciate having martial weapon proficiency right off the bat.


Good Reflex and Will saves, Bad Fortitude save: I consider the strong Reflex/Will save progression to be the second best in the game after strong Fortitude/Will. Wherever you stand on the issue, everyone can agree that strong Will saves are by far the most important to have. Fortitude can be compensated for fairly easily, both by boosting your Constitution score at point buy or else by taking the Great Fortitude feat later in the game. A number of spells from allies (delay disease, delay poison, death ward, etc.) can also help bolster you against specific types of Fortitude saves.


Attribute Allocation: Vigilantes are dependent on a bunch of attributes—MAD as all get-out. Strength-based combat builds will probably go for something like Strength > Constitution, Dexterity > Charisma, Wisdom > Intelligence, using the armor skin vigilante and heavy training vigilante talents to keep AC high even with modest Dexterity scores. Dexterity-based builds that use the lethal grace vigilante talent will still be incentivized to retain positive Strength modifiers, which reshuffles the normal priorities for normal Dexterity-based builds: Dexterity > Constitution, Charisma > Strength, Wisdom > Intelligence.

---

# Class Features

Class Features


        (1) Dual Identity (Ex): Dual identity isn’t so much a ratable class feature as it is just a fact of life for the vigilante class. As with any good superhero, you’re living a double life: by day, you’re a mild-mannered citizen, and by night, you run around the streets beating up bad guys (or good guys—I won’t judge) with your bare fists. Assuming that you don’t want people to associate your vigilante identity with your social identity, managing the tension between these two alter egos will be at the core of your playstyle, because the two skillsets are, for the most part, mutually exclusive. If you want to use social skills, well, you’d better be in your social identity, and if you want to use vigilante skills, you’d either better be in your vigilante identity or else really good at Disguise checks. If you’d rather take the Iron Man approach and just broadcast to the whole world that your vigilante and social identities are one and the same, well, you can just stay in your social identity most of the time and ignore dual identity. Let’s hop into the rules text.


A vigilante hides his true identity, allowing him to move about social circles and nobility without carrying the stigma of his ruthless actions. In effect, the vigilante has two identities: one is a polite member of society while the other is a skilled and cunning warrior. To keep up this charade, the vigilante usually has two names: his true name, used in polite company, and his vigilante name, used to strike fear in the hearts of those who oppose him. Knowledge checks about one do not reveal information about the other, unless the vigilante’s true identity revealed to the world at large.


        Sure, yes, Knowledge checks don’t reveal information about the one from the other. It’d be pretty weird if someone said, “Hey, you know Bruce Wayne?” and all of a sudden the other person was like, “Bruce Wayne? Sure, I know…hey, wait a minute! He’s Batman!” It’s hilarious to imagine how the plot of The Dark Knight would have unfolded had that been the case, but fortunately, that’s not the story here.


The vigilante can start each day in either of his identities, referred to simply as social or vigilante. Changing from one identity to another takes 1 minute and must be done out of sight from other creatures to preserve the vigilante’s secret. Changing identities is more than just changing outfits and clothing (although that is certainly a part of it); the process often also involves applying make-up, altering his hair, and adjusting other personal effects. Furthermore, the change is as much a state of mind as of body, so items such as a hat of disguise and similar spells and effects that change the user’s appearance do not reduce the time required to change identities. Most social talents require the vigilante to be in his social identity, but a vigilante who uses vigilante talents in his social identity risks exposing his secret.


        Changing between your identities takes a full minute, which, while only mildly annoying in normal play, becomes a real time crunch when you need to hop into an unexpected combat. Ironically, this makes vigilantes somewhat weaker at intrigue play than other classes, because they can’t go from social interaction to sudden but inevitable betrayal without hiding behind a tapestry for a minute while they get themselves in the appropriate Uta Hagen headspace to start living their double life. For this reason, I highly, highly recommend that you take the quick change social talent at 7th level, and possibly the immediate change social talent at 13th level, as well. Quick change reduces your changing time to a full-round action, while immediate change will further reduce it to a move action. You can’t reliably use the two halves of the vigilante class together if you want to maintain secrecy, so the best that can be done in most cases is to make the transition between the two halves more streamlined or to ignore the delineation altogether and play the whole game in your social identity.


Despite being a single person, a vigilante’s dual nature allows him to have two alignments, one for each of his identities. When in an identity, he is treated as having that identity’s alignment for all spells, magic items, and abilities that rely on alignment. For the purpose of meeting a qualification for a feat, class, or any ability, he is only eligible if both of his alignments meet the requirements. A vigilante’s two alignments cannot be more than one step from each other on a single alignment axis. For example, a vigilante with a lawful neutral social identity could have a vigilante identity that is lawful good, lawful neutral, lawful evil, neutral, neutral good, or neutral evil. If a vigilante is the target of an effect that would change his alignment, it changes both of his alignments to the new alignment.


        Vigilante multiclass builds can be a nightmare when they interact with alignment-locked classes such as barbarian, monk, cleric, paladin, antipaladin, etc., because you have to think about how each of your two identities is going to interact with those classes. Some vigilante archetypes (cabalist, zealot, warlock, psychometrist, etc.) also turn you into a spellcaster of some stripe, and spellcasters can occasionally mess up their alignments with good, evil, lawful, or chaotic spells. That’s pretty rare, fortunately, but still worth keeping an eye on.


Any attempts to scry or otherwise locate the vigilante work only if the vigilante is currently in the identity the creature is attempting to locate (or if the creature knows that the two identities are the same individual). Otherwise, the spell or effect has no effect, revealing nothing but darkness, as if the target were invalid or did not exist.


        The final paragraph in dual identity’s rules text simply outlines some of the defenses that vigilantes have against high-level divination effects. Under no circumstances would you want spellcasters to be able to use greater scrying, locate creature, etc. to find you in your social identity based on your vigilante identity, or vice versa.


        (1) Seamless Guise (Ex): Seamless guise is the backstop that ensures that most enemies (barring, I don’t know, inquisitors who are 10 levels higher than you are) can’t figure out your little dual identity ruse. Circumstance bonuses stack with everything except other circumstance bonuses from the same source, so there shouldn’t be any overlap issues with feats or buffs that affect Disguise, but you do need to remember one crucial bit of information: seamless guise does not protect you if you use vigilante talents in your social identity. How exactly your GM interprets the clause (found in the vigilante talent rules text) will determine a lot—if you have the magical familiarity vigilante talent as a gnome, do you automatically lose your seamless guise bonuses just for casting a cantrip, when everyone would know that gnomes have some racial magic? Or if you have the shadow’s sight vigilante talent but can see in the dark as a human, would that trigger a Disguise check? You’ll probably want to communicate with your GM about how often they’re going to call for Disguise checks, and how ruthlessly they’re going to interpret seamless guise, as that might inform how much you invest in certain social talents. If you’re going to ignore the social/vigilante identity divide, then seamless guise doesn’t do much for you, as you’re not attempting to conceal the fact that you’re the vigilante everyone’s been looking for.


A vigilante knows how to behave in a way that appears perfectly proper and normal for his current identity. Should anyone suspect him of being anything other than what he appears to be while either in his social or vigilante identity, he can attempt a Disguise check with a +20 circumstance bonus to appear as his current identity, and not as his other identity.


        (1) Social Talents: Social talents are your bread and butter abilities for skills, social interaction, utility, disguise, and downtime. One thing that you’ll notice if you do a quick search on the Archives of Nethys feat page is that there is no means short of the halfling favored class bonus of acquiring more social or vigilante talents. Nope, no “Extra Social Talent” feat, and no “Extra Vigilante Talent” feat—they would be far too powerful for that. Instead, all you have to work with is the progression that you’re given at every odd level, and you can’t go back and revise choices that you’ve made. Consider every option carefully, and make every level count!


        (1) Vigilante Specialization (Ex): Your vigilante specialization determines which class your vigilante is going to draw inspiration from. If you choose to be an avenger, that class is going to be the fighter; if you choose to be a stalker, that class is going to be the rogue/ninja. Both have different complements of vigilante talents available to them that encourage different styles of play, so the choice of specialization is one of the biggest decisions you’ll ever make when building a vigilante.


At 1st level, a vigilante must choose to be either an avenger or a stalker. An avenger gains a base attack bonus equal to his vigilante level instead of using those listed on Table 1–1. He adds this value to any other base attack bonus gained from other classes or racial Hit Dice as normal.


        Woo, full BAB for avengers! To my knowledge, trappings of the warrior occultists are the only other characters that can get full BAB as a d8 Hit Die class, so you should take this class feature as the blessing that it is. Full BAB means faster access to many combat feats, better chances to hit with attacks, more damage from Power Attack or Deadly Aim, better CMD to resist combat maneuvers, and more.


A stalker gains an ability called hidden strike, which allows him to deal an extra 1d8 points of precision damage on melee attacks (or ranged attacks from within 30 feet) against foes who are unaware of his presence, who consider him an ally, or who are made flat-footed by startling appearance (see page 17). This extra damage increases by 1d8 at 3rd level and every 2 vigilante levels thereafter. A stalker vigilante can also deal hidden strike damage to a target that he is flanking or that is denied its Dexterity bonus to AC, but in these cases, the damage dice are reduced to d4s. A stalker can deal hidden strike damage against targets with concealment (but not total concealment).


        Stalkers, meanwhile, get a pseudo-clone of the rogue’s sneak attack class feature. You deal a bit more damage under limited circumstances, including (typically) attacking a foe at the start of combat, but a bit less damage against foes who are already aware that you’re there. Stalker vigilantes will probably deal slightly less damage than rogues in the long run, but the difference isn’t huge. Of note, the final sentence of the paragraph indicates that stalkers get the equivalent of the Shadow Strike feat for free, enabling them to use hidden strike on creatures who are under the effects of blur, concealed by obscuring mist or fog cloud, shrouded in dim light, etc.; the strike the unseen vigilante talent will let you use hidden strike on targets with total concealment, as well.


Once this choice is made, it can’t be changed. While many vigilante talents are usable by both specializations, some are unique to each specialization. A stalker vigilante can apply only one talent marked with an asterisk (*) to a given hidden strike, and only when that hidden strike is dealt against a foe that is unaware of the stalker vigilante’s presence (or who considers him an ally), unless otherwise noted.


Unlike rogues, who get to apply sneak attack talents every time they sneak attack an enemy, stalker vigilantes can only apply their special hidden strike talents when they’re attacking someone from stealth or betrayal. This isn’t huge, but it does make vigilante talents that modify hidden strike much less worthy of your purchase than comparable talents would be for a rogue.


(2) Vigilante Talents: Now we’re talking. Vigilante talents comprise all of the abilities that relate directly to combat, and y’all, let me tell you, these puppies are mean. Some vigilante talents will grant you between two and four feats’ worth of value, while others will poach class features from different classes or even grant effects that are available nowhere else in the game. There’s a reason that no effect in the game apart from the Past Your Limits alternate capstone allows you to gain more vigilante talents—they would be too over-the-top powerful to hand out willy-nilly.


Starting at 2nd level and every 2 levels thereafter, a vigilante gains a vigilante talent. Unless otherwise noted, a talent can be selected only once. Some talents require the vigilante to meet prerequisites before they can be chosen, such as possessing another vigilante talent, possessing a particular specialization, or attaining a minimum level. Once a talent has been selected, it can’t be changed.


        You can’t swap once you’ve chosen. Choose wisely.


If the vigilante uses any of these talents while in his social identity, he must succeed at a Disguise check against the Perception checks of all onlookers (without the +20 circumstance bonus from seamless guise) or the onlookers will realize that he is more than his social identity appears to be and perhaps discover the social and vigilante identities are one and the same. If a talent calls for a saving throw, the DC for that save is equal to 10 + 1/2 the vigilante’s level + the vigilante’s Charisma modifier, unless stated otherwise.


If you intend to keep your identities separate, you’ll want to check with your GM about how they intend to rule on which vigilante talents would trigger a Perception check. Unless you’re extraordinarily skilled at Disguise, Perception checks will regularly beat your DCs, and once the secret’s out, the music stops. This paragraph is a good argument for saving one of the slots you get with the 9th-level skill familiarity social talent for Disguise—you don’t want to accidentally roll a natural 1 and give yourself away when low-level commoners are watching you get ambushed in your social identity. Of course, the option is always there to let your social identity predominate, in which case feel free to go all John Wick on people with no preamble.


(3) Unshakable (Ex): Cue the Kimmy Schmidt theme song, eh? Anyway, increasing the DCs for enemies to Intimidate you is horribly, horribly underpowered. I can think of…yeah, nope, zero times that this has ever happened to me or any of the players I know.


(5) Startling Appearance (Ex): Stalker and avenger vigilantes are going to care about startling appearance for entirely different reasons. Stalker vigilantes will care about startling appearance because it lets them trigger their hidden strikes for an entire round, dealing a bunch of precision damage; avenger vigilantes will mostly care about forcing enemies to take a -4 penalty to attack them in the first round of combat. In either case, it’s a pretty good class feature—nothing amazing, but also not worthless.


At 5th level, a vigilante learns to use the element of surprise to his advantage, startling foes that are unaware of his presence. Whenever a vigilante with this ability attempts an attack against a foe that is completely unaware of the vigilante’s presence (usually due to Stealth or invisibility), the foe is treated as flat-footed for the rest of the vigilante’s turn (uncanny dodge or a similar ability prevents this effect unless the vigilante is at least 4 levels higher than the foe with uncanny dodge). The foe also takes a –4 penalty on attacks made against the vigilante until the start of the vigilante’s next turn. 


The important thing to notice about startling appearance is that an enemy must be completely unaware of your presence to use it. I don’t know of any official ruling about what happens if you use hide in plain sight, vanish, invisibility, etc. to go back into invisibility or stealth, but I would think that an enemy knows you’re there at that point—they just can’t locate you. Talk to your GM about their interpretation, though.


(11) Frightening Appearance (Ex): Frightening appearance is kind of like a miniature Dazzling Display. It’s fine, but the radius is pretty small, you’re not guaranteed to have built for Intimidate, enemies become immune after you use it once, and it’s a mind-affecting fear effect, which means that a large-ish group of enemies (constructs, oozes, vermin, undead, etc.) will be flat-out immune. A good rule of thumb for vigilante archetypes is that surrendering anything other than social and vigilante talents won’t impact the build much.


At 11th level, whenever a vigilante with this ability makes an attack against a foe that is unaware of the vigilante’s presence (see startling appearance above), the vigilante can, as a free action, attempt an Intimidate check to demoralize the target of his attack and any enemies within 10 feet who can see the attack. This check is attempted before the attack roll against the foe is made and resolved. The vigilante rolls only one Intimidate check and applies the result to all the targets. If the check succeeds against the target of the vigilante’s attack, that foe is also frightened for 1 round, in addition to being shaken as normal, unless the foe succeeds at a Will save (DC = 10 + 1/2 the vigilante’s class level + his Charisma modifier).


Free action, 10-foot radius, and only usable against foes that are totally unaware of your presence. The Will save negates only the frightened condition, not the shaken condition that accompanies a successful use of the Intimidate skill to demoralize a foe.


Once a creature has been the target of this ability (either as the target of the attack or as a nearby creature, regardless of whether or not it was successful), it is immune to that vigilante’s frightening appearance for 24 hours. This effect is in addition to the bonuses gained from the startling appearance ability. This is a mind-affecting fear effect. 


Mind-affecting and fear tags limit your target selection, but them’s the breaks with Intimidate. And since you can’t target the same enemy with frightening appearance multiple times, it doesn’t truly matter whether startling appearance can be triggered multiple times in the same combat.


(17) Stunning Appearance (Ex): The stunned condition is brutal, of course, especially at this high of a level. Any enemy that fails their save will be dead before the status condition ever wears off. On the flip side, nearly every enemy is guaranteed to have more HD than you by this point, which essentially grants a blanket +4 to any enemy you use stunning appearance on. You’re unlikely to get a failed save except on a natural 1 from most enemies.


At 17th level, a vigilante can leave his foes unable to respond when he appears. Whenever a vigilante with this ability makes a successful attack against a foe that is unaware of the vigilante’s presence (see startling appearance), the foe must make a successful Will save (DC = 10 + 1/2 the vigilante’s class level + his Charisma modifier) or be stunned until the end of the vigilante’s next turn. If the creature has more HD than the vigilante’s class level, it receives a +4 circumstance bonus on this save. This ability is in addition to the effects of startling appearance and frightening appearance. Once a creature has been the target of this ability (regardless of whether or not the saving throw is successful), it is immune to that vigilante’s stunning appearance for 24 hours. This is a mind-affecting fear effect. 


(20) Vengeance Strike (Ex): Vengeance strike is fun, and definitely plays into the whole Batman feel of the class. I’d gladly trade it out for Perfect Body, Flawless Mind or Past Your Limits, though.


At 20th level, a vigilante can study a target to strike more viciously. He can spend up to 5 standard actions, which all must be in consecutive rounds. On the following round, he can make a vengeance strike against the studied creature. The target must remain unaware of the vigilante’s presence (or not see him as a threat) until the attack. For each round spent studying the target, the vigilante grants himself one of the following benefits on his vengeance strike: gain a +4 circumstance bonus on the attack roll, deal an additional 3d6 points of precision damage if he hits, or treat the attack roll as if it were 2 higher for the purposes of determining whether the attack hit or threatens a critical hit (to maximum effective roll of 20). Each round of study can be spent in a different way or the same way (they stack with each other), but each round of study must be allocated before the attack roll is made. If the vigilante is an avenger, he automatically confirms any critical hits threatened by his vengeance strike. If the vigilante is a stalker and would normally be able to add a special effect from a vigilante talent with an asterisk (*) to this attack, he can instead add two special effects to his vengeance strike.


Alternate Capstones: But wait, there’s more! Chronicle of Legends introduced alternative capstones that you can take instead of your normal capstone. Each class has one class-exclusive capstone, and then several others that are more general for all characters, all spellcasters, etc. Do note that you can’t select an alternative capstone if your archetype replaces the vengeance strike class feature. Here are my ratings:


* Past Your Limits (Ex): You can’t get more vigilante talents by any means short of this capstone, and you can’t get more social talents by any means short of this capstone or…being a halfling? Favored class bonuses are weird, is the moral of the story. Anyway, being able to grab talents outside of your specialization is a wonderful idea.
* Perfect Body, Flawless Mind (Ex): PBFM is usually a fantastic capstone, and there’s no denying that grabbing a +8 to your Strength or Dexterity scores (whichever is your main combat attribute) will leave you far deadlier than you were previously. You’ll need to weigh the benefit of these stat increases with the benefit of getting more social and vigilante talents with Past Your Limits in order to arrive at a decision that’s best for your character—there’s not really a one-size-fits-all answer.
* With This Sword (Ex): A good runner-up to PBFM, but still inferior to it.
* The Boss (Ex) and Won’t Stay Dead (Ex): More story points than anything else—fitting sunsets for your characters. They won’t have overwhelming effects in combat.
________________

---

# VIG 151: Vigilante Roles

VIG 151: Vigilante Roles


        It’s become customary in my guides for me to lay out the roles—both in-combat and out-of-combat—that Pathfinder classes can become great at. The vigilante is above all a modular class: everyone receives certain basics like startling appearance, dual identity, and seamless guise, but beyond that, the class (whether for a rogue-like stalker or a fighter-like avenger) is whatever you decide you want it to be. And that’s pretty cool! I can come up with a build for everything from a silent, shuriken-slinging ninja to a raging behemoth wielding a greatsword with equal ease in this class. Ya just gotta know whatcha want.

---

# Combat Roles

Combat Roles


        “Combat roles” refers to something that every class needs to figure out: how am I going to deal damage in combat? Full casters can often get away without any reliable means of dealing damage in combat, provided that they can offer enough buffing, debuffing, summoning, battlefield control, etc. to support the team anyway, but ¾-BAB strikers or ⅔ casters must often juggle twin mandates: deal effective damage, and offer spellcasting or skill functionality outside of combat. Much of a class’ suitedness to certain combat roles is decided by their weapon and armor proficiencies, as well as—in the case of ¾-BAB classes—their ability to debuff or self-buff in order to soften enemies up a little for their invariably lower attack rolls. In the next sections, we’ll spend some time examining the vigilante’s facility with each role, and talk about opportunity costs you might face for specializing in one thing over another. With respect to attributes, you can expect to build vigilantes very similarly: STR > CON, CHA > DEX, WIS > INT for Strength-based melee vigilantes wearing medium or heavy armor, and DEX > CON, CHA > STR, WIS > INT for Dexterity-based melee vigilantes or ranged vigilantes wearing light armor. Owing to the vicissitudes of the lethal grace vigilante talent, it does behoove melee vigilantes who use Dexterity as their attack stat not to dump Strength, so keep that in mind as you’re building.

---

# Melee (STR, Two-Handed)

Melee (STR, Two-Handed)


        If you’re thinking of a classic Strength build for your PC, the avenger vigilante specialization is the logical move. Avengers get full BAB, and they’ve got a number of exclusive talents that predispose them to lower Dexterity scores, higher Strength scores, and more iterative attacks. Shield of blades is the ideal 2nd-level vigilante talent to take as a two-handed Strength build, because it gives you both offense (free Power Attack, and the chance to retrain that feat if you already took it at 1st level) and defense (shield bonuses to AC that scale with your Power Attack attack penalty). All you truly need to get started down this path is Power Attack and heavy armor proficiency, making it an ideal path for newcomers to Pathfinder.

---

# Melee (STR, Sword and Board)

Melee (STR, Sword and Board)


        Sword and shield builds don’t seem to be as popular as two-handed builds in the Pathfinder 1e meta. I can think of a few reasons for that: first, damage is king in high-level Pathfinder play—you either kill an enemy quickly, or they kill you quicker. Two-handed Strength builds do more damage than any other melee playstyle, so it’s QED that you’d want to build for massive damage and cut your opponents down before they get the chance to act. Second, AC (the main selling point of a shield, no?) is one of the least important defensive metrics in Pathfinder at high levels. Enemy attack bonuses scale pretty hard, and there will come a time in most players’ careers when monsters would have to roll exceptionally poorly to miss a hit. Shield bonuses always help, of course, but for the most part you’re better off building for DR, saving throws, miss chances from concealment, etc. than continuing to invest large amounts of gold for 1 or 2 more points of AC. Third, sword and board builds require double the financial investment of two-handed builds. Instead of just enchanting a weapon and armor, you’re now enchanting a weapon, armor, and a shield. Gold spent on those items is gold not spent elsewhere. Finally, if you’re going to actually fight with both a sword and shield, you’re looking at a feat-heavy and attribute-thin build. To retain your shield bonus to AC, you’ll need Improved Shield Bash; if you want to debuff or use combat maneuvers as you shield bash, you might need Stumbling Bash, Toppling Bash, or Shield Slam; Two-Weapon Fighting and its older cousins will be needed to keep your iteratives up to speed; and Shield Master is particularly nice after BAB +11. The TWF feats require Dexterity 15, 17, and 19, respectively, however, which isn’t exactly an easy sell when you’re trying to work in high Strength bonuses.
        Where vigilante sword and board builds get a bit of a boost, though, is from their vigilante talents. They can’t solve any of the first three points above—damage is still king, AC still loses at high levels, and you still have to spend money on a good shield—but they can significantly ease the pain of #4. The most essential vigilante talent to have for these builds is shield of fury. In addition to giving you Improved Shield Bash and Two-Weapon Fighting for free, shield of fury also permits you to ignore the Dexterity requirements of any feat that lists Two-Weapon Fighting as a prerequisite, including Double Slice, Heaven’s Step, Two-Weapon Rend, or anything else you need. To recap: two feats for free and the ability to piledrive all your attribute investment into Strength is a huge benefit. So use it!
        One fun build that I haven’t seen done (but will probably create in the Builds section of this guide) is a build that utilizes Shield Gauntlet Style with shield of fury. Shield Gauntlet Style turns a gauntlet into the equivalent of a buckler, which at 6th level can be used with Two-Weapon Fighting and Improved Shield Bash via shield of fury. Where it really gets fun is the steel soldier vigilante talent + Shielded Gauntlet Master: the former allows you to apply your armor’s enhancement bonus as a weapon enhancement bonus on a gauntlet, and the latter says that any weapon enhancement bonus you have on your gauntlets also counts as a shield enhancement bonus. This neatly solves #3 above, since you’re using the enchantment costs of one piece of equipment (armor) to grant you enhancement bonuses to (effectively) two other pieces of equipment (gauntlet as shield and gauntlet as weapon).

---

# Melee (STR, Two-Weapon Fighting)

Melee (STR, Two-Weapon Fighting)


        Woah, woah, woah! Don’t let that red rating fool you: vigilantes, especially avengers, are as effective as other classes at Two-Weapon Fighting playstyles if you can get the right feats to them. The problem is that vigilantes don’t have extra feats to spare—barring those granted by vigilante talents—and their vigilante talent lineup bizarrely only supports sword and board builds. Shield of fury only grants Two-Weapon Fighting and the ability to ignore Dexterity prerequisites if one of your weapons is a shield, and there aren’t any other TWF vigilante talents. My advice would therefore be to make peace with the sword and board TWF build (it’s not so bad—check out the Bladecatcher build below the fold!)

---

# Melee (STR/DEX, Unarmed Strike)

Melee (STR/DEX, Unarmed Strike)


        Curiously enough, unarmed strikes and natural weapons are some of the combat roles that vigilantes do best at, and both Strength and Dexterity routes are eminently achievable thanks to the wonders of vigilante talents. The first question for pretty much any unarmed strike build is, “Could a dip into monk or unchained monk improve what I’ve got here?” The question is non-negligible: a one-level dip into the scaled fist unchained monk archetype gets you +2 Fortitude and Reflex, +1 BAB, Charisma to AC when unarmored, three free feats (Improved Unarmed Strike, Stunning Fist, and your wildcard feat—probably Dragon Style), and the ability to flurry for an extra attack; a two-level dip will get you evasion and another free feat (probably Dodge or Intimidating Prowess). That’s not nothing! Under normal circumstances, you might consider progressing far enough into the monk class (3rd level for core monks; 4th level for unchained monks) to get the still mind class feature, then later branch into the Monastic Legacy feat, don a monk’s robe, and enjoy scaling unarmed strike damage that’s almost as good as a single-class monk’s. Alas, the scaled fist archetype in both classes gives up still mind, so one- or two-level dips are pretty much the rule. Okayo corsair swashbuckler dips can also be worth it if you want to pursue combat feats with high mental stat requirements (lots of style feats and combat maneuver feats require some amount of mental stat investment) but don’t want the hassle of, ya know, actually being intelligent or wise. Right! On to vigilantes themselves.
If you’re going to build an avenger, Strength is probably your best bet. Dragon Style, granted for free with a one-level scaled fist dip, relies on good Strength scores to deal more damage, and it’s far and away the easiest stat to build around for melee combat. Pick up the fist of the avenger vigilante talent and some enchantable handwraps, and voilà! You’ve got a pretty effective unarmed striker right off the bat. It probably won’t deal sickening levels of damage, but it’ll be passable. The less conventional route is to build entirely for Dexterity and Charisma, take the same one-level scaled fist unchained monk dip (or possibly two-level, to score that sweet stalker-exclusive evasion class feature) and pick up the lethal grace vigilante talent. Although you’ll still use Strength as your damage stat, you’ll get to add half your vigilante level to that damage, scaling nicely for free as you grow. If you have the scratch, of course, you’re welcome to just purchase agile handwraps and add your Dexterity modifier to damage without waiting on lethal grace’s scaling. Either works! You’ll have better AC, better Reflex saves, and better initiative, all without a huge hit to damage.
Stalkers do unarmed builds well enough, especially if they follow the Dexterity recipe above; given that every level away from vigilante is a level that they’re not progressing their hidden strike damage, though, it’s a harder sell than for an avenger. I’d still consider a one-level scaled fist unchained monk dip—it’s that good—but without fist of the avenger, you need your hidden strike damage all the more. The teisatsu vigilante archetype unfortunately doesn’t grant access to ninja master tricks, which would permit you to grab unarmed combat mastery, so build an unarmed stalker like you would any other stalker…or look to natural weapons builds, which are where the real sauce is hidden for stalkers.

---

# Melee (STR/DEX, Natural Weapons)

Melee (STR/DEX, Natural Weapons)


        Natural attack builds aren’t altogether common in Pathfinder outside of specific builds like beastmorph alchemists, but in the correct hands (or talons) they can be devastating. Although natural attacks generally sport smaller damage dice than manufactured weapons and can take both attack and damage penalties if they’re classified as secondary natural attacks, they often have the advantage of coming online faster than BAB-based iterative attacks would progress; moreover, with the addition of the Multiattack feat, you could have a theoretically infinite number of primary and secondary natural attacks, all of which hit at BAB or BAB - 2, ignoring the usual -5/-10/-15 format for iterative attacks.
Practically, these features mean that natural attack builds are incredible for rogue-like classes with high Dexterity scores and sources of precision damage. After all, what do these classes want to do? Full-attack enemies, dealing as much precision damage as possible. How do they do that? Get better battlefield positioning tools; get better ways to proc precision damage; get more attacks; get better attack bonuses on the attacks that they have. What prevents them from doing that? Battlefield positioning and tools to deny enemies Dexterity to AC are widely pursued by these classes, so the rate-limiting factor is generally their ¾ BAB progression, which limits iterative attacks and attack bonuses at the same time. Natural attack builds frontload the number of attacks you can make (three attacks or more are typically available in short order, whereas an iterative attack build would have to wait until 15th level for three attacks) and, per Multiattack, permit your secondary natural attacks to hit at only BAB - 2—and that’s usually on top of any other weapon attacks you may make with your iteratives. A 15th-level stalker vigilante can attack three times with a weapon at +11/+6/+1; a 15th-level stalker vigilante with morphic weaponry and Multiattack can attack three times with a weapon at +11/+6/+1 and an additional four times at +9/+9/+9/+9 with their natural attacks. Who cares what kind of static damage you’re doing with Strength/Dexterity bonuses at that point? If you hit an enemy with a full-attack and proc your hidden strike damage, those extra four attacks will likely be the decisive factor in whether an enemy lives to see the next combat round or gets eviscerated right then and there. Now, individual classes and races don’t typically have a means of acquiring more than one, maybe two natural weapons, but vigilantes have two routes open to them that collectively grant four natural attacks in addition to weapon attacks: the Chu Ye enforcer route and the morphic weaponry route.
        The Chu Ye enforcer route, appropriately enough, involves taking the Chu Ye enforcer archetype. In addition to dumping some strong bonuses to unarmed attacks into your lap, Chu Ye enforcers get access to what is essentially at-will alter self, giant form I, and giant form II. Each of these polymorph spells has forms that grant three natural attacks, plus a helmet gore for a fourth. Polymorph spells are generally pretty good for hybrid natural attack/unarmed strike builds, since amulets of mighty fists meld with your body for the duration of the spell but continue providing their bonuses to attack and damage, unlike manufactured weapons. A scaled fist unchained monk 1 / Chu Ye enforcer stalker vigilante 4 who turns into a lizardfolk berserker can have five attacks by 5th level (flurry of blows made as kicks +4/+4, bite -1, and two claws -1; adding the Multiattack feat improves the bite and claws to +2) which all but assures an enemy’s death if you’re able to land even three of your five. If you don’t like the Chu Ye enforcer route—and it does surrender quite a few vigilante talents, which not everyone may be fond of—the simplest way to flex into natural attacks is through the morphic weaponry route. This one is dead simple: take the malleable flesh vigilante talent, then the morphic weaponry vigilante talent. For a standard stalker build, I’d recommend lethal grace (2nd) → malleable flesh (4th) → morphic weaponry (6th) to pick up your third natural attack right at 6th. You’ll get your final natural attack at 15th level. Assuming you’re attacking with weapons, too, your morphic weapons will all be classified as secondary natural attacks, so be sure to pick up Multiattack to reduce your penalties to -2 instead of -5. Both of these routes add huge damage to stalker vigilantes if you can spare one feat (Multiattack) and either two vigilante talents (malleable flesh + morphic weaponry) or an archetype (Chu Ye enforcer).

---

# Melee (DEX, Finesse)

Melee (DEX, Finesse)


        Regardless of how you get there, all finesse builds converge toward a single purpose: to use Dexterity, not Strength, as your attack and damage stat. Done well, this role has plenty of advantages: better Reflex saves and initiative bonuses, comparable AC to more heavily armored builds without the armor check or movement penalties that heavy armor confers, and a wider array of useful skill bonuses, to name just a few. Generally speaking, stalker vigilantes will be much more interested in finesse builds than avenger vigilantes will be, although there’s more cross-pollination (Strength-based stalkers and Dexterity-based avengers) possible in the vigilante class than in other classes. We’ve got three workable routes to build a finesse vigilante: lethal grace, Grace feats, or agile weaponry. Each of them will obviously cost you something, whether feats, vigilante talents, or monetary resources.
        Starting with the simplest, lethal grace builds are stupidly easy to create. Take Weapon Finesse at 1st level so that you’re not using your (likely abysmal) Strength score for your attack bonuses in the first level of a campaign, then grab the lethal grace vigilante talent at 2nd level, retraining Weapon Finesse into whatever feat you like most. Your damage bonus will scale with your vigilante level, and you get to use your Dexterity to attack. Easy peasy. One quirk of lethal grace is that you still use your Strength score for damage, so try not to dump Strength down the toilet at character creation—a 10 is fine, but a 12 or even a 14 is better if you can spare the point buy.
        Another monetarily free (but otherwise costly) option is to get the Weapon Finesse + Weapon Focus + [Weapon] Grace feats. Although not every feat in the [Weapon] Grace category actually has the word “Grace” in the title, it’s a general appellation to refer to feats that allow you to add your Dexterity modifier to damage. The only feats in this category, to the best of my knowledge, include Fencing Grace (rapier), Slashing Grace (any one-handed slashing weapon), Starry Grace (starknife), Dervish Dance (scimitar), and Bladed Brush + Slashing Grace (glaive). If not for the Weapon Focus feat tax or the highly weapon-specific nature of the feats, I’d recommend this route more enthusiastically, but as it is, lethal grace just steals the limelight with its flexibility, ease of use, and scaling damage. Three feats is a big ask in a class that receives no bonus feats.
        Finally, you can always take Weapon Finesse and purchase an agile weapon by sacrificing a +1 from your enhancement bonus. Your list of available weapons diminishes even further here from every Finesse-able weapon (lethal grace) to every weapon of a specific type ([Weapon] Grace) to one specific weapon (agile). It’s there as an option if you need it, and I do believe it’s generally superior to getting [Weapon] Grace, but the lethal grace vigilante talent is still head and shoulders better than agile weaponry.

---

# Ranged (DEX)

Ranged (DEX)


        Holy cannoli, did someone at Paizo forget to send a few vigilante talents to the printer!? For a class that prides itself on being able to facilitate nearly any combat build under the sun, vigilantes get almost zero support for ranged builds. I mean, there’s the tragic gunmaster and warlock archetypes, if you want to roleplay a person who stinks at everything, and some minor support for thrown weapon builds through the teisatsu archetype and returning weapon vigilante talent. If you want to be a boilerplate archer, though? Zilch, zip, nada. Even the volatile arrows vigilante talent, while powerful, is dramatically limited by its uses per day. Go be a zen archer monk, pistolero or bolt ace gunslinger, hinterlander prestige class, or base slayer if you want archery so badly.

---

# Ancillary Roles

Ancillary Roles


Ancillary roles are the roles that Pathfinder characters might expect to play outside of their primary combat or casting roles, including scouting/reconnaissance, anticaster functions, skill monkeying or Knowledge jockeying, non-magical healing, etc. Only in rare instances would you want to make these roles the main focus of a build, but some amount of support for them helps to create a well-rounded character that can contribute off the battlefield as well as on. 




Face


        “The face” is a generic term for the party member who handles most of the team’s social interaction. Whether that’s a Diplomacy check to sway an unfriendly NPC, a Bluff check to explain why the party was somewhere they weren’t supposed to be, or an Intimidate check to subtly intimate that terrible things might happen if an NPC was ever to double-cross you, the face role is ideally suited for vigilantes. You’re a Charisma-heavy class; you’ve got tons of social talents that amplify your social abilities, such as feign innocence, renown, skill familiarity, social grace, and subjective truth; and you’ve even got plenty of incentives to invest in combat builds that use those skills, such as feint builds (Bluff) and Shatter Defenses or Dazzling Display builds (Intimidate). Only you can know whether your party needs face skills, and if so, which ones, but suffice it to say that vigilantes can stand with the best of them—their skill familiarity social talent in particular lets them achieve shockingly consistent results even in heated conversations or interrogations, which is a boon when you have a good skill bonus but a low roll would still result in a failure.

---

# Feinting

Feinting


        But speaking of combat builds that involve face skills, let’s. In general, avengers have the luxury of not needing to care about feinting, demoralizing, etc., because their role is simple: get into the brawl and smash. For stalkers, though, it is essential that you have multiple ways to proc hidden strike. Teisatsu builds with vanishing trick are great, and you’ll never regret having a feat like Gang Up or Press to the Wall that allows you to flank more effectively. Ultimately, however, you need a method of proc’ing hidden strike that doesn’t rely on expendable resources (ki points from vanishing trick) or enemy/ally positioning (flanking). More esoteric tactics like darkness (Eclipsed Spell + continual flame, potentially), fog (smokesticks + ashen path, a goz mask, or fog-cutting lenses), or the Circling Mongoose feat might work, but the three workhorses most stalkers will use (beyond invisibility and flanking) are dirty tricks, feinting, and demoralizing. Dirty tricks (combat maneuvers generally, really) are a beast of their own, so we’ll skip those for now.
        Feinting compares the result of your Bluff check against 10 + an enemy’s BAB + an enemy’s Wisdom modifier. If you succeed at this standard action feint, they lose their Dexterity bonus against your next attack, allowing you to hit them for your reduced hidden strike damage. Feinting has…a lot of weaknesses, typically. You’ll take a penalty to feint anything other than a humanoid, and it becomes progressively harder to pull off the dumber enemies become—against mindless enemies, it’s outright impossible unless you’ve got some mesmerist dip lying around that I don’t know about. Moreover, the action economy is just bad, surrendering your standard action (ya know, the one you’d usually use to attack with?) for one hit at a later date. But fear not! As with all things except ranged combat, there’s a vigilante talent for that. Cunning feint neatly solves all your problems for you. At 1st level, you can feint as a move action (the equivalent of the Improved Feint feat, although it doesn’t count as that feat for the purposes of satisfying prerequisites), and at 8th level, you deny an enemy their Dexterity bonus to AC against all attacks until your next turn begins (the equivalent of the Greater Feint feat). Cunning feint tosses in one more bonus, though: you can replace your first attack in a full-attack action with a feint. This is incredible! Although you may still choose to feint as a move action and attack as a standard action (potentially with Vital Strike or something) when you need your best chances of hitting an enemy, sacrificing your highest-BAB attack can still be a smart choice if you’re burning down an enemy with low AC or if you already have a buff that grants an extra attack (think haste, blessings of fervor, or a monk’s flurry of blows) running.

---

# Demoralization

Demoralization


        On their own, demoralization builds are nothing to write home about. You compare the result of an Intimidate check against 10 + an enemy’s HD + an enemy’s Wisdom modifier as a standard action. If you succeed, they become shaken (-2 penalty to most things) for at least 1 but potentially a handful of rounds. Now, Intimidate is a mind-affecting fear effect, so much like with feint, a good tranche of enemies are outright immune to it. What’s more, fear effects don’t cause enemies to lose their Dexterity bonuses to AC; unlike feinting—which is tailor-made to deny Dexterity—fear effects have to be massaged into doing that. The key feat here is Shatter Defenses, which makes an opponent flat-footed against every attack you make against them until the end of your next turn…if you can hit them while they’re already shaken, frightened, or panicked. This mandate is laughably easy for stalkers, who will probably already want to pick up Weapon Focus + Dazzling Display for their excellent twisting fear vigilante talent. Enforcer + the take ‘em alive vigilante talent is by far the easiest way to get single targets shaken with any weapon, and Dazzling Display does a habitually excellent job at handling crowds. You’ll typically lead with a Dazzling Display + twisting fear, then 5-foot step for a full-attack action (if you’re close enough) or charge for a hit to dole out flat-footed (if you’re far away), and finally continue full-attacking with hidden strike until the enemy is dead. Rinse and repeat for all remaining enemies, refreshing Dazzling Display + twisting fear as needed.

---

# Infiltration

Infiltration


        I don’t think I’m going out too far on any limbs by saying that vigilantes are the premiere class for infiltrating enemy organizations in Pathfinder 1e. Better than rogues or ninja? Oh, by a country mile, honey. Better than infiltrator or urban infiltrator inquisitors? A bit of a tougher sell, but the answer still ain’t no. Better than mesmerists? You bet your sweet bippy. Let’s break down what makes vigilantes so incredible at insinuating themselves deep undercover:
        First, vigilante talents and social talents are exceptionally good at getting this class into places it shouldn’t be. I’ve left some pairings below as examples, but even a cursory glance at their social talent list should let you know just how much the vigilante’s infiltration game slaps. It’s like getting an entire second class pasted onto a fighter or rogue chassis. Second, nearly all of the vigilante’s abilities are free, inexhaustible, and largely non-magical. Whereas just about any other class—even heavily specialized ones like those I listed above—would need to cast spells to conceal their identity, thereby risking wasting essential combat resources or running afoul of dispel magic, greater detect magic, antimagic field, etc., vigilantes can comfortably inhabit their roles all day, every day. Third, the mechanical oomph is here. Most new players assume that just because rogues have 8 + Intelligence skill ranks, that must mean that they’re the best at skills—but it’s simply not so. You need depth in Pathfinder, not just breadth, and seamless guise, feign innocence, skill familiarity, etc. all provide the consistency and massive skill bonuses that you need to truly confound even determined searchers or interrogators. Alright: onto the combos.


   * Notorious fool + seamless guise: The classic “Wait, I wasn’t supposed to be in this highly restricted area? Man, the guys down at the temp agency sure pulled one over on me!” talent, notorious fool lets you stealth or steal your way into places and then just pretend like you’re an innocent idiot when you get caught. Pretty funny, even if it’s not as effective as it could be.
   * Seamless shapechanger + malleable flesh + seamless guise: Malleable flesh is a prerequisite for the excellent morphic weaponry vigilante talent, but there are plenty of other reasons you would take it, chief among them this excellent combo. Now, RAW, malleable flesh’s disguise self isn’t called out specifically as a polymorph effect, and therefore not subject to seamless shapechanger’s ability to add your seamless guise bonuses to your Disguise checks. But if malleable flesh is “physical, rather than illusory,” as the rules text state, doesn’t that align much better with the polymorph rules, which tell us that “a polymorph spell transforms your physical body to take on the shape of another creature”? (Emphasis mine.) I dunno. At my table, this would be an open and shut case. And if so, what a powerful combo! Seamless guise grants a +20 circumstance bonus to Disguise; disguise self, a further +10 untyped bonus. Even if your GM rules that the halving from malleable flesh applies to both bonuses, that’s still a cool +15 to appear as any creature you choose—pretty nifty.
