And that’s without going into malleable flesh’s ability to let you squeeze through cracks! Incredibly useful for getting through keyholes, out of prisons or restraints, etc.
   * Many guises + everyman + any guise: What. A. Talent. Line. What a talent line! The many guises suite initially allows you to assume a totally expendable “burner identity” separate from your social and vigilante identities—one that you can switch at will between various peasants and menials in Golarion society. By the time you reach any guise at 17th level, you can become literally any person (it’s right there on the label, man, I don’t know what to tell you) at will, permanently, non-magically, and with your incredible seamless guise bonuses. If you want to skulk somewhere that a carpenter, cook, mason, chambermaid, gardener, waiter, etc. could go, this is your pick.
   * Hidden magic: Hidden magic is a gem of the social talent list, permitting you to completely conceal any magic items or buffs you may be toting around from casual—and even fairly concerted—scrutiny. Glamered armor and weaponry will set you back only 6,700 gp in total and would permit you to walk, fully armed, past nearly all divination magic looking like a normally clothed person. You would not part an old man from his walking stick?
   * Renown + feign innocence + subjective truth: Hey! Do you want to lie about why you’re somewhere you shouldn’t be extremely effectively? How about permanent innocence and immunity to most lie detection magic? Why, it couldn’t have been me, officer!
   * Another day: This one’s wacky and requires you to take a truly sub-par vigilante talent, but allies can use the another day vigilante talent to beat you into unconsciousness, whereupon you look like you’re dead. Who hasn’t fantasized about doing the whole “I’m a bounty hunter and I just caught your worst enemy!” routine? Heath Ledger’s Joker would be proud.
   * Hide in plain sight: The Stealth skill is normally limited by the fact that you have to have, you know, a topiary or something to hide behind. Not so with HiPS. Now, you are the stealth.
   * Monkey’s paws + leap and bound: Climbing is all well and good, but leap and bound is something else—in essence, it quadruples the results of your Acrobatics checks to jump. With even moderate investment, you can reliably leap about 30 feet into the air by double-digit levels. That’s gotta be useful for something, right?
________________

---

# VIG 201: Races

VIG 201: Races




Assuming that you’ve made it this far and know that you like the cut of the vigilante’s jib—what it’s good at, what it struggles with, and what role it can be expected to play in a party composition—it’s time to set about the nitty-gritty work of actually building a vigilante. Race (or ancestry, as Pathfinder 2e calls it) is one of the first decisions that you’ll make, and it’s undoubtedly an important one. Fortunately, we don’t need to review every one of Pathfinder’s enormous number of races, because a simple heuristic will help you arrive at a good racial choice 99% of the time. Some things that vigilantes are intensely interested in acquiring through racial abilities:


      * Bonuses to Strength or Dexterity principally, and to Charisma secondarily. Vigilantes are first and foremost a combat class, so they positively must have an attribute bonus that goes to their attack stat. If you’re considering a race that doesn’t grant such a bonus or (gods forbid) imposes a penalty to Strength or Dexterity, you’re far better off opting for a race that allows you to assign one +2 bonus to any attribute—races like humans, half-elves, and half-orcs will all suffice.
      * Bonus feats. Vigilante talents often duplicate or improve upon feats that are already available, but since vigilante talents are incredibly valuable, you’ll want to buy as many feats as you can with your normal feat progression unless there’s a compelling, unique bonus to the talent. (For example, cunning feint allows you to feint in place of your first attack in a full-attack action, which can’t be found anywhere in Pathfinder’s feinting feats. Since that’s a really good ability for feinting builds, they’ll typically want the talent, not the feats.) Humans are the classic choice for this category, as they grant one bonus feat at 1st level, when feat crunches tend to be felt most acutely.
      * Favored class bonuses. Favored class bonuses are bonuses that Pathfinder races can take in lieu of bonus skill ranks or HP at every level. As later additions to the Pathfinder canon, the vigilante never got as many favored class bonuses as other classes (and fewer still are useful!) but there are a few worth highlighting: first, the cream of the crop, the king of the castle, +⅙ of a new social talent. Only halflings receive this favored class bonus, and that fact combined with their excellent attribute bonuses puts them in easy contention for the best race to play ranged and finesse vigilantes. It’s impossible for me to overstate how powerful most vigilante talents and social talents are—and this is the only place in the game where you can get more of them. At a somewhat distant second, you have +½ on the Disguise bonus provided by seamless guise. Seamless guise already provides a +20 bonus to conceal the fact that your social and vigilante identities aren’t one and the same; barring a tangle with, say, a high-level inquisitor, you should almost never have to worry about that doomsday scenario unless you’ve skimped on your investment in Disguise. This favored class bonus gets considerably better if you have the seamless shapechanger talent, which allows you to add your seamless guise bonus to your Disguise checks whenever you’re polymorphed. Of course, it’s also notably worse if you’re not attempting to keep your identities separate, and would militate for skill ranks or HP, as you prefer.
      * Vision. Vigilantes have ways to get both low-light vision (the owl’s sight social talent) and low-light vision + darkvision (the shadow’s sight vigilante talent) but it’s better not to have to spend limited resources on them.
      * Skill bonuses. These bonuses have to be evaluated in light of how useful the skill is. Bonuses to Perception, like the elf and half-elf’s Keen Senses? Pure gold. Bonuses to Appraise or Handle Animal? Poop on a stick.






________________
Core Races

---

# Dwarf

Dwarf


        With the exception of the hardy racial trait, dwarves have nothing to recommend them as vigilantes. Their Charisma is generally too low to make good use of Intimidate, Bluff, Diplomacy, and Disguise, all of which are bread and butter skills for the class; those low Charisma scores also hurt their vigilante talent save DCs. I can’t in good conscience push you in this direction.


Pros
	Cons
	Hardy. A perennial favorite of dwarf PCs, the hardy racial trait gives you a +2 racial bonus to saves vs. poisons, spells, and spell-like abilities, which covers a huge array of effects.
	Exceptionally poor attribute bonuses. Remember what our criteria were? Bonuses to either Charisma, Dexterity, or Strength, and hopefully no penalties to Charisma or your chosen physical stat. Not only do dwarves not grant any relevant bonuses to physical or mental attributes, they also take a -2 Charisma penalty. That’s not as bad as it woulds be for a Charisma-based spellcaster, but it’s still not good.
	Darkvision.
	Pretty bad favored class bonus. Mundane crafting is unfortunately an exceptionally poor use of your time as a PC. Go get a crafting cohort or familiar, ya hippie.
	



________________

---

# Elf

Elf


        Anything elves can do, half-elves can do better, I’m afraid. Half-elves can get the same weapon proficiencies and visual senses if they want, and have both a more flexible stat bonus and a more consistently useful favored class bonus. Do it for story reasons if you must, but it’s not mechanically optimal.


Pros
	Cons
	Some cool weapon proficiencies. Bonus weapon proficiencies fall a bit flat when the class is already proficient with all martial weapons, but elven curve blades and branched spears are undeniably great.
	Very, very narrow favored class bonus. Skill bonuses to move about stealthily in forested terrain are all well and good, but you run into the classic Ranger Syndrome of being in your favored terrain a small percentage of the time. Potentially green for Ironfang Invasion, but not much besides.
	Low-light vision, but no darkvision.
	No bonuses to Charisma, and a penalty to Constitution. Vigilantes are weak on their Fortitude saves, have a d8 HD (even avengers!), and generally need to feel comfortable in melee range. A hit to Constitution with bonuses to the vigilante’s usual dump stat (Intelligence) hurts.
	



________________

---

# Gnome

Gnome


        Gnomes do excellent work for spellcasting vigilantes, but they’re generally a B-tier race for other types of vigilantes. Halflings beat them easily in the Small size category, and any of the humans/half-humans available among the core races are better Medium-sized picks. They’re still good—you won’t shake your fist at the heavens and rue the day you chose to play a gnome—but you are leaving a bit of optimization on the table.


Pros
	Cons
	Pretty good stat distributions. You’d never build a Strength-based gnomish Pathfinder PC anyway, so gnomes do fairly well for themselves as infiltrators and Finesse fighters. Not having a bonus to your main attack stat isn’t ideal, though, especially given the huge bevy of races that grant bonuses to Dexterity and Charisma. If you’re playing a spellcasting vigilante, though, Constitution + Charisma is ideal.
	Lame favored class bonus. Maybe I’m just too smooth-brained to think about how this favored class bonus would benefit you, but I don’t see any bonuses here that couldn’t be replicated with the feign innocence and subjective truth social talents.
	Spellcasting boons. Gnomes can snag a number of racial increases to their spell save DCs, which can be huge for archetypes like the cabalist that focus on one school of magic. Most vigilantes aren’t casters, though.
	

	Small size. You’d probably want to build a gnome with the stalker specialization, in which case very little of your damage would be coming from a Medium-sized weapon damage die. Just enjoy your improved AC, attack bonuses, and Stealth bonuses!
	

	Low-light vision.
	

	



________________

---

# Half-Elf

Half-Elf


        Half-elves don’t distinguish themselves from humans as much as they do in some other classes, thanks to a favored class bonus that is the same between both races. They’re still strong choices for vigilantes, with adaptability, a floating stat bonus, and access to two separate favored class bonuses.


Pros
	Cons
	Floating stat bonus. Floating stat bonuses are the name of the game for half-elves, half-orcs, and humans, and they’re flexible enough to let you build any kind of vigilante you want. 95% of the time, you’ll go with Strength or Dexterity.
	None. Half-elves are almost always a top-tier race. The vigilante is no exception to this rule.
	Adaptability. Adaptability (and the racial traits that replace it) is a fantastic racial feature. Skill Focus is a flexible feat that can help with Stealth, Bluff, Intimidate, Disguise, or many of the ancillary roles that vigilantes often occupy. Moreover, ancestral arms, weapon familiarity, and dual-minded are all excellent replacements for adaptability, if you’re interested in replacing it.
	

	Decent favored class bonuses. Half-elf vigilantes don’t get the usual trifecta of favored class bonuses that they sometimes do in other classes. The elf favored class bonus is pretty bad unless you plan to spend your entire career in a forest, and the human and half-elf favored class bonuses are the same time—a bonus to seamless guise. I like that favored class bonus, as I’ve discussed previously, but it’s not as good as the halfling’s favored class bonus.
	

	Multitalented. Multiclassing is fairly common among vigilantes, especially to swashbuckler, scaled fist unchained monk, etc., and while you’re probably not going to spend huge amounts of time in those secondary classes, it helps to be scaling your favored class bonuses while you do so.
	

	Low-light vision, but no darkvision.
	

	



________________

---

# Halfling

Halfling


        Halflings are the superlative race for vigilantes. Especially for stalker vigilantes, there must be an extremely compelling reason why you’re not playing a halfling! They’ve got all the right stat bonuses for Dexterity-based vigilantes, great racial traits, Small size, and an unbelievable favored class bonus that stands head and shoulders above every other race in the game. Y’all come get some.


Pros
	Cons
	Insanely good favored class bonus. Every once in a while, you run into an favored class bonus where you ask, “What was Paizo thinking when they created this?” The gathlain kineticist favored class bonus is a great example of mechanics that prompt this question; this is another. There is no force in Pathfinder that can give you more social talents, save this favored class bonus. Three bonus social talents over the course of your career means you can pick up the entire any guise line for free, snag hidden magic + ancestral enlightenment + companion to the lonely for great bonuses, take your renown game to the next level, or more. It is the preeminent favored class bonus in the game.
	No racial low-light vision or darkvision.
	Great stat bonuses. Bonuses to Dexterity and Charisma are a perfect fit for most Weapon Finesse and stalker vigilante builds. Note that because the lethal grace vigilante talent explicitly forces you to use your Strength modifier for damage and the halfling gives you a penalty to Strength, you may want to stick with the classic Weapon Finesse + an agile weapon instead of using lethal grace.
	

	Good racial traits. Fleet of Foot and Adaptable Luck are the only two alternatives that I would consistently recommend. Fate’s Favored + Adaptable Luck is a classic combo that can give you a +3 luck bonus on most d20 rolls 3/day.
	

	Small size. Small-sized races do great as rogues and stalker vigilantes, where their smaller weapon damage dice aren’t nearly as relevant when stacked against all the precision d6s or d8s they’re slinging around. Everything else—AC, Stealth, attack bonuses—is gravy.
	

	



________________

---

# Half-Orc

Half-Orc


        The usual half-orc/half-elf/human holy trinity stands strong! If you’re looking for a race with darkvision, bonuses to Intimidate, strong saves, and great weapon proficiencies, the half-orc is a solid pick. It doesn’t have quite as much to recommend it without a useable favored class bonus, but the human favored class bonus is the second-strongest after the halfling favored class bonus. We can’t all be halflings.


Pros
	Cons
	Floating stat bonus. Floating stat bonuses are the name of the game for half-elves, half-orcs, and humans, and they’re flexible enough to let you build any kind of vigilante you want.
	None. Half-orcs are typically a very strong choice.
	Sacred Tattoo + Fate’s Favored: A classic powergamer move that grants a +2 luck bonus to all saves. Half-orc, babyyyy!
	

	Great weapon proficiencies. Vigilantes already do well for themselves with weapons, but half-orcs can get cool weapons like the orc hornbow much more easily than other races.
	

	One decent favored class bonus. The native half-orc favored class bonus is hot trash (unshakable is your worst class feature by a country mile) but as a half-orc, you have access to the human favored class bonus. Use that, I beg of you.
	

	Racial darkvision. You can always upgrade to 120 feet with the Dragon Sight trait!
	

	



________________

---

# Human

Human


        I wonder whether humans ever get tired of being Paizo’s clear favorite among all the races? The favored class bonus differential between halflings and humans is the only thing holding our native species back from the purple rating among the core races. More feats and skill ranks never, ever hurt.


Pros
	Cons
	Floating stat bonus. Floating stat bonuses are the name of the game for half-elves, half-orcs, and humans, and they’re flexible enough to let you build any kind of mesmerist you want.
	No racial darkvision or low-light vision.
	Bonus feat. Even though many vigilante talents approximate feats, it can still take a bit of massaging to get a combat-ready build up and running. That being the case, the human bonus feat can buy you a whole two levels of advancement ahead of other races.
	

	Extra skill ranks. Why, don’t mind if I do! 6 + Intelligence is already quite good, but many vigilantes will need to dump Intelligence to accommodate other stats. Nice to be able to buy back that lost rank.
	

	Good favored class bonus. Seamless guise bonuses aren’t top priority for most vigilantes, but with seamless shapechanger, you can definitely become very hard to spot while disguised.

---

# Selected Other Races

Selected Other Races


        Under normal circumstances, I do a whole write-up of non-core races that can work for a class. But if I’m being honest, the vigilante doesn’t have a large enough stable of good favored class bonuses to make one non-core race demonstrably better than another. If you have bonuses to Charisma or Strength/Dexterity and no penalties to Constitution, you’re probably going to do just fine, attribute-wise. Everything else is about finding a theme and a few mechanical perks you like. So go, then: there are other sections than this.
________________

---

# VIG 245: Social Talents

VIG 245: Social Talents


If vigilante talents are what make the vigilante feel like an à la carte rogue or fighter, social talents are what make them feel like à la carte bards, investigators, and mesmerists. The most mechanically powerful social talents tend to come online in a very well-ordered progression, so I almost always find myself taking something like the following:


      * 1st: Companion to the lonely / owl’s sight / social grace
      * 3rd: Dealer’s choice of 1st-level social talents
      * 5th: Many guises
      * 7th: Quick change
      * 9th: Skill familiarity
      * 11th: Everyman
      * 13th: Immediate change
      * 15th: Hidden magic / ancestral enlightenment / mockingbird
      * 17th: Any guise
      * 19th: Dealer’s choice


        Everyone’s mileage will vary, of course, but for my tastes that’s the best, most flexible package of social talents you can have at a given level. Races like halflings that have a +⅙ social talent favored class bonus will be in an even better position, since they can take some of the wildcard talents listed at 1st and 15th levels at 6th, 12th, and 18th levels instead. Remember that there are no ways—short of those favored class bonuses—to get more social talents. Choose wisely.




Universal


1st Level


Companion to the Lonely (Ex): How exactly one hour of sexytimes per day translates to a bunch of free rerolls on Will saves, we may never know, but regardless, Marvin Gaye, I’m ready to get it on! Vigilantes naturally have a strong Will save progression, but you can never have a high enough Will save. I would advise that you not use your rerolls on Charisma-based skill checks unless they’re the most important skill checks you’ve ever rolled. The utility of this social talent is obviously predicated on having a willing partner to…hem…woo. If you don’t, well, no one ever handed out bonuses for romancing your own nethers. Regardless, for the sake of all involved, figure out your group’s lines and veils before showing up with this social talent. Please and thank you.


Always Prepared (Ex): Always prepared really shines with creative players who are adventuring in one location consistently—think campaigns like Curse of the Crimson Throne with Korvosa. Probably the best use for the talent is to purchase a bunch of potions, scrolls, alchemical items, etc. that you might need on the fly. If your campaign is going to involve constantly moving around to different locales, you may want to skip it.
Morphic Mask (Su): In addition to being thematically cool, morphic mask increases your Disguise bonuses from seamless guise, and I’m all about that. Can’t have anyone guessing that Lance Hunt is Captain Amazing.
Owl’s Sight (Ex): Sure. Owl’s sight gives you some decent vision and skill bonuses, and you don’t have to spend any vigilante talents for ‘em.


Case the Joint (Ex): Case the joint is…interesting. The reliance on Knowledge (Engineering) is a bit bizarre; that skill generally isn’t worth your skill ranks unless you’re playing with the Background Skills rules. If you can become good at Knowledge (Engineering) and expect to spend a lot of time in urban environments where hanging around in your social identity wouldn’t be conspicuous, sure, you have my permission to take it. The bonuses are nice.
Guise of Unlife (Su): Unlike guise of life, guise of unlife at least opens up some interesting roleplay and interaction opportunities. Maybe you want to infiltrate a necromantic cult and appearing to be undead would give you an advantage, or you want to really spook someone by making them think that a powerful vampire is monitoring them. It’s not good enough to rate better than yellow, but I can think of one or two situations in which it could be useful.
Kalistocrat’s Acument (Ex): Potentially of use if you intend to adventure out in the boonies where purchase limits are low. Of course, you need to be fine with living life as a Prophet of Kalistrade, which may not fit most people’s backstories.
Notorious Fool (Ex): An easy way to “get let off with a warning” when attempting to break into places you don’t belong. If they catch you, just pretend you were asleep!
Seamless Shapechanger (Ex): Seamless shapechanger is only going to be useful to you if you also took the malleable flesh vigilante talent. If that’s the case, it becomes extraordinarily easy to impersonate just about anyone and anything, and all that well before the many guises social talents start to come online. It does take some investment, though.
Transformation Sequence (Su): Personally, I think the disadvantages to transformation sequence outweigh the advantages, but your mileage may vary. Most vigilantes can simply wait until the quick change social talent comes online at 7th level.
Well-Known Expert (Ex): Benefits from renown, but doesn’t require it. A fun, thematic social talent that encourages you to focus on aiding your teammates instead of hogging the spotlight. Adjunct Professor Maxine Vetro of the Glass Cannon Podcast’s Side Quest Side Sesh series does a particularly great job with this talent.


Beginner’s Luck (Ex): There are two fatal problems with beginner’s luck. First, you can’t fool many onlookers with your sudden use of a vigilante talent until you attain a high level—if you have even a small crowd present, someone is going to realize that you’re more than what you seem. Second, you can’t fool the same onlooker more than once per week. This doesn’t leave a lot of leeway if you need to get into a combat while in your social identity.
Bellflower Innuendo (Ex): Passing secret messages with Bluff is an extraordinarily niche use of the skill, and one that I’ve frankly never seen used. Remember that talents are precious, precious things—if you can’t envision yourself using it all the time, it’s dead weight.
Conflicted Identity (Ex): Alignment-based effects are very rare—hardly worth building around.
Double Time (Ex): Benefits from social grace, but doesn’t require it. There’s no reason any vigilante should be interested in mundane crafting or wage-earning. Safely skippable.
Entrepreneur (Ex): Benefits from social grace, but doesn’t require it. As with double time, there’s no reason why you should use any skill to earn money through mundane means unless you have literal months of downtime on your hands.
Gossip Collector (Ex): Benefits from renown, but doesn’t require it. I can think of very few if any situations in which you would need to gather information with the Diplomacy skill at top speed, and that tells me that it’s not worth a social talent.
Guise of Life (Su): I suppose dhampir PCs could take guise of life in order to pass as human, but that’s about the extent of the talent’s utility.
Intrigue Feats (Ex): I’m sorry, but none of the feats listed would rate more than about a yellow for vigilantes, especially when they’re up against a lot of other worthwhile social talents. Just forget it. Social talents are too valuable.




5th Level


Ancestral Enlightenment (Ex): An untyped +4 to any Knowledge skill you have ranks in is an easy buy for me. You may not be a Knowledge jockey, but this at least ensures that you can hold your own on monster identification checks, aid another actions, etc.
Hidden Magic (Ex): Magic aura and greater magic aura are fantastic spells for appearing to be less threatening. With the hidden magic talent, you could walk into a heavily guarded fortress carrying all kinds of magically enhanced gear and appear to be no more than a humble commoner toting around some high-quality but ultimately unremarkable items. If you have equipment enchantments like glamered, so much the better! You’ll look like you’re wearing nothing more than normal clothing and toting nothing more than a walking stick.
Mockingbird (Ex): Another highly customizable social talent for creative players. One of the issues with each of these spells for most players is that they’re all pretty low-level, which means the save DCs to disbelieve your warbling will never scale very well. You’re not most players, though: as social talents, your mockingbird save DCs will always scale with your vigilante level, making sound-based trickery much more viable as an adventuring tactic. It’s kind of like using Heighten Spell on ghost sound, ventriloquism, and vocal alteration.




9th Level


Skill Familiarity (Ex): A fabulous social talent that makes life easier in a lot of ways. Disguise and Intimidate have obvious synergy with the vigilante’s skillset; you might also consider Bluff, Acrobatics, Diplomacy, Perception, Stealth, and UMD as strong contenders, depending on your needs.

---

# Many Guises Line

Many Guises Line


5th Level


Many Guises (Ex): This talent opens up sooo many doors for the vigilante. Who pays attention to the gardener or the scullery maid, after all? Paired with hidden magic and some glamered equipment, you can stroll into almost any location on Golarion, fully ready for combat, with no one knowing or caring who you are. It’s really quite extraordinary.




11th Level


Everyman (Ex): Everyman feels like a bit of a step back from many guises, mostly because there may be only limited scenarios in which you need to impersonate a specific farmer, laborer, or peasant, rather than just creating a new, generic mundane identity. The bonuses to Bluff checks can be quite useful, however, because again, who pays much attention to the stonemason, farmer, beggar, or cook as they walk around the grounds of that heavily fortified castle?

---

# 17th Level

17th Level


Any Guise (Su): A fitting capstone social talent for the vigilante class, any guise allows you to impersonate anyone at any time. It’s hard to overstate just how much of an edge this gives you in deception, manipulation, and infiltration, especially given that the ability is supernatural and therefore not subject to dispel magic, detect magic, or most other divinations that would reveal who you truly are. If you have many guises and everyman, this talent needs to be your 17th-level pick, no questions asked. I said no questions.

---

# Quick Change Line

Quick Change Line


7th Level


Quick Change (Ex): If you’re trying to separate your vigilante and social identities, you need quick change, period. At base, changing between identities takes a full minute, which means that you simply will not be able to participate in combat if it breaks out while you’re in your social identity. Unacceptable, right? Well, as crappy as it would be to waste an entire round of combat changing identities, quick change decreases the amount of time you need to change by a factor of 10, and that simply can’t be ignored. If you’re wearing your vigilante identity openly, then it doesn’t particularly matter whether you can transition between social and vigilante quickly, as you’ll just use social most of the time, even in combat.




13th Level


Immediate Change (Ex): Although immediate change isn’t quite as do-or-die mandatory as quick change, it’s yet another layer of protection against getting caught off guard in your social identity when combat breaks out. A move action is pretty low on action costs, all things considered. Similar calculus as quick change for figuring out whether you need it or not.

---

# Renown Line

Renown Line


1st Level


Safe House (Ex): The safe house talent is a fantastic way to hide yourself and your group from prying eyes, magical or mundane. And besides, doesn’t everyone secretly want their own Batcave?


Renown (Ex): Renown is the starting point for the largest social talent web in the vigilante class, but to be honest, the talent isn’t that great on its own. You get some benefits to social interaction skills and starting attitudes from some NPCs, but the size of settlement you can affect with the talent is painfully small. What’s more, the trade-off between the amount of time you need to spend cultivating renown and the benefits you accrue for doing so simply doesn’t make sense unless you’re adventuring in one metropolis for a significant portion of a book in a Paizo AP.


Discreet Inquiries (Ex): Although I appreciate the flavor behind discreet inquiries, it guards against an eventuality that’s vanishingly rare. Just make your Diplomacy checks to gather information and damn the torpedos.
Obscurity (Ex): Unless you traded away seamless guise, that class feature and its +20 bonus to Disguise should keep you safe enough from scrutiny. Don’t do anything stupid like jump into combat in your social identity and you’ll be fine without obscurity.

---

# 3rd Level

3rd Level


Celebrity Discount (Ex): A 10% discount on all equipment below a certain purchase limit is certainly enticing—you’ll probably start off with potions, scrolls, alchemical items, etc., and progress to low-cost wondrous items at later stages of renown. A solid social talent.


Loyal Aid (Ex): Loyal aid has its uses, but your mundane helpers won’t do anything for you that you couldn’t do for yourself, especially if you have a solid social identity going for you or had the many guises talent.
Triumphant Return (Ex): Keyword? “In any settlement where the vigilante previously gained renown.” If you’re traveling from one location to another, triumphant return won’t do a lick of good—it’s useful only if you plan to travel back and forth in between settlements, switching your area of renown every time.




5th Level


Feign Innocence (Ex): Feign innocence pairs nicely with notorious fool; the combo allows you to stroll into places that you don’t belong, get caught, and then reel off incredibly believable lies about how you got lost on the way to the restroom. It’s a lot of fun to get constant spell effects, too, especially ones that are extraordinary abilities that can’t be dispelled or suppressed.


Celebrity Perks (Ex): New life goals: establish an area of incredible renown and then settle in to receive exquisite food and lodging for free, forever. All joking aside, though, celebrity perks deals with stuff that most GMs handwave anyway, and the money that you save is basically pocket change by the time you can acquire the relevant renown talents. If you want to actually save some chunks of change, celebrity discount is a better purchase.




7th Level


Great Renown (Ex): By the time you get to great renown, you’ve actually started to get build toward some decent-sized towns. I only wish there were a way to establish renown faster than with the instant recognition talent at 13th level! One week is a hideously long time to spend on these talents.




9th Level


Subjective Truth (Ex): Meh. I think I’d rather just grab feign innocence and consciously lie about things. Guarding against truth detection magic is kind of a fringe use case.




11th Level


Incredible Renown (Ex): The capstone to the renown social talent line doesn’t disappoint. Finally, your name can live in infamy throughout seedy metropoli in the Inner Sea region! Korvosa, Westcrown, Riddleport—every criminal is going to fear and loathe you.




13th Level


Instant Recognition (Ex): Ugh, finally. GMs, I really think instant recognition could have been a social talent at a much earlier level—the bonuses that PCs get are still gated at 1st/7th/11th levels, and all that instant recognition does is ease everyone’s frustration at not being able to get the bonuses they paid for without literally spending a week working on an in-game PR campaign.




Social Grace Line


1st Level


Social Grace (Ex): Good skill bonuses that can easily overlap with ancestral enlightenment or skill familiarity. You can decide which skills are most needed in your group.




5th Level


In Vogue (Ex): There’s no need to use Profession or Craft to earn money when one combat encounter will probably have a higher payout. Just ignore these talents that deal with crafting and wage-earning.




Songbird Line


1st Level


Beast Friend (Su): Okay, sure. Lots of campaigns have some animals in them, and it’s nice to be able to help them feel comfortable around you. Animals have a much lower chance than humanoids of retaliating once they realize they’ve been mind-controlled, too.
Beast Speech (Su): As beast friend.
Songbird (Su): As beast friend.
________________

---

# VIG 255: Vigilante Talents

VIG 255: Vigilante Talents


Vigilante talents are unquestionably why this class has such a reputation for power and versatility. They often function as thematic “bundles” of abilities, perhaps granting multiple feats, poaching limited version of features from other classes, or even granting benefits that are found nowhere else in Pathfinder. Often one talent is all you need to become good at a particular theme, which is what makes them so appealing. Want to build a Weapon Finesse build? Lethal grace is all you need. Want to be good at feinting? Cunning feint. Nonlethal damage? Take ‘em alive. They’re dead simple to use, modular enough to complement or supplant feats, and powerful enough to make even 1e veterans perk up. One important thing to note is that there is no way to get more vigilante talents. No Extra Vigilante Talent feat, no favored class bonus that grants them every 6 levels, no training weapon enchantment shenanigans, no wondrous items—nothing. You get what you get at the levels the vigilante class says you get them. Choose even more wisely here than you do for social talents, because what you choose will define what your vigilante is capable of.




Universal


Offense


(2) Lethal Grace (Ex): Lethal grace opens up so, so many interesting build options for Dexterity-based vigilantes. Unarmed strike and natural attack builds, Two-Weapon Fighting builds, run-of-the-mill Weapon Finesse builds…all will benefit considerably from adding this much damage to each attack. And you even get to buy back Weapon Finesse, if you took it at an earlier level! Isn’t that a daisy?


(5) Animal Patron (Ex): Minor shifter aspects are essentially juiced-up animal foci from the hunter class, and offer everything from scaling skill or attribute bonuses to free feats. Only you will know exactly what’s right for your build, but consider the attribute-boosting aspects (Bull for Strength, Tiger for Dexterity, Bear for Constitution, Peafowl for Charisma), combat maneuver-boosting aspects (Crocodile or Scorpion for grapple, Elephant for bull rush and overrun), skill-boosting aspects (Dolphin for Knowledge, Falcon for Perception, Lion for Intimidate, Lizard for Acrobatics, Owl for Stealth), save-boosting aspects (Giant Wasp for mind-affecting effects), attack-boosting aspects (Snake for attack of opportunity builds), and initiative-boosting aspects (Deinonychus) very strongly.
(2) Morphic Weaponry (Ex): Requires shapechanger subtype. Morphic weaponry is one of the premiere vigilante talents for natural attack builds, as it eventually grants you four primary natural attacks that can all be enhanced with Strength bonuses or the lethal grace vigilante talent. All vigilantes can gain the shapechanger subtype by taking the malleable flesh vigilante talent.
(2) Take ‘Em Alive (Ex): This talent is notable for two reasons. First, nonlethal damage is just as effective as lethal damage in a wide variety of scenarios, and jeezy pete, those are some big attack and damage bonuses. Second, nonlethal damage is the necessary tie-in to the Enforcer feat, which is, bar none, the most effective single-target demoralization tool in Pathfinder. If you’re intending to use Intimidate heavily in melee range, you should absolutely consider getting this vigilante talent. One final note: I don’t know what to make of the scaling. I looked up the text in The Spymaster’s Handbook, and it does indeed say “starting at 5th level, and every 3 levels thereafter,” which doesn’t make a lot of sense. If they meant every 5 levels thereafter, it would cap out at 20th, like Paizo wrote; if they meant every 3 levels, it caps out at 14th level. The latter is obviously more powerful than the former.


(2) Strike the Unseen (Ex): This is a prime example of how vigilante talents are, in fact, often three or four feats standing on each other’s shoulders in a trench coat, asking to be admitted to the R-rated movie. Let’s see: we’ve got Blind-Fight, Improved Blind-Fight, Greater Blind-Fight, and even a feat that doesn’t technically exist in Pathfinder (let’s call it “Improved Shadow Strike”) that allows you to deal precision damage to targets with total concealment. Avenger vigilantes can benefit from the first three feats as much as any melee character, of course, but stalker vigilantes are the intended audience, since they need to be able to deal hidden strike damage to enemies that are invisible, under the effects of displacement, concealed by supernatural darkness, etc.
(2) Surprise Strike (Ex): On balance, I think that surprise strike really hits its stride with stalker vigilantes. Not only do stalkers have a harder time hitting foes than avengers thanks to their ¾ BAB progression, they’re also way more incentivized to deny enemies their Dexterity to AC so that they can deal their reduced hidden strike damage. If you plan to attack from invisibility or greater invisibility, build toward Shatter Defenses, use dirty tricks to blind foes, feint, or employ any other tactics that would leave an enemy without a Dexterity bonus to their AC, then surprise strike is for you.
(2) Vigilante’s Reflexes (Ex): Vigilante’s reflexes is a lot of fun, and quite powerful in the right hands. Whose hands? Why, Strength-based avenger vigilante builds using reach weapons, naturally. You get to keep your Dexterity low, wear heavy armor, and still get to take a bunch of attacks of opportunity every round with your longspear, glaive, ranseur, etc. Dexterity-based vigilantes will likely want to skip vigilante’s reflexes and instead just purchase Combat Reflexes—the extra attacks of opportunity from this talent are overkill when you’re already adding four to seven attacks of opportunity per round with the feat, and vigilante talents are 450% more valuable than feats. That’s just science, baby.
(6) Vital Punishment (Ex): Vital Strike as a bonus feat, plus the ability to add Vital Strike to one attack of opportunity per round, in the same vein as Diabolic Judgment or the advanced benefit of Torag’s Divine Fighting Technique. It’s good stuff. Full-attack actions are never guaranteed in Pathfinder.


(8) Aquatic Action (Ex): Mega-useful in aquatic campaigns like Ruins of Azlant, but pretty much nowhere else.
(8) Perfect Vulnerability (Ex): Hmmm. Perfect vulnerability guarantees that you’ll hit any enemy—touch AC without a Dexterity bonus is effectively AC 10, if not lower—but the fact that you can do it to an enemy only once per day makes it a bit limited. I’d sooner just pump my attack rolls and deal with things that way.
(2) Poisoner (Ex): Poisoner solves one of the main problems of any poison build—namely, how do I get my hands on more poisons without having to buy or craft them? So now your poisons pay out a 20% dividend daily! How’s that for yield farming? All of poison’s usual weaknesses—widespread immunities, low save DCs matched against enemies’ strong Fortitude saves, etc.—apply, but this is a step in the right direction.
(2) Returning Weapon (Ex): Returning weapon will be absolutely indispensable for thrown weapon builds, and pretty lousy for everyone else.
(2) Shield of Fury (Ex): Absolutely de rigeur for avenger vigilantes going with sword-and-board builds. One of the subtlest—but most interesting—parts of shield of fury is that it satisfies the Dexterity prerequisite for any feat that lists Two-Weapon Fighting as a requirement. This change single-handedly opens up heavily armored sword-and-board builds where Dexterity is a dump stat and Strength is king. Lovely not to have to split point buy.
(14) Signature Arrows (Ex): In essence, a 50% discount on magical ammunition for ranged builds. It comes online so late that I’m inclined to think it won’t be of much use.
(2) Silent Dispatch (Ex): Under normal combat circumstances, the Perception DC to notice the sounds of battle is -10, which is so low that most enemies will beat it even if they’re far away and freaking asleep. Silent dispatch simply gives you the opportunity to roll a Stealth check for it at a -5 penalty; whatever you get, that’s the Perception DC. With Skill Focus (Stealth), shadow armor, skill familiarity to take 10, etc., stalker vigilantes really can start to murder enemies without anyone noticing, especially if they have greater invisibility up and running. A good way to continue reaping the benefits of hidden strike.
(2) Tiger’s Claws (Ex): The malleable flesh + morphic weaponry combo will grant more natural attacks than tiger’s claws in the long run, but also requires more investment than tiger’s claws unless you already have the shapechanger subtype from your race. The phrasing in the morphic weaponry rules text suggests to me that the two powers won’t stack, so you’ll have to consider which route you want to take.
(2) Turnabout (Ex): I really love turnabout, flavor-wise, but it’s hard to parlay into combat effectiveness as a vigilante. Truthfully, what you want is a fighter chassis—Step Up, Following Step, Step Up and Strike, Disruptive, and Spellbreaker being the key feats, along with any dirty trick feats you need—that allows you to follow ranged combatants and spellcasters, impede defensive casting, and take an attack of opportunity whenever your target fails to cast defensively. It’s a powerful ability, and not one available anywhere else in the game, but it seems to be more fitting for a magehunting fighter dipping vigilante. Even with the combat skill vigilante talent, single-class vigilantes can’t qualify for Spellbreaker until 20th level, and I think you’d probably want to get the build online a bit faster than that.
(2) Unexpected Strike (Ex): Quick Draw isn’t really an essential feat in most builds. Unexpected strike is fine, but it can’t compete in the same tier as other vigilante talents like lethal grace or take ‘em alive.
(2) Volatile Arrows (Ex): Call me crazy for taking a pass on alchemist bombs, but I don’t like volatile arrows that much. Although it’s fun to see ranged combat styles get some love, vigilantes are not intended to be built with high Intelligence modifiers, and the number of vigilante talents you get doesn’t allow you to invest much in extra iterations of volatile arrows. If you want my opinion, you should just build a grenadier alchemist if you’re seriously considering volatile arrows as your main mode of attack.
(2) Whip of Vengeance (Ex): It will surprise no one that whip of vengeance is obligatory for whip builds, which can be built pretty easily on a vigilante, especially with the hangman archetype. The final benefit, using your vigilante level instead of your BAB to qualify for whip feats, is clearly intended for stalker vigilantes—and it’s a nice touch.


(2) Environmental Weapon (Ex): Improvised weapon builds are certainly a thing, but they tend to fall into “intentional improvised weapon builds” that utilize Shikigami Style to deal big damage or “unintentional improvised weapon builds” that use feats like Chairbreaker to continually grab and smash weapons from the environment. I honestly don’t see that environmental weapon is of great benefit even to this latter style of build. Just get Catch Off-Guard and call it quits.




Defense


(2) Shield of Blades (Ex): Shield of blades is best used by avenger vigilantes wielding two-handed weapons. Not only do they receive the greatest damage bonuses from Power Attack, they’re also the most likely to need the extra AC that they’re passing up by not using a shield. The scaling is great, giving you a full +5 AC by 20th level, and doesn’t require you to do any more than you would already have been doing. Note that you can in fact use Furious Focus with shield of blades, but that you’ll have to wait until BAB +6 and your first iterative attack to do so.
(2) Steely Resolve (Ex): Frankly speaking, companion to the lonely is more mechanically powerful than steely resolve, and, critically, doesn’t require a vigilante talent slot. Where steely resolve has an edge, however, is that it doesn’t require you to engage in…*hem,* consensual activities with anyone to receive the bonuses. Mind-affecting effects, it goes without saying, are the worst of the worst: charm person, fear, geas, feeblemind, dominate person, synaptic pulse, color spray, mindwipe, song of discord, confusion, oneiric horror, phantasmal killer, you name it. You absolutely want to have every tool at your disposal available to avoid them.


(2) Armor Skin (Ex): I quite like armor skin—it’s like a fighter’s armor training class feature, but better for a vigilante’s purposes. Acrobatics, Escape Artist, and Stealth are perpetually useful skills for escaping grapples, avoiding attacks of opportunity, moving quietly ahead of the group, etc., so you want bonuses wherever you can get them instead of penalties. Avenger vigilantes get even more bonuses with the heavy training and steel soldier vigilante talents.
(2) Mockery (Ex): Antagonize is one of the only feats in Pathfinder that allows heavily defended PCs to truly “tank” in the MMORPG sense of the word. Antagonize, however, is limited by both its duration and the number of times per day that you can use it on a creature. Mockery solves both of these problems. If you’re a heavily armored avenger vigilante who wants to keep their team safe, you can’t do better than mockery.


(2) Another Day (Ex): In general, you shouldn’t be purchasing abilities that rely on unconsciousness to work. More than that, though, appearing to be dead will confuse your team more than it will confuse your enemies. If your team prevails, they need to waste time determining whether you’re really dead or not, and if your team all falls, you walk away with intense survivor’s guilt—assuming that the monster doesn’t just eat you and really make you dead.




Mobility


(2) Close the Gap (Ex): Monsters with huge reach are a fact of life in high-level Pathfinder combat, and melee-focused PCs typically lack the ability to dance in under the guard to deal damage. No longer! Close the gap makes attacks of opportunity far, far less common. You Want That.™ Plus, eliminating the penalty for charging is pretty perky, too. Maybe consider some charge-based style feats, or the mad rush avenger vigilante talent?
(6) Fantastic Stride (Ex): Spring Attack has always been a good feat, but most people are put off (justifiably so) by the oodles of prerequisites that it has. With those gone, it’s actually okay!
(2) Sure-Footed (Ex): The initial benefits of sure-footed are certainly nice, especially for stalker vigilantes, but what really clinches the talent’s blue status is the 8th-level upgrade. No more difficult terrain, whether magical or natural? Huge score.


(10) Leap and Bound (Ex): Haha, that’s a lot of fun. Between armor skin, leap and bound, and possibly heavy training, even heavily armored vigilantes can start parkouring around the battlefield like they’re in the opening of Casino Royale. The bit about being able to grapple flying creatures is particularly interesting for grapple builds, who may want to investigate the talent as a means of snagging flyers and bringing them back down to earth.
(5) Monkey’s Paws (Ex): Requires rooftop infiltrator. Whether anyone will actually spend two vigilante talents to acquire a climb speed is doubtful, but that’s not to say that monkey’s paws is a bad ability—not at all. Bonuses to Escape Artist checks are always relevant, too, especially for ¾-BAB stalker vigilantes.
(2) Shadow’s Speed (Ex): Increases to base speed improve your Acrobatics checks to jump, and stack with just about any other kind of movement speed increase—longstrider, slipstream, haste, and the like. I certainly wouldn’t kick shadow’s speed out of bed for eating crackers.


(2) Rooftop Infiltrator (Ex): Even a slow climbing speed is better than none at all. Rooftop infiltrator will double the speed at which you can climb unaided and quadruple the speed at which you can climb a rope, not to mention that the Climb skill bonuses will ensure that you can almost never fall.




Combat Maneuvers


(2) Expose Weaknesses (Ex): This talent is a really nice addition to a dirty trick build. Although the ideal is that every member of a team has tools for dealing with DR of all types, the reality will often be that only one person—or no one—does. Expose weaknesses therefore acts as a force multiplier for the team in a way that simple status conditions don’t. You could probably save it for 8th or 10th level, though, as DR isn’t prevalent before that point.


(2) Cunning Feint (Ex): Cunning feint is essentially Improved Feint and Greater Feint packaged as one vigilante talent, plus a little bit of Two-Weapon Feint. The option to feint as part of a full-attack is particularly nice, as it ensures that high-level avenger vigilantes can get two or three attacks off after an opponent has already been denied their Dexterity bonus to AC. Pairs nicely with the vital punishment vigilante talent so that you can feint as a move action and then hit someone with a standard action Vital Strike.
(2) Gator Wrangle (Ex): Gator wrangle is part defense, part offense, part combat maneuver, but when you put all the parts together, it’s actually not bad! Grapples are most characters’ worst nightmare, so getting huge bonuses to avoid grapples, reduce common damage from grapples, and punish those who grapple you will ensure that your grappled experience is much nicer. Heck, you might even try to get grappled, if it keeps a creature from grappling squishier allies.
(2) Living Shield (Ex): Because you lose your grapple-specific CMB bonuses, living shield won’t always work the way you want it to, even for very specialized grapplers. When it does, though, it’s hilarious, both saving you some damage and forcing your enemy to eat that same damage.
(2) Shackle Smash (Ex): You’d be surprised at how relevant shackle smash really is—not necessarily for its in-combat applications with the sunder maneuver, unless you’re into that kind of thing, but for its applications in smashing down doors, walls, floors, and any other obstacles you might need to get through. Quiet and efficient are the watchwords here.


(2) Brutal Maneuver (Ex): I won’t say that combat maneuvers are totally useless options in Pathfinder, only that they’re often heavily outclassed by other available options. Brutal maneuver might boost your damage a little, but the penalty to attack rolls is simply not worth it in many cases. Probably viable only for avenger vigilantes, thanks to their higher BAB.
(6) Concealed Strike (Ex): Intended to be a “curse your sudden but inevitable betrayal!” talent that lets you backstab unsuspecting foes before they can react. Hard to see why a feint would be useful here—if you’re getting a surprise round, you’ve already made the enemy flat-footed.
(2) Deceitful Trick (Ex): Deceitful trick does the same basic thing for the dirty trick maneuver that cunning feint does for the feint maneuver, but where cunning feint gives you the Improved and Greater versions of its combat maneuver for free, deceitful trick does not. Still nice to be able to inflict two status conditions and perform a combat maneuver as part of a full-attack action.
(2) Favored Maneuver (Ex): Repeat the refrain with me: vigilante talents are worth more than feats. You want an Improved [Maneuver] feat, my recommendation is that you pick it up in your free time, not with vigilante talents. If, in spite of my recommendation, you would still like to use a vigilante talent, the option is here.
(2) Pull into the Shadows (Ex): AKA the Batman Begins move. It suits stalker vigilantes thematically, but mechanically fits only avenger vigilantes. Hard to get excited when only totally unaware enemies can be affected.


(2) Combat Expertise (Ex): Always, always remember this: vigilante talents are worth way more than feats. If they’re doing the same thing as a feat, they’re not worth your time. Combat Expertise in particular is a heinous feat tax that no one should spend a vigilante talent on. Heck, I typically play with Elephant in the Room feat tax rules at my tables, and that just gives everyone Combat Expertise for free. Perhaps the one exception here is for bellflower harvesters, who need Intelligence 13 and Combat Expertise to qualify for Swift Aid, Team Up, Open Up, and Got Your Back.
(2) Vortex Splash (Ex): Being able to feint or dirty trick every adjacent target is a great benefit, but you can’t possibly expect to be fighting in rain or waist-deep water regularly enough to merit a vigilante talent.




Spellcasting


(2) Magical Familiarity (Sp): Ugh, magical familiarity might as well be a rogue talent, it’s so bad. If you want cantrips, buy something like a hand of the mage. Don’t waste your precious, non-refundable vigilante talents.
(4) Major Magic (Sp): A bad upgrade to a bad vigilante talent, major magic has nothing to offer players that couldn’t be better accomplished by the vigilante class’ many spellcasting archetypes.
(2) Minor Magic (Sp): Somehow even worse than magical familiarity. And you thought it couldn’t be done!




Utility


(2) Malleable Flesh (Su): Malleable flesh is wacky, weird, and I love it. In addition to getting an at-will, non-magical disguise self, malleable flesh lets you squeeze through extremely narrow openings and opens up the morphic weaponry and seamless shapechanger vigilante talents. There’s really a lot to like here, despite the minor loss of seamless guise bonuses.


(2) Inspired Vigilante (Ex): Without investigator talents or the ability to use inspiration for free on certain skill checks, there’s a certain je ne sais quoi missing from inspired vigilante. It’s not terrible, just not nearly as useful as the investigator class feature.
(2) One with the Wild (Ex): Requires environmental weapon vigilante talent. The prereq is kind of a stinker, and only gives you a limited form of the ranger’s favored terrain. I dunno. I like the skills getting bonuses, but competence bonuses are common, especially for skill checks, and can be easily duplicated with cheap wondrous items.
(2) Racial Paragon (Ex): I’m going to rate this talent yellow, since its utility will vary wildly depending on which race your vigilante is, which racial feats are available to that race, and which situations those feats are useful in. For some races, getting the equivalent of the brawler’s martial flexibility class feature for racial feats could be extremely powerful; for other races, it could be laughably weak.
(2) Shadow’s Sight (Ex): If you already have darkvision, you can probably skip shadow’s sight. If you don’t have darkvision, though, you’d normally have to pay 12,000 gp and take away your eye slot with goggles of night in order to get it. I can think of much worse things to spend a vigilante talent on.
(2) Team Player (Ex): Swift Aid isn’t a particularly powerful feat, but bellflower harvesters can take advantage of the archetype’s bonuses to become superb at aid another actions.


(2) Chase Master (Ex): Please name for me the last time you were in a chase. I’ll wait.
(2) Instant Plan (Ex): Eh. The morale bonuses are easily superseded by heroism, and they don’t apply to really critical checks even when you don’t have duplicate morale bonuses floating around.
(2) Perfect Fall (Ex): Boots of the cat cost 1,000 gp and do a better job of preventing lethal fall damage than this talent does. And anyway, it’s not like flight is uncommon once you get past about 7th level.
(2) Swamp Concoctions (Ex): Only 2/day, and with a bad prereq. Yawn.

---

# Avenger Specialization

Avenger Specialization


Offense


(12) Mad Rush (Ex): Well, sure, Paizo, if you’re going to offer me freaking pounce, of course I’m going to rate that purple. The AC penalty is problematic, but can be offset with shield of blades, close the gap, and other AC-boosting effects. Not to mention that a full-attack pasted onto the end of a charge action is much more likely to outright kill your target than a single attack is, making mad rush somewhat safer to use than it appears at first blush.


(2) Fist of the Avenger (Ex): When you combine fist of the avenger, take ‘em alive, steel soldier, and Dragon Style (facilitated by a two-level dip in scaled fist unchained monk for flurry of blows), you can get some truly frightening bonuses to unarmed strikes on an avenger vigilante. I’m going to be making a sample build with all of the above, so look for that down in the builds section.


(2) Signature Weapon (Ex): Weapon Focus is an unfortunate feat tax in many builds, so you might truly want signature weapon to smooth that tax away. Weapon Specialization is fine on its own, but deserves a special shout-out for ranged builds, who need it to unlock Point-Blank Master, and Dazzling Display builds, who often won’t want to have to purchase Weapon Focus à la carte. “Nice, but not essential” is the motto here.
(2) Sweeping Strike (Ex): Cool, Cleave and Great Cleave for free, plus the ability to initiate a Great Cleave even if you miss your first attack. If you’ve got access to size increases or other abilities that will allow you to reach more foes, sweeping strike sets you up nicely for melee carnage, but it can also be easily ignored if you don’t prefer Cleave tactics.


(2) Combat Skill (Ex): The only, and I mean only reason to ever take combat skill is if you need a fighter-exclusive feat. Otherwise, you’re making a 1:1 talent-to-feat trade, and that trade is almost never worth it.
(2) Sucker Punch (Ex): Nearly all melee vigilantes have to at least consider whether they want to create a nonlethal damage build, given how powerful the take ‘em alive vigilante talent is, and given how effective the Enforcer feat is at demoralizing foes. Sucker punch is fine for these builds, but faces the usual obstacle that enemies must be completely unaware of the vigilante in order for it to function. This honestly feels like more of a stalker ability, so I’m frankly surprised that avengers are the intended audience.


(2) Weapon Familiarity (Ex): Weapon familiarity is strictly inferior to signature weapon unless you desperately need access to proficiency for an exotic weapon. Even then, you may be better off with a one- or two-level dip into a class that grants you proficiency, rather than wasting a vigilante talent on it.




Defense


(2) Heavy Training (Ex): There are Dexterity-based avenger vigilantes, but many full-BAB builds go with Strength as their main combat attribute because of the superior damage such builds typically offer. Heavy training pairs nicely with these Strength builds, allowing you to safely dump Dexterity while maintaining a good AC value. The fact that armor skin also applies to heavy armor with heavy training is only icing on a very nice cake.
(2) Unkillable (Ex): There are a bunch of bonuses packed into this talent, all of them good. Diehard is a polarizing feat, but leads to some wonderful prereqs; what’s more, the 12th-level benefit ensures that you can stay alive, well, forever, so long as it was HP damage that “killed” you, and so long as your team is quick enough to use healing magic on you.


(2) Steel Soldier (Ex): Alright, let’s unpack this talent. You modify a set of armor that you’re proficient with, paying both a time cost and a financial cost to do so. Once modified, the armor (1) can be concealed on your person with Sleight of Hand, and (2) ports over your armor’s enhancement bonus to the enhancement bonus of any gauntlets you use to attack. The first benefit is mediocre, since it’s often easier for heavy armor users to conceal the fact that they’re wearing armor (using the glamered armor enchantment, etc.) rather than take it off and put it on again. The second benefit is astoundingly good for unarmed strike/gauntlet builds, and will save you tens of thousands of gold on amulets of mighty fists or enchanted gauntlets/handwraps. You’ll definitely want to purchase steel soldier if you’re building toward unarmed strikes.




Mobility


(2) Nothing Can Stop Me (Ex): As hilarious as it is to Kool-Aid Man your way through walls, doors, etc., nothing can stop me is in no way a priority for most combat builds, which means that it will get set aside much of the time in favor of more relevant vigilante talents.




Stalker Specialization

---

# Offense

Offense


(2) Twisting Fear (Ex): Haaahahaha, Paizo really outdid themselves on this one. Twisting fear turns any fear effect you have (Cornugon Smash, Enforcer, Dazzling Display, etc.) into a sustainable nonlethal damage source. For stalkers, Cornugon Smash might not be the preferred form factor, but Enforcer and Dazzling Display are aces for single-target and AoE demoralization, respectively. If you’re going for AoE fear, you’ll probably want Disheartening Display so that you can deal the damage in two successive rounds, and any twisting fear build will want Signature Skill (Intimidate) so that you can crush Intimidate DCs and start enemies at frightened or even panicked. Endless fun!


(4) Up Close and Personal (Ex): Let’s get one thing clear off the bat: unless you’ve heavily invested in Acrobatics, stalker vigilantes are not going to want to use this talent on huge targets with monstrous CMD scores. Where up close and personal really finds its stride is in tackling pint-sized enemies, spellcasters, and other squishy targets, none of whom will be able to effectively prevent you from shanking them as you travel through their square. Especially if an enemy is not currently flanked or denied their Dexterity bonus to AC, using this talent will often beat the damage from a full-attack action based on hidden strike’s contribution alone. Pairs exceptionally well with the mighty ambush hidden strike talent.


(6) Sniper (Ex): Required for ranged stalker builds. If you’re using thrown weapons with a small range increment, I’d skip sniper—you shouldn’t be attacking from more than two range increments away with shuriken, for example.




Defense


(2) Evasive (Ex): Evasion and improved evasion, as the rogue class feature. Easy purchase, between your strong Reflex save progression and the high likelihood that stalker vigilantes will choose Dexterity as their main combat attribute.


(2) Stalker Sense (Ex): Uncanny dodge and improved uncanny dodge are a little less potent than evasion and improved evasion, but still perhaps worth your investment if you like to live in melee range.

---

# Utility

Utility


(8) Hide in Plain Sight (Ex): Look, stalker vigilantes are supposed to be sneaky snakes, and you can’t be a sneaky snake unless you can use Stealth whenever you need it. HiPS does that for you, and can be combined with the sure-footed vigilante talent to make you fast and silent.


(2) Blind Spot (Ex): Awww, blind spot, you could have been so good! The effect here is similar to the Dampen Presence feat, but where Dampen Presence just shuts down blindsense and blindsight, no questions asked, blind spot instead grants monsters scaling +20 bonuses to Perception to notice you for each sense they have. This is no bueno. Even if you’re invisible, it’s not going to take much for monsters (especially high-level monsters) to spot you in an instant. I debated a red rating here, but it does still have some uses.


(2) Rogue Talent (Ex): Look, I’m going to be honest: the vast majority of rogue talents are bad, plain and simple. Unchained rogue talents are better, and a benevolent GM might allow you to take those talents, but those are not the rules as written. Honestly, you’re better off going into the teisatsu archetype, which unlocks ninja tricks—a much better proposition than rogue talents.

---

# Hidden Strike

Hidden Strike


(10) Mighty Ambush (Ex): Stalker vigilantes are truly the masters of taking foes out before they get the chance to react! Pending a failed Fortitude save, mighty ambush simply drops enemies unconscious, whereupon you can coup de grace at your leisure if they’re isolated from their friends. Silent dispatch is a useful adjunct to mighty ambush, since it helps fewer enemies become aware that an attack is taking place, and up close and personal is practically mandatory so that you can attempt to knock each enemy out (even those that are aware of you!) at least once in a combat. Doing so greatly shifts the odds of any encounter in your favor.


(2) Foe Collision (Ex): Unlike many vigilante talents that modify hidden strike, foe collision will work even if your enemy is aware that you’re there, but you’ll still need a means of denying them their Dexterity bonus to AC, such as Shatter Defenses, dirty tricks to blind someone, or a feint—mere flanking won’t cut it. At any rate, a solid hidden strike talent for dealing damage in a wider area.
(4) Throat Jab (Ex): The 1/day per enemy restriction is a little onerous, but even so, throat jab is a fantastic way of taking out isolated sentries or spellcasters without anyone being the wiser. Pair with the silent dispatch vigilante talent for maximum effect.


(2) Leave an Opening (Ex): A little extra damage after a successful hidden strike doesn’t excite me too much, although it pairs nicely with vital punishment and animal patron (snake), if that’s your jam.
________________
VIG 305: Feats


        As a mostly-martial class that can play like a fighter, rogue, or something in between, the parameter space that a vigilante can occupy is incredibly wide. I’ve tried to pick out the cream of the crop for you, but what exactly you choose to take will depend almost entirely on your build.

---

# Core Combat Feats

Core Combat Feats


Universal


Nature Soul / Animal Ally / Boon Companion: The classic three-feat trade for a fully scaling animal companion. You’ve got a good Charisma score for Handle Animal, so perhaps a decent investment.
Champion of [Alignment]: Your GM may not be planning to play with the shifts and affirmations subsystem from Pathfinder Unchained, but if they’re open to the idea, many of these feats give you some great weapon/spell damage bonuses in addition to powerful spell-like abilities. Worth investigating.
Divine Fighting Technique: A great, flavorful, versatile feat for vigilantes. Cayden Cailean, Gorum, Iomedae, Lamashtu, Sarenrae, Torag, and Urgathoa are generally thought to have the strongest styles, but I need to give a special shout-out to Norgorber’s Silent Shiv for stalker vigilantes. The main issue with stalker vigilantes is that once an enemy becomes aware of your presence, you lose a decent chunk of your combat efficacy—everything from your downgraded d8 → d4 hidden strike damage dice, to your ability to apply hidden strike rider talents, to the efficacy of startling, frightening, and stunning appearance is affected. In defending this feat, then, I’m just going to quote from Norgorber’s rule text: “At the start of each round, choose one opponent that you can see and attempt a Stealth check as a swift action. If the result of your check exceeds the target’s CMD, treat the target as if it were completely unaware of your presence for the purpose of the initial benefit of this divine fighting technique. If you beat the target’s CMD by 5 or more, you also treat it as being completely unaware of your presence for the purposes of all class features and feats you have.” In other words, if you can succeed at a high enough Stealth check, none of your combat efficacy goes away. Deal your full d8 hidden strike damage all the time! Proc startling appearance continuously! Use the foe collision and leave an opening vigilante talents every round! This is truly the most powerful combo in the game that I know of for stalker vigilantes, and grows to ridiculous levels of effectiveness when you have feats like Skill Focus (Stealth) or Dampen Presence, magical items like armor with the greater shadow enchantment, and vigilante talents like sure-footed, owl’s sight, hide in plain sight, and skill familiarity pumping your Stealth score through the roof.
Flickering Step: You’re telling me you couldn’t use a free dimension door a few times per day?
Improved Initiative: Never, ever a wasted feat.
Martial Focus: Martial Focus is a noteworthy feat because it gives you access to weapon mastery feats—normally feats that are exclusive to the fighter class—for a relatively small investment. Cut from the Air, Smash from the Air, and Spellcut are classics for ensuring that martial classes need never fear a spellcaster or archer ever again, but Burrowing Shot, Devastating Assault, and Weapon Style Mastery can also be good in the right builds.
Noble Scion (Scion of War): Swapping Dexterity for Charisma on initiative checks is a great idea for Strength-based vigilantes who wear heavy armor and like the smashy-smashy. It’s likely to add up to at least Improved Initiative over time, if not more.
Wanderer’s Fortune: Wanderer’s Fortune effectively eliminates any fear you might have had about being grappled or swallowed whole.


Additional Traits: Heck yeah! Get you some additional traits.
Barroom Brawler: Barroom Brawler is a limited version of the brawler class’ martial flexibility feature, and although you may not necessarily have the build space to purchase it, it’s definitely a powerful versatility option for vigilantes of all stripes.
Big Game Hunter: It’s not the most exciting feat on the block, but it does make you better at attacking the Large and larger enemies you’ll find in high-level Pathfinder play.
Canny Tumble: Although the prereqs are kinda nutty, stalker vigilantes who want to consistently proc hidden strike damage may wish to investigate Canny Tumble. It’s way easier to get working if you’re using the Elephant in the Room feat tax rules, which combine Dodge and Mobility into one feat.
Combat Reflexes / Combat Patrol: A pretty easy feat combo to flex into, thanks to the vigilante’s reflexes talent, and excellent for avenger vigilantes using reach weapons to set up massive “oh no no zones” where any movement of any kind triggers an attack of opportunity. Pair with the vital punishment vigilante talent for extra chips and guac.
Critical Focus: A classic feat for both avengers and stalkers, although avengers will tend to have both more attacks and less reliance on non-multiplying precision damage.
Vital Strike / Improved / Greater / Devastating Strike / Improved Devastating Strike: Vigilantes can get their hands on Vital Strike pretty easily, thanks to the vital punishment vigilante talent. Not only does Devastating Strike allow you to apply the bonus damage to a Vital Strike as normal for the feat, vital punishment + Devastating Strike would allow you to apply the bonus damage to an attack of opportunity 1/round!
Great Fortitude: Vigilantes are, somewhat bafflingly, a Fortitude-weak class. Can’t walk around failing saves against poisons and nausea, ya hear?
Ghostslayer: Everybody hates incorporeal fights, right? Well, Ghostslayer is a ghost touch weapon, but as a feat. Sure can come in handy, especially for stalkers, who need to deal hidden strike damage like a diabetic needs a new pancreas.
Improved Critical: Avengers will be most likely to get a lot of use out of Improved Critical, as they’re going to be attacking more often and dealing higher static damage than stalkers. Stalkers, who deal a good chunk of their damage through non-multiplying hidden strike dice, won’t get nearly as much out of crits.
Lunge: Especially for reach builds, Lunge can be an excellent feat, allowing you to tag enemies farther and farther outside of your normal threatened range. Remember that your reach increase ends when your turn ends, so you can’t take attacks of opportunity while lunging
Power Attack: As with Deadly Aim and Piranha Strike, Strength-based avengers will want Power Attack; stalkers will most likely not.
Toughness: More HP. Not thrilling, but serviceable.
Weapon Versatility: Probably the simplest, cheapest way to get around DR/damage type. That’s not usually penetrable with enhancement bonuses, so the feat is well worth considering.


Anatomical Savant: Of some use to high-level stalker vigilantes, who may need to deal with precision damage immunities regularly. Not perfect, but better than bringing nothing to the fight.
Arcane Strike / Riving Strike: Certain vigilante archetypes (cabalist, magical child, warlock) turn the vigilante into an arcane caster. Arcane Strike and Riving Strike are good feats for keeping damage and spell save DCs up in such builds.
Armor Proficiency, Heavy: I suppose you could be a heavily armored stalker; avengers will mostly just take the heavy training vigilante talent and reap the benefits there instead of taking this feat.
Armored Athlete: Fine, I suppose, if you’re not taking the armor skin vigilante talent.
Butterfly’s Sting: Desna worship and Combat Expertise requirements notwithstanding, Butterfly’s Sting is a wonderful feat, especially for stalker vigilantes, who will deal most of their damage through hidden strike. Instead of unsuccessfully trying to multiply that precision damage, you can instead pass the crit off to allies!
Deceitful Incompetence: Quite good for reach builds, which will be interested in hitting with every attack of opportunity they possibly can.
Dodge / Mobility: Although Dodge and Mobility lead into a ton of feat combos, the main ones that you’d consider are Spring Attack, Crane Style, Osyluth Guile, and perhaps Canny Tumble. If you’re just here for Spring Attack, skip it and grab the fantastic stride vigilante talent. If you’re here for other purposes, you have my blessing to proceed.
Equipment Trick (Thunderstone: Startling Noise): AoE hidden strike damage is great for stalker vigilantes, although you’ll have to put up with the low save DCs on thunderstones.
Weapon Focus: So many feats depend on Weapon Focus, it’s not even funny. Take it if you need prerequisites out of the way, but otherwise leave it alone.


Blind-Fight: While Blind-Fight is a good feat, vigilantes (especially stalker vigilantes) are better off just taking the strike the unseen vigilante talent to acquire Blind-Fight, Improved Blind-Fight, Greater Blind-Fight, and the equivalent of Improved Shadow Strike.
Careful Sneak: The armor skin vigilante talent does a much better job of enabling stealth builds, if that’s what you’re looking for.
Diehard: Even if you wanted Diehard, only avenger vigilantes have any business using it, and the unkillable vigilante talent does a much, much better job of things than the feat does. Now I have a machine gun. Ho ho ho.
Shadow Strike: Stalker vigilantes can already deal hidden strike damage to targets with concealment! If you want to deal precision damage to targets with displacement or other forms of total concealment, the strike the unseen vigilante talent does a much better job.
Spring Attack / Improved / Greater: Again, replicated better by the fantastic stride vigilante talent.

---

# Melee (Two-Handed)

Melee (Two-Handed)


Furious Focus: An easy picks for two-handed vigilantes of any specialization. You want the extra damage that Power Attack will provide, and you want your attack rolls to be as juicy as possible, even if your first attack is the most likely to hit anyway. Note that you have to have at least one iterative attack that takes the Power Attack penalty in order to use the shield of blades vigilante talent.


Shield of Swings: Shield of Swings is a decent feat for two-handed builds that want some defensive options. Your damage output will definitely suffer, but against low-level trash mobs that are going to die easily and have a hard time hitting your improved AC, the tradeoff might be worth it.

---

# Melee (Two-Weapon Fighting)

Melee (Two-Weapon Fighting)


Possessed Hand / Hand’s Autonomy: Who knew that two-weapon fighters could get so much mileage out of detaching a hand? In addition to a +1 insight bonus to attack and damage rolls and a reduction in TWF penalties, possessed hands can do some wacky, awesome things while you’re incapacitated.
Two-Weapon Fighting / Improved / Greater: Required for the playstyle, for obvious reasons.
Two-Weapon Rend: Once you have enough attacks, Two-Weapon Rend is essentially a free attack every round, which is fantastic.


Double Slice: You want full damage on off-hand attacks, thanks very much.
Heaven’s Step: You’ll need Step Up, Following Step, and Step Up and Strike to get the most mileage out of Heaven’s Step, but man, it’s a lethal combo for magehunters.

---

# Melee (Sword and Board)

Melee (Sword and Board)


Shield Focus: It’s no one’s favorite feat, but you can’t really get anywhere in shield tactics without Shield Focus. That’s the way that it goes.
Shield Slam / Shield Master / Bashing Finish: Lots and lots of good stuff in these three feats, although obviously only sword-and-board avengers will be interested.
Two-Weapon Fighting / Improved / Greater: Although I still think it’s a little silly that sword and board builds have to get the TWF feats in addition to all the shield feats, Paizo wrote the system that way for what I can only assume are good reasons. You’ll need these if you’re going to deal maximum damage as a sword and board avenger.


Stumbling Bash / Toppling Bash: Some free debuffing and combat maneuvers, although frankly you probably won’t have the build space to take them, given the many feats that sword and board builds need to succeed.


Improved Shield Bash: Granted for free by the shield of fury vigilante talent.

---

# Melee (Unarmed)

Melee (Unarmed)


Cerberus Style / Snare / Crush: Cerberus Style is a strong unarmed strike style in general, and doubly so for the vigilante class, which gets so many bonuses on unarmed strike builds. Avengers can easily get everything they need for a Cerberus Style build with the fist of the avenger, vital punishment, and sweeping strike vigilante talents. This will eventually allow them to hit three opponents per round with Greater Vital Strike, preventing each from taking 5-foot steps or using teleportation magic.
