Dragon Style / Roar / Ferocity: Dragon Style is a classic fighting style for unarmed builds, packaging great bonuses to damage with some other nifty buffs. This style feat line will probably be most applicable to vigilantes who are taking a scaled fist unchained monk dip.
Possessed Hand: A +1 insight bonus to attack and damage rolls, plus a few skill rolls. Pretty great.
Snake Style / Sidewind / Fang: Assuming you’re willing to invest in Sense Motive, Snake Style can be an amazing style feat line, giving you the chance to take lots of attacks of opportunity and negate enemy hits with little effort. Pairs amazingly well with the animal patron (snake) vigilante talent to get huge bonuses to hit with your attacks of opportunity, as well as the skill familiarity social talent to ensure that you can take 10 on Sense Motive checks in combat.


Boar Style / Ferocity / Shred: Boar Style is a nice unarmed strike style feat line that does a lot of what vigilantes want to do: deal some bleed damage, demoralize as a free action, get around DR/damage type, and more.
Chaos Reigns: An extra slam attack for the price of one feat! Not bad at all.
Crane Style / Wing / Riposte: Defensive fighting builds are somewhat niche, but a halfling avenger vigilante with Crane Style, Crane Wing, Crane Riposte, Osyluth Guile, Cautious Fighter, Blundering Defense, and Uncanny Defense can get ridiculous bonuses to AC, CMD, Reflex saves, and more while taking only a minor penalty to attack rolls.
Outslug Style / Weave / Sprint: A very solid style feat line for unarmed or gauntlet builds, Outslug Style gives you offense, defense, reach, and mobility, all packaged into one.


Adder Strike: Unarmed strike builds are eminently doable for many vigilantes, and with the poisoner vigilante talent, you can both synthesize your own poison and qualify for Adder Strike. It’s great for applying poisons to enemies without sucking up too many of your actions.
Belier’s Bite: A little extra bleed damage never hurts, but you might do better with the Boar Style feat line.
Shielded Gauntlet Style / Attack / Master: Gauntlet builds are surprisingly good for avenger vigilantes, between steel soldier, take ‘em alive, and fist of the avenger, and the Shielded Gauntlet Style feat line—especially the latter two feats—can really, really pump up your damage and AC. Shielded Gauntlet Attack, for example, can take your gauntlet damage from 1d3 at 20th level to 2d6 at 20th level. Definitely no slouch!


Improved Unarmed Strike: Nearly no one should take Improved Unarmed Strike with one of their normal feats. Either get it through the fist of the avenger vigilante talent, or grab a monk/brawler dip to get it. (That said, IUS is obviously essential to unarmed strike builds, and can’t be ignored at all.)

---

# Melee (Improvised)

Melee (Improvised)


Catch Off-Guard: Improvised weapon builds need the ability to use improvised weapons without penalties and Catch Off-Guard is the main feat you’ll use to achieve that goal.
Shikigami Style / Mimicry / Manipulation: Shikigami Style was introduced relatively late in 1e’s run, and it really changes the game for improvised weapon builds. The ultimate goal is to wield a CL 20 wondrous item in order to benefit both from three effective size increases (1d6 → 1d10 → 2d8 → 3d8 for two-handed weapons, or slightly less for one-handed weapons) and from a +5 enhancement bonus. Sovereign glue is the classic example, as a CL 20 wondrous item that only costs 2400 gp, but you can also use a traveler’s any-tool to get a +2 sledge weapon as soon as 5th level. It’s a ridiculous and cheesy feat line, but c’mon, who wouldn’t want to wield a bottle of glue with as much lethality as a holy avenger? Only idiots, my friends. Only idiots. Of note, Shikigami Style builds make some of the best Vital Strike builds in the game, because you multiply your already-boosted base damage dice when calculating Vital Strike damage.


Chairbreaker: Shikigami Style is a much more potent avenue for vigilantes to pursue, and doesn’t require you to destroy weapons in order to deal good damage.

---

# Melee (Weapon Finesse)

Melee (Weapon Finesse)


Agile Maneuvers: Dexterity-based avengers might be interested in Agile Maneuvers, but they’re about the only ones. Stalkers probably shouldn’t bother because of their ¾ BAB progression.
Piranha Strike: As with Deadly Aim and Power Attack, avengers will want Piranha Strike; stalkers will most likely not.


Weapon Finesse + [Weapon] Grace / Bladed Brush / Dance of Chains / Dervish Dance: The lethal grace vigilante talent is the optimal means of building a finesse vigilante, whether avenger or stalker. Set your Dexterity as high as you can, park your Strength at 12-14 (or more, if you can afford it!) and let the talent do the rest—no need to go grabbing three feats to accomplish what one talent can accomplish.

---

# Ranged

Ranged


Clustered Shots: DR penalizes lots of low-damage shots quite harshly, which makes Clustered Shots vital for most ranged builds, especially those that use Rapid Shot. You’ll want it.
Manyshot: Probably of more interest to avengers than stalkers, especially since stalkers don’t get to double-proc hidden strike damage on a manyshot. Regardless, blue for anyone who has the feats, the desire, and a bow.
Point-Blank Master: Only accessible to avenger vigilantes who took the signature weapon vigilante talent, Point-Blank Master is a gem for being able to continue shooting enemies who close the distance and get up into your face.
Point-Blank Shot / Precise Shot / Rapid Shot / Improved Precise Shot: The feats in this list are presented in roughly the order you’d take them in—if you’re using Elephant in the Room feat tax rules, feel free to skip Point-Blank Shot. Anyway, each feat here is absolutely essential to a ranged combat build, and ensures that you can hit hard and hit often.


Amateur Gunslinger: A better option for gun-toting vigilantes than the gunmaster archetype, and that’s saying something. Quick clear should be your deed of choice.
Deadly Aim: Avengers will be the only ones who can take full advantage of Deadly Aim’s bonuses to damage; stalkers will likely need to pass due to their slower BAB progression.
Hammer the Gap: A decent little damage bonus for ranged avengers.
Overwatch Style / Tactician / Vortex: Overwatch Style is fun for interrupting multiple casters, striking multiple enemies when they become visible, etc., and can yield some great results for Pathfinder players who really understand tactics.
Reckless Aim: Reckless Aim can actually prove to have a pretty big upside, assuming you’re judicious about when you use it. If you’re not currently threatened by an enemy, it’s essentially a free +2 to hit, and if you target a crowd of enemies, rolling a natural 1 will still allow you to hit someone. Just maybe don’t use it on targets your allies are threatening.
Snap Shot / Improved / Greater: Snap Shot and its older cousins allow you to threaten enemies with your bow and take ranged attacks of opportunity. It’s not hugely important for vigilantes, but always nice to have.


Expert Sniper / Master Sniper: Potentially of use to stalker vigilantes who want to remain hidden while continuing to attack enemies.


Rapid Reload / Crossbow Mastery: There’s really no reason for a vigilante to be using crossbows or guns, and yes, that includes the abysmal gunmaster archetype. Skip.




Ancillary Feats

---

# Anticaster

Anticaster


Cut from the Air / Smash from the Air / Spellcut: These three feats are incredible magehunting feats that can be selected if you take the Martial Focus feat at an earlier level. Cutting arrows and ray spells out of the sky, substituting your BAB for your save bonus 1/round…it’s all coming together.
Step Up / Following Step / Step Up and Strike: I’m amazed that I don’t hear of people taking Step Up more often. First, it’s great against ranged enemies and spellcasters. Ranged enemies will be unable to escape you without an Acrobatics check or full withdraw action, and with clever use of readied actions, spellcasters can be interrupted every time they try to cast a spell, most likely breaking their concentration. Second, it allows you to stop the use of most magical items. If an enemy five-foot steps away, you follow; if they try to draw a potion, scroll, etc., you use your attack of opportunity to sunder the item. Boom. Third, by the time you get to Following Step, you’re getting some pretty amazing battlefield mobility even when it’s not your turn. Fourth, by the time you get to Step Up and Strike, you’re getting free damage for playing along with tactical movements that all Pathfinder PCs and monsters take all the time. What’s not to love?


Diabolic Style / Humiliation / Judgment: Diabolic Style is an excellent magehunting style for unarmed avenger vigilantes using the vital punishment vigilante talent and Step Up and Strike. If an enemy tries to five-foot step away from you, you follow them (Step Up), hit them with an attack of opportunity (Step Up and Strike) that deals Vital Strike damage (vital punishment), and force them to save or become staggered for at least two rounds. Ouch. Of note, unless you’re planning to take more than one attack of opportunity per round, Diabolic Judgment is probably overkill, since vital punishment already allows you to apply Vital Strike to any attack of opportunity you choose in a round.
Disruptive: Avenger vigilantes can pick up Disruptive at 12th level using the combat skill vigilante talent. (Not that you’d do that when mad rush is available, but I’m just here to point out options.) It’s a good anticaster feat, always, and that rule doesn’t break when the feat is tacked onto the vigilante class.
Divine Defiance: Bonuses to saves vs. divine spells and spell-like abilities. You could do a lot worse.

---

# Cleave

Cleave


Cleave Through / Goblin Cleaver / Orc Hewer / Giant Killer: Why are some of the best Cleave feats dwarf-only? Beats me. Dwarves don’t make fantastic vigilantes, with their penalties to Charisma, but they can be incredibly hardy, and these feats make them the unquestioned kings and queens of cleaving in Pathfinder.


Cleaving Finish / Improved Cleaving Finish: Cleaving Finish lets you whirl through a crowd of minions like they’re nothing, but requires you to be dealing consistently high damage against enemies with consistently low hit points. Good if your GM likes to add more trash mobs to encounters to heighten the difficulty.
Surprise Follow-Through / Improved Surprise Follow-Through: Denying Dexterity to AC is never a bad build goal, especially when you can get it as part of an action you would already be taking. Avenger vigilantes don’t get hidden strike dice, however, which incentivizes them to take Surprise Follow-Through less than stalkers.


Cleaving Sweep: Somewhat nonsensically requires you to be using a greataxe, and forces you to give up your iterative attacks for a chance to knock everyone down. Still, you get to make every trip maneuver at your highest BAB, so it’s a great feat for trip builds that plan on growing to Large size.


Cleave / Great Cleave: Better just to take the sweeping strike vigilante talent to get these bad bois.

---

# Combat Maneuvers

Combat Maneuvers


Chokehold: An excellent feat for grapplers who need to stop an enemy from crying out, casting spells with verbal components, etc. Unfortunately, it doesn’t work on all enemy types, so make sure you have some Knowledge checks under your belt before you try to choke someone out.
Demonic Style / Momentum / Slaughter: Demonic Style is a lovely adjunct for any avengers using the mad rush vigilante talent—an extra +1 to attack and +2 to damage never goes out of fashion. The following feats, Demonic Momentum and Demonic Slaughter, are probably unnecessary unless you’re building heavily for a bull rush build.
Dirty Fighting: If you need to flex into combat maneuvers for some reason, Dirty Fighting is the way to do it. Not only does it give you a (generally) better bonus than Combat Expertise, it also satisfies the prereqs for Combat Expertise, Improved Unarmed Strike, Intelligence 13, and Dexterity 13, at least for the improved maneuver feats or anything that lists them as prerequisites. That’s a lot of bang for your feat buck.
Dirty Trick Master: Dirty trick is among the more effective combat maneuvers, and Dirty Trick Master opens you up to some nasty debuffing combos. Shout-out to the nauseated option, which essentially prevents enemies from removing the status condition (you need a standard action to do so, but nauseated enemies are limited to a single move action per round).
Equipment Trick (Net Tricks): Excellent bonuses for grapple and dirty trick builds.
Improved [Maneuver] / Greater [Maneuver] / Quick [Maneuver]: If you’re building for combat maneuvers, you should basically assume that you need the Improved, Greater, and Quick maneuver feats. No one gets away without them.
Rapid Grappler: 100% useful for grappling builds. Streamline that action economy as much as you can, my droogs.
Superior Dirty Trick: Dirty Trick Master and Superior Dirty Trick are both essential to a high-quality dirty trick build. My advice would be the blinded condition, but YMMV.
Throat Slicer: Assuming you can get good enough at grappling, Throat Slicer will put a quick end to most opponents: you initiate a grapple (standard action) and pin (move action with Greater Grapple) in Round 1, then coup de grace (standard action with Throat Slicer) at the top of Round 2. Fast and lethal.


Bushwhack: Since vigilantes spend so much time attacking from Stealth, Bushwhack is a great adjunct to grapple builds. Grapple someone from invisibility, pin as a free action, and then do whatever you want after that.
Disengaging Feint / Flourish: Fun mobility feats for feint builds. I’m not a huge fan of feint, but the cunning feint vigilante talent does at least make it a viable option for the class.
Equipment Trick (Tanglefoot Bag: Captivating Embrace): Literally gluing yourself to an opponent you want to grapple is hilarious for grapple builds, and confers some pretty strong bonuses.
Felling Smash: Felling Smash is compatible with Vital Strike, making it a nice option for trip builds. Hey, why not take a swift action trip opportunity?


Deadly Grappler: Unfortunately for this feat, most of the damage from avenger vigilante unarmed strike builds comes from static damage bonuses—fist of the avenger grants a static bonus, steel soldier grants a static bonus, take ‘em alive grants a static bonus—that don’t increase your base damage die beyond 1d3, 1d4, and other small numbers. As such, it’s more useful for monks and brawlers, who get the chance to progress their damage to bigger dice, and therefore stand to gain more from increases in effective size.
Unfair Grip: Minor bonuses for grapple builds. Every last bit helps when you’re trying to make combat maneuvers usable.


Combat Expertise: Even for avengers, combat maneuver builds are probably sub-optimal—but if you’re determined to make one work, you’ll need Combat Expertise, guaranteed. The combat expertise vigilante talent is probably worth it in this instance, as the ability to ignore the Intelligence 13 prerequisite for any feat is too tasty to pass up. If you’re playing with Elephant in the Room feat tax rules, Combat Expertise is simply an option given to every character, depending on their BAB, and won’t need to be selected at all.

---

# Demoralize

Demoralize


Cornugon Smash: Melee vigilantes who deal lethal damage (and are therefore ineligible for Enforcer) will do better picking up Cornugon Smash, which lets you demoralize as a free action whenever you hit someone with Power Attack. Avengers in particular can be very frightening with this playstyle.
Dazzling Display / Disheartening Display: Vigilantes are a Charisma-based striker class with no spellcasting, so they’ll always be looking out for ways to use UMD or Intimidate to contribute spellcasting or debuffing to the team where they wouldn’t normally be able to. Dazzling Display is a wonderful AoE debuffing tool; stalker vigilantes especially should strongly consider it for its easy synergy with the twisting fear vigilante talent.
Enforcer  / Bludgeoner: Enforcer is perhaps the single best method of single-target demoralization in Pathfinder, at least against enemies that are susceptible to nonlethal damage. You may need Bludgeoner in some builds to convert warhammers, heavy flails, etc. to nonlethal damage.
Hurtful: A classic feat for wailing on demoralized enemies. More attacks taken as swift actions are always good things!
Shatter Defenses: Hit a shaken enemy with an attack, make them flat-footed until your next turn. Apart from being a great way to continuously proc hidden strike damage and improve your chances to hit, Shatter Defenses is also one of only a handful of abilities in the game that imposes the flat-footed condition; Sap Master builds are the main builds for which the flat-footed vs. Dexterity to AC distinction matters. Note also the excellent synergy between Shatter Defenses and the malevolent armor enchantment.
Signature Skill (Intimidate): It’s relatively easy to make enemies shaken, and you can sometimes ratchet that up to frightened if you use Disheartening Display, but what about more severe fear conditions like panicked or cowering? If you want those, there’s no feat purchase more important than Signature Skill (Intimidate). It takes a while to get going, but by 10th or 15th level, you should be able to make enemies at least frightened—possibly panicked—regularly. Great for peeling enemies away from hard fights.


Dreadful Carnage: While not quite as “demoralize on demand” as Dazzling Display, Dreadful Carnage fits better into a two-handed weapon build and doesn’t require nearly the action cost as Dazzling Display. An interesting choice to consider.
Equipment Trick (Cape: Dazzling Trail): An extra 1d4 rounds on any demoralize effect ain’t bad at all.
Golden League Tattoos: Take 10 on Intimidate checks. Pretty nice, especially if you’re not an ifrit with the Fiery Glare trait.
Gory Finish: Gory Finish doesn’t permit full-attack actions, but it does permit Vital Strikes, so you can use it on weakened enemies when you have to move up to them or know they’re on their last legs.
Shadows of Fear: Free sneak attack damage as long as you’re targeting a shaken creature. Yes, stalker vigilantes will be more than happy to have that, as it guarantees hidden strike damage at least once per round against enemies that aren’t immune to fear effects.


Frightening Ambush: Stalker vigilantes in particular love to attack enemies from Stealth, but many will have better modes of demoralization—Enforcer, Dazzling Display, etc. If you’d like at least some facility with Intimidate, Frightening Ambush isn’t the worst feat in the world.
Intimidating Prowess: Many avengers will likely be Strength-based, making Intimidating Prowess an easy (if not particularly exciting) boost to Intimidate checks.
Motivating Display: Morale bonuses are pretty common, but still, free bonuses for using Dazzling Display, which you would have been doing anyway.




Face Skills

---

# Non-Magical Healing

Non-Magical Healing


Healer’s Hands / Incredible Healer / Signature Skill (Heal): Healer’s Hands is absolutely vital for non-magical healing builds, but whether you go for Incredible Healer and early-game healing or Signature Skill (Heal) for late-game healing is more or less up to you. Don’t forget to max out Knowledge (Planes) if you pick these up, though!


Acupuncture Specialist: Non-magical remove curse. That could be handy.
Improvisational Healer: Almost nicer for the potion benefits than for the Heal benefits! Nice not to have to tote around healer’s kits for treat deadly wounds, too.
Noble Scion (Scion of Peace): Scion of Peace is noteworthy for vigilantes because of the skill familiarity social talent, which lets you select four skills that you can always take 10 on. The power move here is obviously to select all Wisdom-based skills (Perception, Sense Motive, Heal, and whatever free pick you feel is relevant) and then benefit from being able to “take 13” on those skills in addition to the bonuses you get when you take 13 out of combat.
Pathologist: Non-magical neutralize poison and remove disease. Again, could be quite handy.


Acupuncture Specialist: You may not have the Wisdom necessary to really capitalize on Heal checks, but skill familiarity and social grace can help. It’s nice to be able to cast remove curse without any spell slots.

---

# Stealth

Stealth


Dampen Presence: Dampen Presence is the rare feat that actually beats its comparable vigilante talent, blind spot. Where blind spot grants monsters huge bonuses to notice you, Dampen Presence simply beats blindsense and blindsight, no questions asked. You’ll still have to have negate aroma, fly, and other tools to beat other senses, but Dampen Presence beats the two hardest senses there are.
Shadow’s Shroud: Essentially the hide in plain sight class feature, but for limited rounds per day. You’d probably want to use it to proc hidden strike damage in combat, rather than for mundane scouting—the duration isn’t long enough for the latter.


Expert Sniper / Master Sniper: Sniping is a bit of a niche market, but ranged stalkers will most certainly be interested in attacking enemies without those enemies becoming aware of where they are. The fact that Master Sniper eventually allows you to eke two attacks out of a single Stealth check is icing on the cake. You’ll probably want to grab the sniping weapon enchantment (the one from Ranged Tactics Toolbox) to maximize the playstyle.
Moonlight Stalker: Some decent bonuses here, especially for stalker vigilantes.


Eldritch Assault: Teisatsu stalkers who’ve taken the vanishing trick ninja trick might be able to get some mileage out of Eldritch Assault, although you have to wonder whether you’re not better off just picking up Critical Focus.
Hellcat Stealth: If it were me, I think I’d wait until 8th level and pick up the hide in plain sight stalker vigilante talent, but Hellcat Stealth will do in a pinch, too.




Support


Combat Advice: Spend a move action, give someone a +2 competence bonus to their next attack roll. It’s not fabulous, but it’s an all-day source of buffing.


Battle Cry: Morale bonuses are fairly common, but the buffs against fear effects are particularly tasty.




Spellcasting

---

# Universal

Universal


Combat Casting: Vigilante archetypes that trade away vigilante talents in exchange for spellcasting can expect to spend a significant amount of time casting spells while being threatened by enemies, so Combat Casting is a must-have feat for cabalists, warlocks, zealots, magical children, ferocious hunters, etc.


Feign Curse: Feign Curse is a fun debuffing feat that can best be thought of as ill omen, but with no spell resistance, and keyed off a skill check, rather than a spell save DC. Vigilantes tend to have good Bluff scores and Charisma modifiers, so you could do far worse than Feign Curse if you want to get enemies to fumble all over themselves.
Spell Focus / Greater: Several vigilante archetypes receive spellcasting capabilities, and if they want to target enemies with those spells, they’d be well-advised to take Spell Focus.
Spell Penetration / Greater: As with Spell Focus, beating caster level checks to overcome spell resistance is a necessity for offensive casters.


Divine Interference: Only zealots will qualify for Divine Interference, but holy crap, what a feat. Force an enemy to reroll any successful attack at a penalty for the cost of a spell slot. Monsters critting your allies too often? Not anymore, they’re not!

---

# Vigilante-Specific Feats

Vigilante-Specific Feats


Convincing Persona: I don’t know how many vigilantes will have build space for Convincing Persona, but it’s a good feat that prevents your social and vigilante identities from merging in the public eye. Maybe talk to your GM about whether they would permit this as one of the intrigue feats available in place of a social talent, because I’d consider picking it up through that route in certain builds.


Notorious Vigilante: It’s a mild action economy boost for Dazzling Display builds, but otherwise isn’t super necessary.
Sinister Reputation: The biggest benefit to Sinister Reputation is the ability to demoralize enemies as a swift action, but because that feature only works on creatures with fewer HD than you have, it’s always going to be limited.
Startling Getaway: Startling Getaway assumes that you’re beginning each combat by attacking an enemy that’s unaware of your presence. Stalker vigilantes will manage that more than most, but it’s still a bit niche.


Agent of Fear: Unfortunately, Agent of Fear doesn’t work twice on the same enemies in the same combat, per the rules text, which means you still haven’t solved the primary problem of frightening appearance and stunning appearance. Womp womp.
Masked Symbol: Blasé bonuses to skill checks. You’re better off taking Skill Focus if you’re truly trying to become good at Bluff, Intimidate, Diplomacy, etc.
________________

---

# VIG 410: Archetypes

VIG 410: Archetypes


Whereas most classes have a base class and some number of archetypes, vigilantes are unique in that they already come loaded with a pseudo-archetype—that is, the distinction between avenger and stalker. Some archetypes will lock you into one specialization or another (teisatsu are always stalkers, e.g.), some give you the choice, and some replace your specialization entirely with other class features. It’s…a bit of a cognitive mess, if I’m honest. But that’s what this guide is here for! Let me gently guide your inflatable giraffe down the lazy river of vigilante options. As usual in my guides, I’ve split the archetypes into ones that generally strengthen the class (the wheat) and ones that generally weaken the class (the chaff). You’re welcome to go against the grain (no pun intended) if you like, but just know that your work building an effective combatant might be a smidge harder in the chaff archetypes.

---

________________
The Wheat

---

# Bellflower Harvester

Bellflower Harvester


The Basics


        Typically halfling vigilantes who focus heavily on the aid another action and teamwork tactics. Interesting as a support choice for a mostly combat-oriented class, and it segues directly into the bellflower tiller prestige class, if you’re interested in going that direction.


        Versatility: +0
        Power: +1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Rebellious Identity (Ex): Alters dual identity. I mean, this makes total sense for the archetype conceptually. Bellflower harvesters are, to all appearances, slaves, so the social identity matches that.


        (1) Bellflower Crop (Ex): Bellflower crop is absolutely necessary for the playstyle that this archetype encourages, but it has three major drawbacks: first, the action cost; second, the 30-foot range; and third, losing allies from your crop if they ever move outside of that range. GMs, please consider a little tactical homebrew on one of these three points. A swift action cost makes it bearable, as does a range that scales with vigilante level or the ability to “reinstate” allies into the crop if they/you move back in range. As it stands, though, it’s very hard to keep bellflower crop when combats can be mobile, far-ranging, and demanding on the action economy.


        (1) Crop Vigilance (Ex): Replaces vigilante specialization. Hoo boy! There’s a lot to go over with this one. Crop vigilance turns the bellflower harvester into a support-oriented melee class that employs the aid another action to great effect. The class feature slowly scales your bonuses up, so you need traits, feats, wondrous items, etc. that add bonuses directly to the top (rather than modifying the progression itself), give you a more streamlined action economy for using the aid another action, improve the duration of aid another bonuses, or increase the range of aiding another. Of note, feats and class features that improve the flat numerical bonuses of aid another don’t qualify under crop vigilance, but the rules text says nothing about other types of improvements (e.g., improvements to the action economy or duration of aid another), or improvements to the numerical bonuses that come from non-class feature, non-feat sources. There’s a summary below, and then a longform discussion after that.


      * Improving the Bonuses of Aid Another
      * Item: Benevolent weapons and armor
      * Item: Gloves of arcane striking
      * Feat: Got Your Back
      * Feat: Vanguard Style
      * Improving the Action Economy of Aid Another
      * Feat: Combat Reflexes + Bodyguard
      * Feat: Swift Aid
      * Feat: Team Up
      * Improving the Duration of Aid Another
      * Feat: Harrying Partners
      * Improving the Range of Aid Another
      * Feat: Magic Trick (Mage Hand) → Ranged Aid
      * Feat: Equipment Trick (Ladder) → Guarding Ladder
      * Feat: Cooperative Swarming
      * Wondrous Items
      * Benevolent Weapon and/or Armor: An absolute no-brainer for bellflower harvesters, benevolent weapons and benevolent armor add your enhancement bonus to whatever your granted aid another bonus is. The archetype caps out at +6 at 20th level, but if you have +5 benevolent leather armor or a +5 benevolent dagger, you can squeeze a +11 bonus out. So squeeze, pal.
      * Ring of Tactical Precision: An easy +1 to all aid another bonuses, along with some more teamwork feat shenanigans. And, uh…Profession (Soldier) bonuses? Sure. Sounds good.
      * Gloves of Arcane Striking: Ohoho, how I wish this combo worked. Bellflower harvester is tragically incompatible with other vigilante archetypes (cabalist, magical child, warlock) that grant an arcane caster level, but this would be a phenomenal pickup for a gestalt build. Again, all you’re doing is adding your arcane strike bonus right onto the top of what you’ve already got, so there are no conflicts between the bellflower harvester’s aid another bonus progression and this item.
      * Vigilante Talents
      * Team Player: Team player truly is all bellflower harvesters need from their vigilante talents. Gaining Swift Aid without the Combat Expertise requirement is peaches, of course, but the real power of team player is that you can aid every adjacent ally with one standard action. If you have three melee allies at your 11:00, 12:00, and 1:00, a single standard action will grant them all bonuses to hit. Add in Harrying Partners, and you’ll grant those bonuses for the entire round; add in Bodyguard, and you can use attacks of opportunity instead of any further actions for aiding AC. Truly, an incredible vigilante talent for this archetype.
      * Feats
      * Harrying Partners: Oh, good grief, what an amazing feat for this class. There are so many useful teamwork feats that involve the aid another action, but Harrying Partners is undoubtedly the best. The issue with aid another, beyond the relatively low bonuses and high action costs, is that your aid only persists for one triggering event. Aiding only improves one attack roll, not all of them on a full-attack; aiding only improves AC against one attack, rather than many. Harrying Partners is the solution. Now, whenever you aid another, the bonuses last until your next turn begins. All of your ally’s iterative attacks are buffed! One use of Bodyguard defends against an entire round’s worth of attacks! It’s hard to overstate what a big deal this is for the archetype. (Just remember that aid another is enemy-specific, so if you buff an ally’s attack rolls, the bonuses will only apply to the enemy you aided against. Ditto for AC.) You could ask your team to pick up Harrying Partners, but teamwork feats are usually tough sells, so using your tend the garden ability or a wand of shared training might be a better bet.
      * Combat Reflexes + Bodyguard: Combat Reflexes and Bodyguard are fairly effortless ways to ensure that your team rarely has to worry about AC. Grab a high Dexterity build, plant yourself (no pun intended) as close to as many allies as possible, and then just burn your attacks of opportunity from Combat Reflexes to blast those allies’ AC values into the lower stratosphere with benevolent armor, crop vigilance, and Harrying Partners.
      * Magic Trick (Mage Hand): We’re here in Magic Trick for the Ranged Aid option, which lets you use mage hand (probably from a hand of the mage) to aid another at a range of 30 feet. This is a huge gain! Aid another is typically restricted to what you can reach in melee range, which necessitates either a ratfolk + Cooperative Swarming build (so that you can share the space of your ally and wield a normal melee weapon) or a reach weapon build (so that you can stand behind an ally and poke at their legs). Now you can stand back with the spellcasters and provide Assistance From A Distance!™
      * Swift Aid: Swift Aid is an excellent feat, but it has to be considered in the context of the team player vigilante talent, which grants the feat for free and provides additional functionality for Swift Aid on top of what it normally does. With the team player talent, you’ll seldom find yourself actually using your swift actions to aid allies—team player’s standard action use case will buff the attack rolls of all of your adjacent melee teammates, and Combat Reflexes + Bodyguard is a much more effective means of buffing AC than Swift Aid. It’s not a bad idea to have it, since natural 1s do happen on aid another rolls, but most of what you need is provided by team player, not Swift Aid.
      * Equipment Trick (Ladder): Guarding Ladder is the trick we’re after here, which permits even Small-sized vigilantes to aid another from up to 10 feet away. I prefer Magic Trick (Mage Hand), but if you’re intent on not having a magical means of aiding another, this would be a good one.
      * Got Your Back: I would normally love Got Your Back, as it’s a quick way to turn Bodyguard into an anti-rogue/anti-slayer/anti-feint powerhouse. Unfortunately, it’s a teamwork feat, and tend the garden never scales you up past one teamwork feat—a teamwork feat that must go to Harrying Partners—shared with the team. The Combat Expertise requirement is also a bit onerous unless you’re willing to part with a vigilante talent for combat expertise. If you’re playing a gestalt build that grants shared training, tactician, etc., by all means, pick it up with all haste.
      * In Harm’s Way: In Harm’s Way is a bit more niche than the Combat Reflexes + Bodyguard wombo combo, since it requires you to take damage if your ally gets hit. Paired with a traditional non-magical healing build, however—that’s Healer’s Hands + Incredible Healer, for those following along at home—you can take hits from allies, then heal yourself for the damage. Think of it like a nonmagical oradin build!
      * Vanguard Style: I like Vanguard Style, but crop vigilance doesn’t scale the bonus to Reflex saves. The bonus will therefore remain small throughout your career.
      * Cooperative Swarming: This is a ratfolk-exclusive feat that would normally bump up your aid another bonuses, but the rules text of crop vigilance negates that.
      * Exceptional Aid: Modifies the bonus scaling.
      * Open Up: In addition to all the usual problems with bellflower harvesters sharing more than one teamwork feat, far too few enemies will ever carry physical shields for Open Up to be effective.
      * Team Up: Team Up is a good feat to have if you want to use aid another multiple times per round. Bellflower harvesters already have the team player vigilante talent, Harrying Partners, Combat Reflexes + Bodyguard, and more to enhance their efficiency, though. Skip the Combat Expertise tax. Skip Team Up.
      * Virtuous Creed (Protection): Modifies the bonus scaling.
      * Traits
      * Battlefield Disciple (Basic: Combat): Battlefield disciple grants a straight +1 to any aid another attempt used on an attack roll, and has no clause about adjacent allies. Great.
      * Azlanti Inheritor (Race: Gillman): Azlanti inheritor is a great trait, but you have to be a gillman to take it, and it only works for adjacent allies—Magic Trick (Mage Hand) won’t work.
      * Kin Guardian (Basic: Combat): A flat +2 to AC with aid another, but the catch is that you need to be buffing a member of your family. I’ve played family games of Pathfinder pretty successfully, but it’s not everyone’s deal.
      * Helpful (Basic: Combat) and Helpful (Race: Halfling): These are classic traits for halflings, but they modify the bonus scaling, and that’s a no-go.
      * Protective Faith (Religion: Torag): Modifies the bonus scaling.


        (1) Obsequious (Ex): Replaces 1st-level social talent. Obsequious could be a good social talent! Bluff is generally a much more useful skill than Disguise, so using Bluff in place of it when you need to shank someone in your social identity will help you get away scot-free more often. Seamless guise bonuses don’t apply when you’re making your vigilante identity conspicuous like that; if it comes down to a straight competition between Bluff with a bonus and Disguise with no bonus, I know which one I’m taking to the ball.


        (2) Tend the Garden (Ex): Replaces 2nd-level vigilante talent. I’m conflicted about tend the garden. On the one hand, it’s entirely necessary for the playstyle, since so many of the best aid another feats are teamwork feats; on the other hand, tend the garden costs a standard action, has a pretty abysmal duration, and never scales the action economy or number of teamwork feats you can share. I offer only two suggestions to lenient GMs: first, for the love of all that’s holy, make the duration 1 round per 2 levels. It’s ridiculous that a 20th-level vigilante could only share one teamwork feat for 24 seconds. Increasing the duration certainly doesn’t bust the power curve, it just allows the archetype to spend standard actions on other things such as—and this is just a spitball here—the aid another action. You know, as it was designed to do. Second, allow bellflower harvesters to sacrifice vigilante talents for improvements to the action economy (standard → move → swift → free) or for more teamwork feats shared (1 → 2 → 3 → 4). This puts the ability in line with the cavalier’s tactician ability, but the continued sacrifice of vigilante talents would ensure that the power level isn’t overtuned. With those two changes, I wouldn’t have any problem improving my rating of tend the garden.


Is It Worth It?


        Bellflower harvester has some serious limitations, notably the preponderance of standard action abilities, the tragically short range of bellflower crop, and the short duration of tend the garden. Is it better than other aid another builds? No, probably not. But at 17th level, team player, Swift Aid, a +5 benevolent weapon, and a ring of tactical precision would allow you to grant a +12 bonus to attack rolls for all adjacent allies against one enemy. Bodyguard, meanwhile, could grant that same +12 AC bonus to any ally as an attack of opportunity. Assuming that you take Harrying Partners and are regularly sharing it with allies (either through tend the crop, a wand of shared training, or some other means) then all of these bonuses will last an entire round. If given a little love by sympathetic game masters, I don’t see any reason it couldn’t be a great archetype, but you may be more frustrated by its potential than fulfilled by its implementation if you play it strictly as Paizo wrote it.
        Of note, the mechanics of the bellflower harvest archetype and the aid another action favor large, melee-oriented adventuring parties. Bellflower crop has no target cap; neither does tend the garden or the team player vigilante talent. The more melee allies you can cram in next to you, the more you’ll be able to pass out the monstrously large aid another bonuses that the bellflower harvester is capable of. If you have eidolons, animal companions, summoned creatures, cohorts, etc., start looking at this archetype much more seriously.




________________

---

# Cabalist

Cabalist


The Basics


        Dark, nefarious, half-magus/witch vigilantes who use blood, shadow, and necromancy to disable opponents.


        Versatility: +2
        Power: -1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Class Skills: Alters class skills. Despite losing more class skills than you gain, this is probably still a net positive for the archetype. Knowledge (Engineering) and Survival are mostly useless for the class, and Disable Device won’t be everyone’s jam. The only truly useful skill you lose is Perception, and that’s often easily reacquired through alternative racial traits like Fey Thoughts. Knowledge (Arcana) and Spellcraft, meanwhile, are always pretty useful.


        (1) Skill Ranks per Level: Alters skill ranks per level. Losing skill ranks doesn’t matter, because you’re Intelligence-based now, and Intelligence-based classes never have trouble with skill ranks.


        (1) Weapon and Armor Proficiencies: Alters armor proficiencies. Since the archetype strips away medium armor permanently—you can’t ever cast in it without arcane spell failure chance, even at later levels—cabalists will necessarily be high-Dexterity builds, likely taking the lethal grace vigilante talent at 2nd level to make ends meet.


        (1) Spellcasting: Replaces 4th-, 8th-, 10th-, 14th-, and 16th-level vigilante talents. The cabalist’s spellcasting class feature means that it will functionally only ever have three vigilante talents in the course of a normal campaign, which is…*checks notes*...yeah, not many. Lethal grace should almost certainly be your 2nd-level vigilante talent, but the rest are more or less up to you: cunning feint and perfect vulnerability are the most useful for proc’ing bleed damage from spill blood, but there are a number of cabalist-exclusive talents that will also be competing for your attention. Familiar or tattoo chamber are excellent picks at 6th level, and 12th could be shadow jump or any other dance partner who tickles your fancy.
        With regard to spellcasting…yeah, it’s great to have a spellcasting vigilante. Especially in conjunction with your bloodbound spell class feature, the cabalist can muster some nasty save DCs on big debuffs. Some cherries from the witch list:


      * 1st Level
      * Hermean Potential/Ill Omen: Probably your most valuable 1st-level spells, notable in both cases for the lack of a save. A lesser quicken metamagic rod + ill omen is a recipe for an enemy’s disaster, and the same rod + hermean potential is a wonderful buffing opportunity for a teammate.
      * Long Arm: Cabalists do best when enemies are bleeding, and your spill blood class feature or a wounding weapon is the quickest way to get that bleed damage flowing. The longer your reach, the more attacks of opportunity you have available for blood.
      * 2nd Level
      * Tears to Wine: A lovely spell that, while a bit more expensive at 2nd level on the witch list, is still a workhorse. A +10 bonus to all Intelligence- and Wisdom-based skills for 10 minutes/level is…really good.
      * Blood Transcription: Kill a caster, steal their spells. It’s evil-aligned, of course, but cabalists are in a great place to take advantage of this effect.
      * Mortal Terror: This spell has some interesting overlap with bleed damage, because as long as a target keeps taking even 1 point of damage, they keep having to save against fear conditions. An easy one to slap on a melee bruiser and stand back for the fireworks.
      * See Invisibility: Your spill blood class feature doesn’t work against creatures with total concealment. Don’t let enemies get away with being unseen.
      * Spectral Hand: Any bad touch build loves spectral hand, and cabalists are no exception. You’ve got bestow curse, touch of idiocy, mathematical curse, curse of befouled fortune, vampiric touch, fleshworm infestation, baphomet’s blessing, and more on your spell list. The fact that most of these also happen to be necromancy spells is icing on a very nice cake.
      * 3rd Level
      * Bestow Curse: You know it! You love it! Give it up foooooor bestow curse! The standby debuff that puts all other debuffs to shame…at least until greater bestow curse shows up. You’ve got a lot of save DC support from class features, too, which is lovely.
      * Accursed Glare: Similar to pugwampi’s grace, but technically a werebat-exclusive spell and saddled with an unfortunate 1-round casting time. It’s still phenomenal, but you might have to resort to pugwampi’s grace. About the only difference is duration (days/level vs. rounds/level), which won’t make much of a difference in a pitched battle anyway.
      * Pugwampi’s Grace: Think of pugwampi’s grace like ill omen’s older, hotter cousin who went off to college a few years ago. A Will save now negates the effect, but if you manage to stick the landing, every d20 roll for rounds/level becomes a roll-twice-take-the-worse jawn. A particularly nasty combo, in fact, is ill omen → pugwampi’s grace, where the former’s lack of a save opens an enemy up to a failed save against the far deadlier effects of the latter.
      * Siphon Might: Most cabalists will use lethal grace to attack in melee range, and that’s actually aces, because lethal grace forces you to use your Strength modifier for damage rolls. If you grab Weapon Finesse and then use siphon might to buff your damage numbers, you’ll be in really great shape. It’s a good spell, plain and simple.
      * Twilight Knife: What a lovely spell. It flanks with you, deals sneak attack force damage, and is impervious to nearly all forms of attack. Think of this like the best possible version of spiritual weapon.
      * 4th Level
      * Dimension Door: You know you’ll need it, so pick it up.
      * Enervation: One more tool in your debuffing toolkit. Pugwampi’s grace + enervation + siphon might = enemies having a bad time.
      * Masochistic Shadow: Masochistic shadow is basically a fast-moving death sentence for any target without rock-solid Will and Reflex saves, especially when paired with pugwampi’s grace or bestow curse. Slap this on a fighter and their Strength score will drain away so quickly they’ll begin buckling under the weight of their own armor.
      * Spite: A very solid witch spell that binds touch-range debuffs onto your skin for hours/level until they’re discharged by a melee aggressor. Bestow curse is the classic example, but others (see my rating for spectral hand) will also do excellent work.
      * 5th Level
      * Mind Fog: An absurd -10 penalty to Will saves makes enemies putty in your hands. My recommendation would be to throw this at a crowd of enemies, see which failed their saves, and then go for possession, dominate person, etc. for a fun time.
      * Possession: Want an easy way to completely take control of an enemy? Because possession is quite a bit better than magic jar.
      * Wracking Ray: Wracking ray deals an average of 12 points of Strength and Dexterity damage on a failed Fortitude save, and between your excellent buffs to necromancy save DCs and all the spells you have to force enemies to reroll saves, it should be a cinch to get that failed save.
      * 6th Level
      * True Seeing: Anyone who can get true seeing must get true seeing. That’s the rule. It counters blur, displacement, mirror image, deeper darkness, invisibility, greater invisibility, mislead, and all manner of illusion and polymorph spells—in short, some of the deadliest tools that high-level spellcasters will leverage against your party. Being able to see the enemy for what and where they really are can easily make the difference between a cakewalk of an encounter and a TPK. Don’t be the person who skips this spell.
      * Greater Heroism: Despite all appearances to the contrary, the cabalist is still an archetype that is expected to perform decently in melee combat in order to spread bleed damage. Greater heroism is one of the best vehicles for ensuring that you can do so.
      * Unwilling Shield: Taking half damage by any means (see, e.g., fire shield) is always a sweet proposition, and the fact that unwilling shield synergizes with your overall focus on necromancy is even sweeter. The duration is long enough for one combat, and barring the somewhat expensive material component, it has no other factors that would detract from its usefulness.


        (1) Spellbook: Overall, casting spells from a spellbook is more powerful than casting spells through a familiar. A book can be stolen, of course, but then again, a familiar can be killed, so on that front there’s no inherent advantage either way. No, the real advantage of a spellbook is that it’s far easier for cabalists, magi, wizards, etc. to learn new spells than it is for witches. While both witches and cabalists can—and should, as often as possible—learn spells through scrolls, witches can only learn spells from the larger witch list by having their familiar commune with the familiar of another witch (or by using more niche effects like blood transcription). This presupposes that you have access to a willing witch’s familiar, a condition that won’t be met in a large majority of cases. Cabalists and other spellbook users, on the other hand, can borrow a spellbook from a willing spellcaster or take spellbooks off of deceased or imprisoned enemy spellcasters; thanks to the larger proportion of wizards and magi in the world of Golarion, you’re likely to meet these conditions much more regularly.


        (1) Spill Blood (Ex): Replaces vigilante specialization. I feel like it’s a little disingenuous to say that spill blood replaces your vigilante specialization, since cabalist is essentially a specialization of its own. Either way, it’s a great ability for the archetype, if a bit finicky to use. First, just to check its power level, it’s one of the strongest sources of bleed damage I know of in the game, beating even Shax’s third-tier sentinel boon. Bleed damage is an odd beast: it works on fewer types of creatures than the precision damage of hidden strike, which is the closest thing we have to an opportunity cost for the class feature, but can be more powerful if the circumstances are right. Assuming you’re fighting just one enemy, hidden strike will always win, especially if you can land multiple blows per round for the full d8 damage. Bleed damage continues to tick even when you’re not attacking, however, and when stacked on multiple enemies can boast competitive damage per round. When you get right down to brass tacks, though, the cabalist is a spellcaster first and foremost, and it’s your 5th-level bloodbound spell class feature that makes spill blood worth your while. Any enemy who’s bleeding takes a -1 penalty to save against your spells—or a hefty -2 for necromancy spells. Combine that with the shaken condition from bloody horror, Spell Focus + Greater Spell Focus, effects like ill omen or pugwampi’s grace, and you’re well on your way to becoming an unstoppable debuffing machine.
        Now the bad part: it’s unreasonably difficult to proc your bleed damage. Unlike the stalker specialization, cabalists don’t deal bleed when they flank a foe, including with something like twilight knife. That’s bad. What’s worse is that witches don’t have invisibility or greater invisibility on their spell list! There are other routes to denying an enemy their Dexterity bonus to AC, of course, like feinting (with the cunning feint vigilante talent) or Shatter Defenses, but invisibility is by far the easiest. Beg, borrow, or steal that effect from your teammates. Since spill blood can’t always be relied upon to get an enemy bleeding, you’ll most likely want to invest in a wounding weapon. The effect is expensive at a +2 bonus, and the bleed damage from wounding and spill blood won’t stack, but wounding weapons do at least get the process started in order to trigger bloodbound spell, which you want up and running as much as humanly possible.


        (2) Cabalist Talents: Alters vigilante talents. While not every cabalist talent is a slam dunk, some are quite good! Let’s get into it.


      * (2) Familiar (Ex): This won’t come as a surprise to anyone. Familiars grant good bonuses, the Alertness feat, the ability to deliver touch spells for their masters—and that’s all without touching the flexibility of familiar archetypes or the Improved Familiar feat. If you have any spare room in your build, you want this talent. My recommendation is to make either familiar or tattoo chamber your 6th-level vigilante talent.
      * (10) Shadow Jump (Su): A short-range, tactically optimal dimension door. Mind the lighting restrictions, but otherwise enjoy teleporting around like Nightcrawler. Since cabalists trade away their 10th-level vigilante talents, you’d have to wait until 12th level to pick up shadow jump.
      * (2) Tattoo Chamber (Su): Tattoo chamber is a great talent whose primary purpose is to allow you to use spell trigger items without actually holding them. What counts as a spell trigger item? Wands, for one, and staves, for another. It’s unclear whether metamagic rods count, because while they’re use-activated items, not spell trigger items, they don’t need to be either donned or activated with a command word, only held in the hand while casting a spell. My recommendation would be to store predominantly wands in your tattoo early on, transitioning to staves at later levels and adding in a quicken metamagic rod or a persistent metamagic rod as your budget (and GM!) allows. In all cases, prioritize spells that you’ll want at your fingertips in combat without having to devote spell slots to them.
      * (12) Living Shadow (Sp): At 12th level, living shadow would earn a red rating because of its criminally short duration and your inability to cast spells or attack. (What is it useful for at this level? Scouting?) At 14th, it finally becomes functional, giving you some nice defensive and mobility options while also allowing you to continue casting spells. Pick up Eschew Materials if you foresee yourself using this power regularly.
      * (2) Bond of Blood (Sp, Su): Gaining a few temporary HP every hour isn’t what I’d call the pinnacle of effectiveness. Especially when you stack bond of blood up against other cabalist or vigilante talents, there’s simply no competition. Skip it.
      * (2) Necromantic Focus (Ex): A cleverly disguised trap. Feats are much less valuable to cabalists than vigilante talents are! Never forget that. With the exception of blindness/deafness and bestow curse (which you’d be picking up with spell slots anyway) none of the other spells are worth your investment, and Spell Focus is decidedly better as a normal feat pick.


        (5) Bloodbound Spell (Ex): Replaces startling appearance. Assuming you can get enemies bleeding, which, again, should be accomplished with a wounding weapon as a backup in case spill blood doesn’t work, bloodbound spell is an amazing class feature—the equivalent of Spell Focus (Everything) and Greater Spell Focus (Necromancy). Save DCs are a perennial problem for ⅔ spellcasters, but bloodbound spell is your ticket to the big time. Try never to target an enemy with a debuff (especially a necromancy debuff) unless they’re bleeding.


        (11) Bloody Horror (Ex): Replaces frightening appearance. Bleed damage and spellcasting are, like, the top two things that cabalists are best at, so sure! Intimidation tactics never hurt. Shatter Defenses is one of the better routes you can take to consistent damage from spill blood, and there’s a great argument to be made for Signature Skill (Intimidate) as your 11th-level feat so that you can start escalating fear conditions.


        (17) Shadowy Appearance (Sp): Replaces stunning appearance. At a lower level, shadowy appearance would easily earn a blue rating, but by 17th level, practically every enemy you face will have some means of detecting invisible creatures or seeing through concealment. Blindsight, tremorsense, true seeing, echolocation—there’s no shortage of ways that this ability could fail to work for you. If enemies aren’t packing those abilities, though, it’s an amazing defensive ability. The 1/day greater invisibility is particularly nice for spreading bleed damage using spill blood without having to resort to Shatter Defenses, blindness/deafness, feinting, etc.


Is It Worth It?


        There’s some debate over whether the cabalist or the warlock is the best spellcasting vigilante archetype, but I think both have lovely niches to fill. Cabalists are excellent at doling out ticking bleed damage, demoralizing enemies, and laying down hard-hitting debuffs, all without surrendering any of the versatility that social talents confer. The loss of vigilante talents hurts, of course, but you can’t get everything in a build without making sacrifices. I quite like it!




________________

---

# Chu Ye Enforcer

Chu Ye Enforcer


The Basics


        Predominantly evil vigilantes from the Tien nation of Chu Ye who draw mechanical and thematic inspiration from oni. Works best for dedicated natural attack/unarmed strike builds.


        Versatility: +1
        Power: +1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Yokai Heart (Su): What an interesting ability! I mean, alignment restrictions usually get an eye roll from me, but if you’re imitating oni (who are pretty bad customers, let’s be honest) then you probably deserve to be non-good. The more interesting thing is that you get low-light vision and 60 feet of darkvision while in your vigilante identity, which is a huge upgrade for races like humans and halflings. The clause about counting as a native outsider or a humanoid, whichever is worse for you, is likely to be more nuisance than true problem—you’d be locked out of buffs like enlarge person or reduced person that affect only humanoids, and susceptible to effects like dominate person or charm person even though you still count as a native outsider. It’s worth tracking potential interactions with team buffs or enemy debuffs, but I’m inclined to think you won’t notice it more than about a dozen times in a six-book Paizo AP.


        (1) Oni Mask (Ex): Alters dual identity. Another polarizing ability. Here’s the positive take: scaling bonuses to Intimidate are great, especially for an already Charisma-heavy archetype that gets to retain its vigilante specialization. The ability makes a strong case for a stalker specialization so that you can take advantage of the twisting fear vigilante talent, Shatter Defenses + hidden strike, and all the other Intimidate-based goodies available to rogue-like classes. The negative, on the other hand, is that binding your class features to an external object that can be destroyed is (as every wizard’s spellbook knows) an invitation for savage GM tricks that destroy your combat effectiveness. There are a few possibilities here, all of them bad:


      * Your oni mask gets sundered. Probably the least likely scenario, both because monsters don’t often sport Improved/Greater Sunder and also because the mask has a decent hardness rating and a good amount of hit points. At higher levels, though, a sustained assault by a sunder-specialized creature will destroy your mask in probably only one or two hits, and without it, you’re down a vigilante identity and a bunch of gold, in addition to having been doxed in front of your team. It’s likely that greater make whole is the only remedy in this case, since I would assume the caster level of the mask scales with your vigilante level. Make friends with a prepared caster who has access to that spell.
      * Your oni mask gets stolen. A sneaky, steal-the-mask-while-you-sleep approach is possible, and certain enemies might do just that. A more front-door approach would involve using the steal combat maneuver to snatch the mask right off your face—and I’m afraid it wouldn’t be that difficult. At best, a mask would count as fastened for the purposes of the steal rules, meaning it would be your enemy’s CMB check against your CMD + 5. At worst, it would count as loosely attached, which is CMB vs. CMD. You may want to explicitly stipulate that you’ve built your mask into some kind of helmet, etc., so that you can advance the argument that it’s closely worn, making it impossible to steal.
      * Your oni mask gets lost. Lost? How did you lose it!? Okay, you may deserve this one.


        (1) Steel Dictate (Ex): Replaces 1st-level social talent. In general, swapping social talents for combat abilities is a great idea—vigilantes frankly have more social talents than they truly need, and never enough combat efficacy. This is a carbon copy of the fist of the avenger vigilante talent, and it’s a phenomenal pickup for the archetype. If you become an avenger, you can always take fist of the avenger anyway, for a combined +10 to damage (yes, please); if you become a stalker, you can simply enjoy more static damage bonuses. Either way, it reduces the need for a scaled fist monk dip (although you may still wish to take one anyway for Charisma to AC, flurry of blows, and some bonus feats).


        (4) Deceitful Form (Sp): Replaces 4th-, 14th-, and 18th-level vigilante talents. Oh, this is a fun one. You might think that losing three vigilante talents isn’t great—and it’s not—but there’s more here than meets the eye, much like with oni themselves. First, at-will alter self is a great ability, both for deception/infiltration as well as for combat. Since most Chu Ye enforcers will go for a hybrid unarmed strike/natural attack build, you’re going to want to turn into a lizardfolk berserker, which grants a bite and two claws right away. Giant form I should prioritize troll forms: troll monarchs get a 19-20/x2 crit range on their claw attacks; troll berserkers get a 40-foot movement speed; troll furies get fire resistance 20, which helps keep that regeneration up and running; rock trolls lose their regeneration to sonic damage, rather than fire damage. All troll forms give you three natural attacks, rend, and regeneration, which is more than worth the price of admission. Giant form II opens things up a bit more: sun giants and moon giants have great energy resistances; ocean giants dominate aquatic combat; and mountain trolls remain the undisputed kings of offense. No matter what, I highly recommend you use my friend Zack’s resource Project Artemis, which has the best polymorph form search engine I’ve ever seen. I used it to write this section, in fact!
        Beyond the immediate applications of functionally unlimited polymorph spells, deceitful form also gives you the shapechanger subtype. This boon allows you to skip the malleable flesh vigilante talent, which is somewhat subpar, and go straight to seamless shapechanger and morphic weaponry. Seamless shapechanger adds your seamless guise bonuses to any Disguise checks you make to appear as the creature you’re impersonating with alter self or giant shape, which is huge for infiltration. It starts out as a +20 bonus, +10 from the polymorph effect itself, and then another potential +10 from favored class bonuses like the human’s, half-elf’s, or kitsune’s. Add a Charisma focus and a few ranks, and literally no one will know that you’re not actually a fire giant, undine, moss troll, etc. Morphic weaponry, unfortunately, grants you natural attacks only up to a maximum of four at 15th level, and they replace other natural attacks, rather than add to them. All of your polymorph forms already have three natural attacks; with your helmet gore, that’s four. Anyway, it’s quite feasible to have about eight attacks in this build (four iteratives made as kicks with flurry of blows + Improved Unarmed Strike + bite + two claws + deadly horns) so consider getting Multiattack to cut down on your secondary natural attack penalties.


        (6) Deadly Horns (Ex): Replaces 6th-level vigilante talent. Okay, this is a bad trade. Consider that morphic weaponry grants four primary natural attacks, and you’ll start to see why. I’m all for more natural attacks, especially when they stack with your existing tools, but still…


        (12) Third Eye (Su): Replaces 12th-level vigilante talent. I love a lot of things about this ability: that you can use it as a swift action, that it has a massive range, and that it targets touch AC. What I don’t love is that the damage is quite anemic for this level, is subject to energy resistance, and replaces a much-needed vigilante talent. Good for tagging runners, pausing regeneration or fast healing, squeezing out a little more DPR in critical rounds, etc., but overall, fire resistance is too prevalent for it to work as well as Paizo seems to want it to work. If I were to rewrite the ability, I would increase the damage (1d6 per 2 levels or even 1d6 per level) and dramatically lower its uses per day.


Is It Worth It?


        I wish that I could give up third eye and potentially deadly horns, but the overall chassis is very solid, especially at high levels. Although a Strength-based Chu Ye enforcer is a perfectly legitimate build, I think I’m going to pop a Dexterity-based stalker version of this archetype into the builds section, so head there if you’d like to see this playstyle in action more. 




________________

---

# Experimenter

Experimenter


The Basics


        Part-alchemist vigilantes who gain the ability to brew potions and mutagens, craft constructs, and more.


        Versatility: +2
        Power: -1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Class Skills: Alters class skills. Nothing but upside! Vigilantes only get two monster identification skills naturally, and with the ancestral enlightenment social talent, there’s a good incentive for investing at least one rank in each Knowledge skill. That only gets better with the lore master class feature coming up in a few levels.


        (1) Weapon and Armor Proficiency: Alters weapon and armor proficiencies. With no martial weapons, no medium armor, and no vigilante specialization, experimenters are naturally going to fall into high-Dexterity Weapon Finesse builds that don’t deal nearly as much damage as either stalkers or avengers.


        (1) Forbidden Science (Ex): Replaces vigilante specialization. Brute, darklantern, experimenter…man, vigilante archetypes sure have a thing for screwing you over with involuntary transformations, don’t they? The skill check bonuses are good, and I’m a big fan of mutagens, but it’s really hard to countenance an ability that forces you to become confused for a bunch of rounds, causes you to ignore “act normally” results, and randomly determines the attribute penalties your mutagen imposes. If you can get past that, mutagens are obviously amazing. Mutagens, cognatogens, fey mutagens, and rasugens are all worth their salt, depending on the build you’re trying to create. Just…pump your Will save as high as you can if you’re playing an experimenter, and guard specifically against fear effects, which are the most common save-based abilities that will trigger an unwanted transformation. Iron Will, Improved Iron Will, Eagle’s Resolve, and Unimpeachable Honor are decent places to start from a feat perspective.


        (1) Mutagenic Change (Ex): Replaces 1st-level social identity. Cool! You get quick change (a talent that’s compulsory for builds that conceal their social/vigilante identities) a few levels early, provided you have a mutagen on hand. As a reminder, you can brew those with 1 hour of effort.


        (3) Brew Potion: Replaces unshakable. Ohhh, what I wouldn’t give for alchemical allocation right now! With this class feature, you can grab potions at their very lowest caster level, then mass-produce them for your team at your caster level. Given your Craft (Alchemy) bonuses and the full investment you’ll be making in the skill (right? right?) it should be a cinch to create your own cottage industry here. And as a replacement for unshakable? Please and thank you.


        (5) Lore Master (Ex): Replaces startling appearance. I’d rather get bardic knowledge than lore master, but it’s undeniably lovely to be able to take 10 on all Knowledge checks—and take 20 a few times a day on truly critical checks.


        (11) Craft Construct: Replaces frightening appearance. Constructs may be monstrously expensive, but there’s no denying that having one on your team can amount to an entirely separate cohort. Just, uh…make friends with someone who can cast greater make whole.


        (17) Mutable Mutagen: Replaces stunning appearance. It’s a crying shame that you need to wait this long to turn your mutagen into an all-day affair—alchemists get their hours/level mutagens at 14th, after all—but I suppose that’s life. Anyway, this is a great upgrade. You don’t need me to tell you that. 


Is It Worth It?


        Absent a vigilante specialization and access to martial weapons/medium armor, experimenters may not hit as hard as normal vigilantes, even stalkers, but what they lack in raw stopping power, they more than make up in versatility, skill use, and team crafting. Mutagens can be turned into dang near anything you want, whether that’s a hulking ball of claws and teeth, a brainiac on par with Einstein, or anything in between. I’ll go ahead with the obligatory plug for levels as an evangelist of Haagenti. I mean, sure, he’s a demon lord, but good grief, an additional +2 to every attribute while your mutagen is active and unlimited oils of life are…good. Too good to ignore.




________________

---

# Faceless Enforcer

Faceless Enforcer


The Basics


        Hellknight vigilantes who specialize in the use of heavy armor and helms to conceal their identities.


        Versatility: -1
        Power: +1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Weapon and Armor Proficiencies: Alters weapon and armor proficiencies. Sure, free heavy armor proficiency. Is there anyone who wouldn’t appreciate this?


        (1) Dual Identity (Ex): Alters dual identity. Here’s the bad news: on its own, with normal armor, trying to keep your identities separate, this version of dual identity is abysmal. Like, truly abysmal. Are you really going to spend 4 minutes donning heavy armor with a friend’s help while they beat you senseless? No, no, a thousand times no. You’ve got two options: first, if you want to keep your identities separate, cheat! And by that, I mean find items, spells, and other effects that let you just skip right past the armor donning rules. Faceless enforcers may not ever be able to pick up the quick change or immediate change social talents, but with the right gear loadout, they can actually change identities as fast as a 13th-level vigilante with immediate change—right at 1st level! (Armored coats. The solution is always armored coats, my friends.) The second option is to ignore your vigilante identity altogether. Who cares if people know that you’re a faceless enforcer? They’re just going to have to deal with it.


      * Armiger’s Armor: Action cost: Standard action, 22500 gp. In my opinion, armiger’s armor is the best unique heavy armor you have available to you: it’s decently cheap, has DR 3/— and the light fortification enchantment baked in, gives the best armor bonus of any item on the list, and can be donned or removed with a single command word issued as a standard action. If you’re not enchanting an armored coat from scratch, this would be my vote for armor in the late books of a Paizo AP.
      * Armored Coat/Erutaki Coat: Action cost: Move action, 50 gp. Armored coats and Erutaki coats have the distinction of being the only mundane armors I know of that ignore armor donning rules. Make friends with these items, because you will be unable to don or remove other armors this swiftly for a very, very long time.
      * Clockwork Armor: Action cost: Full-round action, 33350 gp. Want to be Iron Man? Now you’re Iron Man. Built-in guns, bonuses to Strength and Constitution, and more! Suiting up is slower than with an armored coat, armiger’s armor, or flowing fortress armor, but you may decide that the attribute bonuses and ranged attack options are worth it.
      * Flowing Fortress Armor: Action cost: Move action, 30350 gp. Although flowing fortress armor clocks in as one of the most expensive items on this list, you can don and remove it as a move action from 10 feet away, and the melt and gush options are surprisingly powerful for exploration and dungeon crawls. If you’re looking for a specific set of enchantments not offered by this armor, though, the armored coat is still the superior option.
      * Wand of Serren’s Swift Girding: Action cost: Swift + standard action, 750 gp. While Serren’s swift girding is practically useless in the Pathfinder meta, it’s actually quite good for this archetype, especially in the mid levels when you may want to move away from an armored coat but can’t yet afford unique armors. Basically, you would carry around your armor and a wrist sheath loaded with a wand of this spell, then teleport your armor onto yourself once combat started. The DC 20 Use Magic Device check needed to activate a wand that’s not on your spell list is going to be a constant struggle here, so pump UMD in every way you can and invest in the skill familiarity social talent at 9th level like a good little Pathfinder player. You could also have an ally cast this spell, but that’s a waste of critical actions for a spellcaster at the beginning of combat.
      * Restful Armor: Action cost: none, 4500 gp. Restful armor solves the same problem that a wand of bed of iron does, but better. You only need to sleep 2 hours per night now, and can sleep in heavy armor without penalty. Again, good for nighttime ambushes, but not a solution for daytime encounters.
      * Wand of Bed of Iron: Action cost: Standard action, 750 gp. Bed of iron is a decent spell if you want to sleep in heavy armor, and credit it where credit is due, it will prevent you from having to don anything if you get ambushed in the middle of the night. It does nothing if you’re just in your social identity and get attacked, though, which is why it falls down the ratings quite a bit.
      * Raiment of Chains: Action cost: Move action, 6250 gp. The raiment of chains is a +1 chain shirt that can be donned or removed as a move action, plus some other junk. This gives you a slightly better maximum Dexterity bonus and movement speed than an armored coat, but the cost 125 times higher. You want my advice? Skip it and just upgrade the armored coat.
      * Wand of Guardian Armor: Action cost: Swift + immediate action, 750 gp. I can’t really think how this would work unless you had a very good friend who didn’t need armor and was willing to burn a spell slot to teleport their armor onto you. Like, a warpriest friend with a level in unchained monk: they probably prefer to wear no armor, but they can still clomp around in borrowed armor until initiative gets rolled, at which point they just zip it on over to you. It’s a very clunky solution, and probably not feasible in most circumstances.


        (2) Armored Juggernaut (Ex): Replaces 2nd-level vigilante talent. I was all set to trash armored juggernaut, but after rereading a few vigilante talents, this class feature is actually the armor skin and heavy training vigilante talents smooshed into the space of one vigilante talent. And that’s actually pretty good, especially considering how integral armor is to your overall kit! I wish it had been pushed back a bit to make room for other low-level vigilante talents, but that’s the way the cookie crumbles.


        (5) Faceless Infiltrator (Ex): Replaces 5th-, 11th-, and 17th-level social talents. Again, I was entirely prepared to mercilessly browbeat faceless infiltrator! Much to my surprise, however, it’s another solid ability that essentially grants you all three social talents from the many guises line (many guises, everyman, and any guise) with some helpful tweaks. Many guises specializes in generic, mundane identities that are utterly unremarkable but also lacking any social clout; you don’t gain the ability to assume a form particularly suited to infiltration until any guise comes along at 17th level. While faceless infiltrator doesn’t permit faceless enforcers to change identity nearly as often as many guises (once per month with this class feature, as opposed to once per use of many guises) what it does do is lend much-needed depth and variety to your infiltrations. Your identities, for example, are no longer restricted to common laborers, peasants, and farmers; now you can look like a noble, or a cultist, or a law enforcement officer, or a banker. You have papers—identification cards, visas, written orders from a superior, letters of introduction—to prove you are who you say you are; you get huge bonuses to Disguise and Bluff to maintain the ruse. Many Paizo APs see the PCs infiltrating a specific organization, but it’s rare that you would need to shift an identity rapidly between two organizations. So who cares whether you need to take a month recharge the ability?


        (6) Enforcer’s Wrath (Ex): Replaces 6th-level vigilante talent. I’ve tried to see the good in faceless enforcer, and I’ll admit that there’s more good in the archetype that I suspected at the outset. Enforcer’s wrath, however, is every bit as bad as you would expect. Surrendering a vigilante talent in exchange for a +4 bonus to Intimidate—in only a specific set of circumstances, mind you!—is the poorest of poor trades. Now, without access to many guises, everyman, any guise, quick change, and immediate change, you can probably guess that most of your remaining social talents will go toward the renown social talent line, which also grants Intimidate bonuses. Faceless enforcers should therefore invest in Enforcer, Cornugon Smash, Hurtful, Signature Skill: Intimidate, a cruel weapon, etc. to fully leverage demoralization in their builds. You’ve got to find some way to make hay here.


Is It Worth It?


        There aren’t too many ways to build a heavily armored stalker vigilante, but since faceless enforcer grants heavy armor proficiency, armor skin, and heavy training without surrendering your vigilante specialization, you can definitely make that concept work here. I like faceless infiltrator as an alternative to many guises, and the armor restrictions are actually a lot less onerous than I had anticipated. Remember: armored coats.




________________

---

# Ferocious Hunter

Ferocious Hunter


The Basics


        Exclusively half-orc vigilantes who gain extra proficiency with racial orc weapons. Quite strong, actually, without surrendering much.


        Versatility: +0
        Power: +1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Hidden Heritage (Ex): Alters dual identity. There’s something fun about appearing to be two different ancestries when other people see you, and it heightens the sense of “Bruce Wayne was Batman the whole time!?” It’s a mild mechanical bonus, though.


        (2) Symbol of Pride: Replaces 2nd-level vigilante talent. Sure! Signature weapon is a pretty good vigilante talent, and Strength-based ferocious hunters—you’ll almost have to be Strength-based, by the way, since none of the weapons listed work with Weapon Finesse—can always benefit from more attack and damage bonuses.
