If you play a stalker, it’s fun to be able to poach an avenger vigilante talent.


        (3) Spirit of Ferocity (Ex): Replaces unshakable. Orc ferocity is always a risky gamble, since enemies tend to keep attacking you for as long as you’re standing…and if you’re still standing at -15 HP, you could easily die. It’s better than unshakable, though, so we’ll give it a green. Just play dead if you need to.


        (8) Symbol of Mastery: Replaces 8th-level vigilante talent. So, maybe-not-so-hot take: I think ferocious hunters have plenty of good reasons to be stalkers, rather than avengers. Let’s think: who loves symbol of pride most? Not avengers, who can already select it normally, but stalkers! Who loves symbol of mastery most? Not avengers, who already have full BAB for all purposes, but stalkers, who would normally have to wait quite a while for Improved Critical, Critical Focus, and all the BAB-gated critical feats. Plus, Sneaking Critical and Critical Precision give some fun tools to stalkers that wouldn’t normally apply to precision damage. Definitely choose the falchion if you mean to lean into crit fishing; that x2/15-20 crit range is monstrous. If you don’t want to take maximum advantage of crit fishing, the orc hornbow and greataxe are probably your best bets for ranged and melee combat, respectively.
        Oh! And one last synergy here that I love: Lead By Example, which is a half-orc only feat that grants every ally within 30 feet a +2 morale bonus to attack rolls for 1 round whenever you confirm a critical hit. Think of it like mass heroism every time you do what this archetype incentivizes you to do anyway.


Is It Worth It?


        For crit-fishing half-orc stalker vigilantes, sure, it earns its keep. Grab Critical Focus, Critical Precision, Sneaking Critical, Staggering Critical, and Bleeding Critical, and you’ll be pretty much set for life against any enemy that’s not outright immune to crits.




________________

---

# Hangman

Hangman


The Basics


        Vigilantes who lasso enemies with nooses in order to grapple them. The archetype is a not-so-subtle homage to Wonder Woman and her lasso of truth, and synergizes nicely with whip builds.


        Versatility: -1
        Power: +1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Weapon and Armor Proficiencies: Alters weapon and armor proficiencies. Nets and whips, but no shields. Got it.


        (1) Vigilante Specialization: Alters vigilante specialization. This is a little unfortunate, as ¾-BAB classes will always have a much harder time of making combat maneuvers (lookin’ at you, grapple) stick than full-BAB classes will. You’ll get to do some tasty hidden strike damage if you manage to maintain your grapples, though, so we’ll look for other sources of bonuses.


        (2) Hangman’s Noose (Ex): Replaces 2nd-level vigilante talent. There are a lot of goodies baked into hangman’s noose, not all of them obvious. First, of course, you effectively gain Improved Grapple for free without needing Combat Expertise. Amazing for a feat-starved class! Second, a length of rope can essentially count as three things when wielded by a hangman: a rope, a net, or a whip. Any of these three types of items are therefore interchangeable as main weapons. Some of my favorite rope items are ropes of climbing, gum roap, iron rope, and bloodvine rope. (Iron rope is particularly funny if you hogtie someone, then issue the command word to turn the rope into iron. How are they getting out? I have no idea.) You can also weave nets and whips into nooses, but good unique nets and whips are harder to come by; I honestly can’t recommend the majority. Worthwhile enchantments for grapple-heavy net or whip builds are likewise slim pickings. Still, having some extra variety here in terms of which items you can reasonably loot from enemies and use is lovely. Third, counting a rope as a net and whip allows you to tick multiple boxes of the Equipment Trick feat all at once! Grappling Net is particularly useful for hangmen, grants a large +4 bonus on grapple checks, and is functional right out of the package. Hogtie and Slip Away are probably the standouts from Equipment Trick (Rope). Anyway, a fun vigilante talent that’s undoubtedly highly thematic.


        (3) Bound to Truth (Ex, Sp): Replaces unshakable. Ahhh, here’s where the Wonder Woman parallels start arising. I quite like this class feature—it’s relatively easy to take creatures alive when you’re grappling them, knocking them unconscious with Chokehold, etc., and you may well want to interrogate them later.


        (4) Twisted Rope (Su): Replaces 4th-level vigilante talent. Twisted rope feels more like a talent tax to prevent enemies from simply sundering your main grappling tool. The break DC scales quite well (DC 33 at 20th level compares favorably with the average CR 20 enemy’s Strength modifier of +12) but the noose’s hardness and HP will still leave it vulnerable to concerted sunder attempts.


        (5) Chokehold: Replaces startling appearance. Chokehold imparts a large enough penalty to your grapple check that you wouldn’t want to use it on anything but the softest targets. Spellcasters tend to be pretty soft targets, though, and they definitely care if you deny them access to their verbal components. Bushwhack is a necessary complement to this playstyle so that you can lasso an enemy from stealth, then immediately move to pin them (denying somatic and material components) or choke them (denying verbal components).


        (11) Tighten the Noose (Ex): Replaces frightening appearance. Tighten the noose is one of the better class features that the hangman has. An endgame action flow for a hangman might look something like the following: 


      * Surprise Round
      * Standard action: initiate grapple (the ambuscading grapple rogue talent is great here for a little extra oomph at the start of combat)
      * Free action: activate Bushwhack to move into a pin or Chokehold
      * Round 1
      * Standard action: maintain pin
      * Move action: deal damage with grapple and Greater Grapple, dealing reduced hidden strike damage because enemy is denied Dexterity to AC when pinned
      * Swift action: deal increased hidden strike damage through tighten the noose).


        You can see that there’s not a ton of room for damage in that lineup, although with an enemy pinned and choking, there’s also a good argument to be made that your contribution to the team is not damage but control of a single dangerous enemy while the rest of your allies handle business elsewhere. It’s lovely to get to deal your full hidden strike damage at least once per round.


        (17) Suffocation (Ex): Replaces stunning appearance. I like suffocation, but I’m not going to pretend that it’s much more efficient than simply dealing hidden strike damage every round. You have to establish a grapple, then spend three rounds choking an enemy to death—and that’s assuming that they fail every Fortitude save, which they might well not if you’re invested in Dexterity rather than Strength. Besides, constructs and undead don’t breathe, so they’re outright immune to the ability. It’s fun, but it has pretty obvious flaws.


Is It Worth It?


        Potentially? It’s certainly an interesting way to play a whip build. Stalkers (the baseline vigilante specialization that all hangmen have to take) will deal more damage once they have an enemy grappled, but their CMB scores are always going to be somewhat anemic compared with their full-BAB avenger brethren. Your mileage for a grappling whip build may vary.




________________

---

# Imperial Agent

Imperial Agent


The Basics


        Intrigue-heavy vigilantes who focus on extortion, blackmail, and false flag operations. Although it may not wow you at first blush, there are actually a huge number of interesting things you can do with it between manipulative and false flag—definitely an archetype that’s best in the hands of a devious, conniving player.


        Versatility: +2
        Power: +0


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Slander (Ex): Replaces 1st-level social talent. While I don’t think that slander is more effective than other common 1st-level social talents like renown, social grace, or owl’s sight, it does open up some interesting possibilities for urban campaigns, where you might be able to spend some downtime subtly turning the populace against a public figure. The DC is fairly beatable, too: with skill familiarity in Bluff, 10 ranks in Bluff, a +5 untyped bonus from manipulative, a +3 class skill bonus, and a positive Charisma modifier, you can easily beat the DC 20 + opposed Charisma modifier check for a public figure to which the populace is indifferent. Add even a little bit of investment (say, Skill Focus: Bluff or a wondrous item like the ring of eloquence or circlet of persuasion) and you can hit the DC 30 + Charisma modifier check for targets about whom the populace feels helpful. It’s worth investigating.


        (3) Manipulative (Ex): Replaces unshakable. Ahhh, who cares that manipulative doesn’t affect demoralization or feinting? The Intimidate and Bluff bonuses are great, particularly as replacements for unshakable. I’m fine with this.


        (5) False Flag (Ex): Replaces 5th-level social talent. Good grief, the possibilities! Disguise yourself as a member of law enforcement and extort/threaten the common folk or criminals, backed up by your bonuses from manipulative. Disguise yourself as a criminal and commit a particularly flagrant crime to bring the fuzz down on the real criminal organization. Disguise yourself as an ally from a powerful organization, assure another organization of “your” support when they commit some especially audacious act, then turn them in preemptively. Disguise yourself as a member of a noble house of a particular nation to lend credibility to a diplomatic endeavor. There are so many opportunities to use this ability to make a plan go right, and even more opportunities to use this ability to make a plan go dramatically (and deliberately!) wrong. I love it. Heck, it even acts as many guises, which I purchase at 5th level on nearly every vigilante I create anyway!


Is It Worth It?


        I think so! It’s the rare archetype that really improves your versatility without also making you much more powerful, but I think imperial agent manages it nicely. False flag will give you a vast array of ways to employ cunning rather than force in your adventuring, and manipulative gives you a bit more social power to back it up. Plus, imperial agent doesn’t give up many class features, allowing you to stack it with plenty of other archetypes, if that’s your jam.




________________

---

# Mounted Fury

Mounted Fury


The Basics


        As the name implies, mounted furies are vigilantes who ride animals into battle, gaining all the usual bonuses that mounted builds tend to have when charging.


        Versatility: -1
        Power: +1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Class Skills: Since your mount is treated as a druid’s animal companion, you’ll want Handle Animal in order to teach it new tricks, push it to stick it out in the face of unnatural threats, etc. Just a refresher on the Handle Animal rules.


        (1) Thorough Change: Alters dual identity. Thorough change isn’t necessarily good or bad—it just means that you’ll have to go with the Iron Man approach as a mounted fury. People will know that you work in a stable and that you’re the Lone Ranger, and that you don’t ever apologize or explain yourself for beating up bad guys. The alternative is staying in your vigilante identity more or less permanently, which locks you out of a lot of great social talents (e.g., social grace).


        (1) Mount (Ex): Replaces vigilante specialization. Mount is an amalgam of many different class features. You’ve got a druid-scaling animal companion; you take no armor check penalty on Ride checks, like the cavalier; you share teamwork feats (kind of) like a hunter; and your mount gets to use startling appearance, like…you, I guess? No one else gets to do that. Plus, you get to select avenger talents even though you’re not an avenger, and that ain’t bad, neither!
        Mechanically, it’s hard for me to know what to make of the teamwork feats that you can take in place of vigilante talents. Most teamwork feats exploit either adjacency or flanking, and therein lies the problem: you won’t want to use the mount as its own autonomous, flanking combatant, simply because so much of the archetype’s power—both defensive and offensive—comes from being mounted and using Spirited Charge, Wheeling Charge, mad rush, furious charge, and mighty charge to deal damage with a lance. And yet, if you stay on your mount—in defiance of God and men—you only count as a single entity for the purposes of teamwork feats. What, then, is the point of having teamwork feats when you still need to convince your human allies to purchase them in order to use them? I could see some amazing gestalt build ideas here with inquisitor, hunter, etc., but it won’t be very effective with a single-class vigilante.
        A final word about vigilante talents: if they give you static damage bonuses, You Want That.™ A lance gives you double damage (including static bonuses) on a mounted charge; the Spirited Charge feat gives you triple damage. Under these circumstances, vigilante talents like take ‘em alive and even signature weapon become much, much more valuable. Sure, Weapon Specialization is only a +2 to damage, but triple that, and you’re looking at a different picture. Of note, the mad rush avenger talent that unlocks at 12th level (14th is the earliest you can take it) doesn’t triple every lance attack with Spirited Charge, only the first. Legitimately disappointing, but there you have it. At any rate, any mounted fury build will be very dependent on charging, so consider anything that will let you charge over difficult terrain (Nimble Moves, Acrobatic Steps, air walk on your mount, etc.), around corners or other obstacles (Wheeling Charge), and through allies (also Wheeling Charge).


        (6) Furious Charge (Ex): Replaces 6th-level vigilante talent. A always, B be, C charging. Always be charging. Always be charging. Furious charge is already quite good for these purposes; when you pair it with mad rush, however, it gets way, way better. What’s mad rush’s primary weakness? The big AC penalty you take. And what does furious charge eliminate? The AC penalty from charging—any AC penalty, including mad rush’s. And what’s mad rush’s primary strength? Pasting a full-attack action onto the end of a charge. What does furious charge improve? The attack bonus you get while charging—an attack bonus that can be applied to any attack made after charging, including three lance attacks and four natural attacks from morphic weaponry. So, yeah. She good, fam.


        (12) Mighty Charge (Ex): Replaces 12th-level vigilante talent. I really, really wish Paizo could have moved mighty charge to either 10th or 14th level, because as it stands, it’s conflicting with mad rush at 12th level. And you know what? That really chaps my chops. If you can get past the staggering opportunity cost of the talent, it’s not bad: you get Improved Critical more or less for free, and I’ve already covered how devastating a Spirited Charge lance crit can be. The combat maneuver part isn’t likely to work with a ¾-BAB class that hasn’t specialized in it, especially not when both enemies and CMD scores are getting larger and larger. Since it doesn’t provoke, though, there’s not much risk to trying, so long as you don’t select a combat maneuver that you could fail by 10 or more (trip or disarm) and incur a boo-boo.


        (20) Vengeance Strike (Ex): Alters vengeance strike. Okay? I mean, you’ll want to be mounted most of the time anyway. Just stay mounted.


Is It Worth It?


        Assuming that you’re fine toting around a horse with hosteling armor and having far fewer social talent uses than most vigilantes, the mounted fury can eventually deal competitive damage with Spirited Charge and mad rush. More than a base avenger? That’s debatable. It’s entirely possible to play a base avenger vigilante, pick up Nature Soul + Animal Ally + Boon Companion, put a horsemaster’s saddle on it to share teamwork feats, and play as a mounted vigilante without needing to touch this archetype at all.




________________

---

# Psychometrist

Psychometrist


The Basics


        Psychometrists draw heavy mechanical and thematic inspiration from the occultist class, gaining focus powers and implements.


        Versatility: +1
        Power: +1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Class Skills: Alters class skills. Knowledge (Arcana) is probably better in most games than Knowledge (Dungeoneering), and History and Engineering are about equally pointless. Still, mostly just an even-stevens swap.


        (1) Occult Awareness: Replaces 1st-level social talent. Occult skill unlocks are fun, but I wouldn’t say that they’re better than permanent low-light vision (owl sight) or rerolls on failed Will saves (companion to the lonely), both of which are available instead at 1st level. Meh.


        (2) Implements and Focus Powers: Replaces 2nd-, 6th-, 12th-, and 18th-level vigilante talents. Gosh, if only there were someone who knew about occultists…wait, I do! Occultists are one of my favorite classes (I love all the occult classes, in fact, and have written guides on three of them already) so it bears examining how the psychometrist differs from that class:


      * First—and worst—the psychometrist doesn’t gain any spells or resonant powers from their implements. This is a big, bad deal. I wasn’t necessarily expecting spells (the occultist’s spellcasting is pretty limited anyway) but a huge chunk of the occultist’s power comes from being able to beef up their attributes with a transmutation implement, their saves with an abjuration implement, their animate dead HD caps with a necromancy implement, etc.—and that’s without delving into the greatness of panoplies. Take away the amazing passive buffs, and focus powers on their lonesome suddenly don’t look so appealing.
      * Second, your mental focus assignment is far more finicky than the base occultist. Base occultists assign one pool of mental focus to an implement school, and are then free to budget that mental focus to any focus power from the implement school however they see fit. Expecting to need mind eye from the divination implement school a bunch, but wound up blowing your whole budget on sudden insight? Not a problem. For psychometrists, however, it is a big problem, because they assign mental focus to specific focus powers. If you assigned 5 points of mental focus to mind eye, there’s no overlap—you use it on mind eye or you lose it entirely. 
      * Third, the psychometrist’s relationship with focus powers is a bit bizarre. Base occultists simply get focus powers at every odd level that they can select from among any of their implement schools; psychometrists get only the base focus power of their chosen school, plus one additional focus power from each previous school. This creates a strange endgame power surge, and incentivizes you to always select implement schools with the most potent focus powers first, so that you’ll get more bites at the apple as the levels roll by. Evocation and enchantment are essentially non-starters in the race, and Point #4 below eliminates conjuration. Necromancy starts strong but has little to offer beyond soulbound puppet and necromantic servant. Divination is hurt badly by the loss of resonant powers, but dang, mind eye still delivers, and sudden insight and danger sight are perennial favorites. Illusion is kinda in the same boat—you really want distortion as a resonant power, but unseen, mirage, masquerade, and shadow beast all have their niche. That leaves abjuration and transmutation as the old standbys! You know ‘em. You love ‘em. Aegis, energy shield, and unraveling are the standouts from abjuration; quickness, mind over gravity, and telekinetic mastery are the best from transmutation. Legacy weapon, as always, rocks the house. Check out my occultist guide for recommendations on which enchantments to snag with both aegis and legacy weapon.
      * Fourth, you can’t take a conjuration implement at all. I have no idea why this might be. Servitor, flesh mend, psychic fog, and side step are all great focus powers, but they’re not wildly overpowered, relative to other implement schools. What gives, Paizo?


        Now, even with all that red and yellow in mind, gaining some of an occultist’s implements is still exceptionally powerful. Divination, illusion, abjuration, and transmutation will give you haste, fly, telekinesis, invisibility, dispel magic, legacy weapon, aegis, mind eye, etc., all several times per day. If you wanted to play a pseudo-caster without surrendering too many of your vigilante talents, there are far worse ways to do it.


        (2) Mental Focus (Su): Another subpar knockoff of the occultist. Psychometerists get only ½ level + Intelligence modifier, whereas occultists get their full level + Intelligence modifier. Granted, psychometrists don’t need mental focus to activate resonant powers, but we’re still talking about a full ten points of mental focus missed over the course of your career. That’s a hefty chunk of change! This class feature, by the by, more or less forces you to place all your mental attribute investment in Intelligence, rather than Charisma, in order to maximize your implement save DCs and mental focus pool. It’s the smart thing to do—grab some traits like Pragmatic Activator, Student of Philosophy, Bruising Intellect, etc. if you feel like you’re really hurting for Charisma-based skills.


(2) Object Reading (Su): Replaces unshakable. Unshakable is hot garbage, to use a technical term, so emerging the victor in a head-to-head class feature contest is laughably easy. Nevertheless, object reading is a tremendously useful class even when no comparison is drawn. Mysterious objects of unknown provenance are common in Pathfinder, whether weapons, armor, mementos or keepsakes, etc., and are often one of the only clues left as to a creature’s origin and backstory after the bloodthirsty PCs have summarily slaughtered them. Now you can learn that information yourself by communing with the object directly! It’s a great feature on the occultist, where I try never to give it away if I can help it.


(20) Psychometric Strike (Su): Alters vengeance strike. I suppose psychometric strike is strictly better than vengeance strike—it’s nothing but upside, even if you’re unlikely to run into enemies who have yoinked your belongings that often. Very few people play at 20th level, anyway.


Is It Worth It?


        It depends on how you weigh the main tradeoff psychometrist is asking you to make: four vigilante talents in exchange for 10 focus powers and about 17 points of mental focus by 20th level. I certainly wish the psychometrist’s version of implements and mental focus weren’t so neutered, but I’m guardedly optimistic that the versatility and strength of focus powers would ultimately outweigh the lost vigilante talents. Just be smart about which focus powers you take, when you take them, and how you apportion your mental focus. It matters even more for psychometrists than it does for occultists.




________________

---

# Serial Killer

Serial Killer


The Basics


        Well, the name couldn’t be more obvious: serial killers butcher their enemies, plain and simple, gaining features from the slayer class, as well as the assassin prestige class. Obviously evil-aligned.


        Versatility: -1
        Power: +2


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Alignment: Uhhh…yeah, I think an evil alignment makes sense. Although one could argue that most PC adventuring parties are also in the business of murdering for fun, at least they (mostly) don’t harm innocents.


        (1) Vigilante Specialization: Alters vigilante specialization. Fun fact: I’ve heard from deep, dark corners of the Paizo forums that, early in the playtests for the vigilante class, hidden strike counted as sneak attack for everyone, not just for serial killers. I’m actually still in favor of that ruling—hidden strike has nearly no feat or item support, while sneak attack has tons. Anyway, this is pretty great. Flensing Strike alone makes this version of vigilante specialization worth your time.


        (3) Thwart Pursuit (Ex): Replaces unshakable. Both unshakable and thwart pursuit give bonuses to truly irrelevant DCs and checks. Both should leave you with a bad taste in your mouth, so…no harm done?


        (4) Studied Target (Ex): Replaces 4th- and 14th-level vigilante talents and 9th- and 19th-level social talents. Uhhh, since when did we start handing out insane combat bonuses in exchange for social talents, Paizo!? Studied target, even the slightly weakened version you get here, is hugely powerful, and cuts your vigilante talent budget in the low-level build space—where things are always tightest—by only one talent. Studied target improves the DCs of your class abilities, too, which might supercharge abilities such as mighty ambush. Don’t forget that you can apply studied target as an immediate action anytime you deal your hidden strike damage!


        (5) Charming (Ex): Replaces startling appearance. RAW, I’m pretty sure this ability is broken, and not in a good way. The duration of the witch’s charm hex is a number of rounds equal to their Intelligence modifier, yet no provision is made in the ability text for the vigilante’s Charisma modifier. If you can convince your GM to give you this hex with both the save DC and the duration keyed off of Charisma, it can be an excellent ability, helping you make friends in unlikely places, charm your way past guards, or buy a temporary reprieve from combat in order to parlay. Heck, it even works in antimagic fields! As is, however, the duration is broken.


        (6) Death Attack (Ex): Replaces 6th-level vigilante talent. This ability receives a yellow rating because not every enemy will be susceptible to death attacks or paralysis, and because enemy Fortitude saves tend to be exceptionally high in the endgame. There's no guarantee that even after three rounds of study you'll make them fail that save; if you do fail, you've wasted those three rounds, and the enemy is alerted to your presence. It's far easier instead to simply purchase one of the hidden strike vigilante talents such as throat jab or mighty ambush and use those in place of death attack. More or less identical effects, but less fuss.


        (7) Calling Card (Ex): Replaces 7th-level social talent. As bizarre as it seems, calling card is actually quite a good class feature. The defining weakness of the renown social talent line is that it takes an unconscionably long time to establish renown in new areas. Once you can Joker someone to become known and feared, however, it’s a walk in the park (albeit a gruesome walk) to renown’s mechanical bonuses.


        (11) Grisly Murder (Ex): Replaces frightening appearance. Dreadful Carnage comes functional right out of the box, which is lucky because stalkers wouldn’t be taking Power Attack and Furious Focus most of the time. You’ll almost certainly want Signature Skill: Intimidate so that one merciless kill shot will send enemies into a blind panic. Anyway, better than frightening appearance, for my money.


        (12) Quiet Death (Ex): Replaces 12th-level vigilante talent. Even though you take a -5 penalty to your Stealth check when using the silent dispatch vigilante talent, I’d still rather have that than quiet death. Why? Quiet death only functions when you make a death attack, whereas silent dispatch functions regardless of whether you use death attack or simply rely on studied target and hidden strike to put an enemy into the ground. The versatility of silent dispatch outweighs the slight increase in power from quiet death, easily.


        (17) Swift Death (Ex): Replaces stunning appearance. Finally, at 17th level, you can kill people without having to stare at them for 18 seconds! What a time to be alive.


Is It Worth It?


        Throw away the death attack stuff, and I’d say you had a winner—certainly, counting hidden strike as sneak attack and tossing studied target onto the stalker vigilante chassis can only do good things for the class. It’s not that the archetype loses thematic focus after that point, but it does feel like it loses mechanical focus. The biggest barrier to a PC playing one of these is that it’s a laughably evil archetype; as a terrifying NPC antagonist, though? *Mwah.* Perfection.




________________

---

# Splintersoul

Splintersoul


The Basics


        Vigilantes who have wildly divergent social and vigilante identities.


        Versatility: -1
        Power: +1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Splintered Identity (Ex): Alters dual identity. Splintered identity seems uniquely tailored to multiclassed vigilantes who are dipping into classes with more stringent alignment restrictions. While that’s not necessarily a bad market to try to corner, I know many GMs who dispense with alignment restrictions altogether. Does it make sense that a monk has to be lawful, or that a barbarian has to be chaotic? Paizo itself produced three different versions of the champion class in 2e to accommodate different flavors of “warrior for righteousness.” If you’re just playing a single-class vigilante with no alignment restrictions, splintered identity is nothing but downside—truly. It’s not a huge downside, since many vigilantes will go to great lengths not to reveal their vigilante talents while in their social identity anyway, but still, less flexibility, not more.


        (3) Sudden Change (Ex): Replaces unshakable and alters 3rd- and 7th-level social talents. You mean I get two of the most purchased, most essential social talents for keeping identities separate several levels before other vigilantes? Heck yeah, I want that! This ameliorates many of the problems with splintered identity, since it doesn’t take nearly as long to transition from a social identity that can’t use vigilante talents without being ID’ed to a vigilante identity that can.


        (7) Surprising Change (Ex): Alters startling appearance, frightening appearance, and stunning appearance. Surprising change is the mechanical equivalent of “Call an ambulance—but not for me,” in that your transition from mild-mannered, dog-loving retiree John Wick into slaughter-happy, killed-ten-men-with-a-****ing-pencil Baba Yaga is so sudden that enemies get unnerved by it. It caters to a more social style of play, rather than a dungeon crawl style, since you wouldn’t just wander around in your social identity in the middle of an obviously dangerous locale. The penultimate line should probably read something like, “A splintersoul can use frightening appearance and stunning appearance against foes who are unaware of his presence as normal, but can only do so a number of times per day equal to his Charisma modifier.” I found the version presented on Nethys needlessly difficult to parse. Anyway, I’m going to rate this ability a yellow, but it’s more a sidegrade than a downgrade.


Is It Worth It?


        I’m as surprised as anyone to say that I actually kinda like this archetype, since I always had it in the back of my mind that it was hot garbage. Grabbing quick change and immediate change so many levels earlier than other vigilantes is a recipe for a hit, and neither splintered identity nor sudden change derail gameplay as much as you might fear. Of course, there’s always an opportunity cost to archetypes in that you can’t select another (potentially better) archetype that modifies the same class features, so take with a grain of salt.




________________

---

# Teisatsu

Teisatsu


The Basics


        Teisatsu draw tons of inspiration from the ninja class. The versatility of having a ki pool and access to ninja tricks/ki powers, combined with the relatively few class features that you lose, should make you consider teisatsu as the “base archetype” for the stalker vigilante specialization unless you truly, truly need that 2nd-level vigilante talent.


        Versatility: +2
        Power: +2


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Weapon and Armor Proficiency: Alters weapon and armor proficiencies. Lots of great exotic weapons on tap here. The wakizashi and katana are easily the best offered, especially for crit-fishing.


        (1) Infiltrator (Ex): Alters vigilante specialization. Yes, let’s all just try to imagine an avenger ninja for a second, huh? This decision makes all too much sense. You’re a sneaky snake, so act like it!


        (2) Ki Pool (Su): Replaces 2nd-level vigilante talent. Lethal grace would normally occupy a stalker’s 2nd-level vigilante talent slot, but honestly, it’s not a big deal to just take Weapon Finesse at 1st level, take lethal grace at 4th level, and retrain the feat for free. On its own, a ki pool is already pretty good, most notably for the extra attack you can make at full BAB as a swift action. If you have an enemy dead to rights and lined up for a full-attack with hidden strike, there’s no reason you shouldn’t invest a point of ki to make the attack extra lethal. Where the ability truly shines, however, is in ki powers and ninja tricks, all of which give the teisatsu a huge amount of versatility and build flexibility. Get ready for the full scoop below the fold, and remember that the Extra Ki feat exists! You may want some more fuel in the tank for these abilities.


        (2) Teisatsu Talents: Wow. Just wow. Teisatsu is such an enormous power jump for stalker vigilantes, it’s not even funny. It’s like Paizo said, “Hey, I have this crazy idea knocking around my head. What if we gave an already strong class the best features from two other strong classes?” and everyone else in the room pulled out guitars and started doing sick metal riffs. I won’t bother to review every option available to teisatsu, but we do need to cover a few essentials. Keep in mind that because every ki power and ninja trick needs to be taken in place of a vigilante talent, we can only be satisfied with the absolute cream of the crop. Anything else will be trading down.


      * Ki Powers
      * (8) Freedom of Movement (Su): Freedom of movement slays, plain and simple. Solid fog? You’re gone. Black tentacles? Gone. Insane CMB checks to grapple you? Gone. There’s a reason every prepared caster totes this spell around at all times. There’s a reason you should have it, too.
      * (8) Abundant Step (Su): An ultimate get-out-of-jail-free card, dimension door for 2 ki points seems eminently reasonable. Great for infiltration, exfiltration, and general ninja hijinks.
      * (8) Insightful Wisdom (Su): This wouldn’t be my first choice of ki power, both because of its cost and the existence of higher priorities, but it’s a nice little buffing effect that can save an ally from death on failed saves.


      * Ninja Tricks
      * (1) Forgotten Trick (Ex): Consider forgotten trick the martial flexibility of the ninja class. For the price of 2 ki, you can select any ninja trick (not master tricks, unfortunately) and behave as though you knew it for multiple rounds. True, it’s costly, but the versatility you get in exchange is peerless.
      * (1) Redirect Force (Ex): I first came into contact with redirect force when writing my Spheres of Might guide, and honestly, it’s a hilarious ninja trick. True, you have to take damage to use it well, but if you do, there’s almost no enemy that can prevent themselves from being tripped, dirty tricked, bull rushed 25 feet over a cliff, etc. Great for combat maneuver builds—no enemy will ever see it coming. And best of all, it’s free to use!
      * (1) Shadow Clone (Su): Mirror image, one of the better defensive spells in the game, for 1 ki point. Need we say more?
      * (1) Vanishing Trick (Su): I truly wish the invisible blade master trick were available to teisatsu, but I suppose we’ll have to make do with vanishing trick. Look: stalkers need hidden strike, and while you can sometimes use dirty tricks to blind an enemy, Intimidate and Shatter Defenses to make them flat-footed, Gang Up to flank more effectively, etc., invisibility is a time-honored tactic that works against a solid tranche of Bestiary enemies, at least in early to mid levels. You Want This.™
      * (1) Flurry of Stars (Ex): Shuriken builds can be super fun for teisatsu. Why? Well, a majority of your damage isn’t going to come from your weapons anyway, so you can just dump a huge number of attacks into the meat grinder and proc hidden strike damage like crazy. With enough bonuses, you can nail an enemy with obscene damage before they even figure out what’s going on.
      * (1) Pressure Points* (Su): It’s reasonable to assume that teisatsu are going to be attacking enemies who are unaware of their presence quite often. Why not deal ability damage at the same time? There’s no save to do so, and the Heal checks enemies would need to pass in order to remove the damage are quite cumbersome in the middle of a fight. It’s a good way to soften enemies up for other allies, either on offense (Strength, usually) or defense (Dexterity, usually). And if you can get a big, lumbering enemy down to 0 Dexterity or a small, weak enemy down to 0 Strength, the effect is the same—they’re paralyzed.


Is It Worth It?


        Teisatsu is the pinnacle of a worthwhile archetype: like primalist bloodragers, you give up virtually nothing and gain oodles of powerful options from the unchained monk and ninja. Don’t worry too much about the lost 2nd-level vigilante talent—I assure you, the trade is worth your while.




________________

---

# Warlock

Warlock


The Basics


        Like the cabalist archetype, the warlock archetype is also indebted to the magus class. Where cabalists focus on blood and shadow magic, though, warlocks shoot beams of elemental energy at foes as their primary mode of attack.


        Versatility: +2
        Power: -2


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Class Skills: Alters class skills. I’m going to call this mostly a win. Knowledge (Arcana) and Spellcraft are both great skills, so you’ll just have to work out a way (the fey thoughts racial trait that many races have access to is one such) to grab Perception again.


        (1) Skill Ranks Per Level: Alters skill ranks per level. A straight downgrade, but then again, warlocks are Intelligence-based, so you’ll probably end up with more skill ranks overall than base vigilantes.


        (1) Armor Proficiencies: Alters armor proficiencies. Yeah, so there’s not a great way to build a Strength-based warlock. Weapon Finesse and ranged combat with mystic bolts make far more sense for the archetype, anyway.


        (1) Spellcasting: Replaces 4th-, 8th-, 10th-, 14th-, and 16th-level vigilante talents. Man, you really hate to see that many vigilante talents fly out the window, huh? With only three available to you before the end of most six-book Paizo APs, you’re going to have to be very, very discriminating about which talents you pick. The good news is, you cast spells like a wizard now—at least up to 6th level—and wizards have the best spell list in the game. I’ll refer you over to my Spell Codex resource if you want to spot some generally good picks on the list.


        (1) Mystic Bolts (Su): Replaces vigilante specialization. Mystic bolts gets a lot of grief from the community. It has plenty of weaknesses—susceptibility to energy immunities, no possibility of adding enhancement bonuses through weapon enchantments or greater magic weapon, no feat support, no Deadly Aim (doesn’t apply to touch attacks)—but it’s perhaps not quite as bad as it’s cracked up to be. Deliquescent gloves or demonsmith gloves could be combined via magic item rules or tattoos to give an extra 2d6 energy damage; a rogue or vivisectionist alchemist dip would give you sneak attack that could then be bulked up via Accomplished Sneak Attacker; sense vitals is on the wizard spell list for another source of precision damage; the arcane striker vigilante talent can squeeze some more damage out. It’s still a long haul, but targeting touch AC many times per round is workable unless you run up against a wall of energy resistances.
        GMs, if you’re looking for a quick and dirty way to modify mystic bolts so that it’s better, you might consider allowing players to select one simple blast wild talent from the kineticist class—no burn, no gather power, no elemental overflow—with Intelligence replacing Constitution as the damage modifier. Whenever players would be granted a new damage type (7th, 13th, and 19th levels) they can instead select a new simple blast and a single 1-burn form or substance infusion that they can apply to their blasts for free. You still get the “souped-up elemental blast cantrip” feel of mystic bolts, but it’s actually good: maximum damage at 20th level will be around 10d6+17, which will work better against DR and energy resistance than the nickel-and-dime damage that base mystic bolts deal. You can experiment with the empower metakinesis at high levels if you feel like the modified bolts are falling further behind in power than you’d like. I’ve got a guide on the kineticist class, Going Nova, that gives a thorough rundown of what all that jargon means, if you feel like implementing the changes.


        (2) Warlock Talents: Alters vigilante talents. Many golden oldies will be familiar from the cabalist list, so I won’t bother generating new text for those abilities. 


      * (2) Familiar (Ex): This won’t come as a surprise to anyone. Familiars grant good bonuses, the Alertness feat, the ability to deliver touch spells for their masters—and that’s all without touching the flexibility of familiar archetypes or the Improved Familiar feat. If you have any spare room in your build, you want this talent. My recommendation is to make either familiar or tattoo chamber your 6th-level vigilante talent.
      * (2) Tattoo Chamber (Su): Tattoo chamber is a great talent whose primary purpose is to allow you to use spell trigger items without actually holding them. What counts as a spell trigger item? Wands, for one, and staves, for another. It’s unclear whether metamagic rods count, because while they’re use-activated items, not spell trigger items, they don’t need to be either donned or activated with a command word, only held in the hand while casting a spell. My recommendation would be to store predominantly wands in your tattoo early on, transitioning to staves at later levels and adding in a quicken metamagic rod or a persistent metamagic rod as your budget (and GM!) allows. In all cases, prioritize spells that you’ll want at your fingertips in combat without having to devote spell slots to them.
      * (2) Arcane Striker (Su): I hate that this talent is so integral to warlocks, because it’s a tacit admission that mystic bolts can’t entirely hold their own in combat. More damage is obviously good, but truly, you should just modify mystic bolts using my kineticist suggestion or third-party content from Legendary Games. Remember that the gloves of arcane striking are a bonkers-good wondrous item for these builds!
      * (2) Elemental Armor (Su): Resist energy is so much more versatile and powerful that I can’t see much of a use case for elemental armor. Immunity at 16th level might be intriguing if you were in a game like Reign of Winter, but honestly, the campaign’s about done by that point, and you’d have to wait until 18th level to pick up the talent anyway, since spellcasting trades away your 16th-level vigilante talent.
      * (8) Social Simulacrum (Sp): I’ve seen simulacra used intelligently, but since they’re not under your control, there’s at least as good a chance of things going dramatically wrong when you use them as there is of things going dramatically right. Especially with your tight talent budget, I just don’t buy it.


        (3) Piercing Bolts (Ex): Replaces unshakable. Targeting touch AC is great, don’t get me wrong, but this feels like a class feature tax for something that should have been available from 1st level. If you go with my modified version of mystic bolts, you can just eliminate piercing bolts altogether, as the delineation between physical and energy blasts will determine whether it targets touch AC or not.


Is It Worth It?


        Even if spellcasting, tattoo chamber, and familiars create an incredibly versatile ⅔ caster, the cabalist can do all of those things without the power of manufactured weapons. With my changes to mystic bolts, I’d leave my versatility rating at +2 but change my power rating to +0, leading to a green rating overall. The archetype could be so cool, but it just needs a little help over the finish line. C’mon, GMs. You know you want to dance with the occult classes.




________________

---

# Wildsoul

Wildsoul


The Basics


        Even more so than avenging beasts, wildsouls are in tune with the natural world, emulating aspects and abilities of one particular type of animal. This archetype is really four archetypes in one, so it’s best to read carefully.


        Versatility: Varies
        Power: Varies


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Natural Course (Ex): Alters vigilante specialization and replaces 2nd-, 6th-, 12th-, and 16th-level vigilante talents. So, the first thing to get out of the way with the wildsoul is that they’re still stalkers or avengers: no matter which natural course you pick, you can still be either a full-BAB mauler or a ¾-BAB hidden strike user. Either way, it’s worth thinking about whether your animal form will grant you vigilante talents from one specialization or another, as it may predispose you to take the opposite specialization in order to broaden your horizons.

---

# Arachnid

Arachnid


        (2) Heightened Senses (Ex): Uncanny dodge and improved uncanny dodge got a green rating in the vigilante talent section, so that feels fair here. Could this be a not-so-subtle homage to a certain friendly neighborhood Spidey Sense?


        (6) Shoot Webs (Ex): Away, web! Tanglefoot bags are surprisingly effective alchemical items, but what’s no surprise is that they get better with scaling save DCs. I’m frankly not sure why any part of this ability is tied to Constitution, as it doesn’t seem to match the vigilante’s general design ethos, but we’ll roll with the punches here. Remember that Huge and larger creatures aren’t affected by tanglefoot bags at all!


        (12) Web Specialist (Ex): A climb speed is fun, but nothing you couldn’t have gotten elsewhere. As for your web swinging action, well, I hate (I hate) that they cost a use of shoot webs. For my money, you could package the 12th- and 18th-level arachnid abilities in with the 6th-level ability and I wouldn’t even blink. I mean, fly is a 3rd-level spell. Do we really have to pump the brakes on an ability that allows you to “fly” at your base speed at 18th level? That just seems needlessly cruel. Yeah. We’re gonna third-party this jawn. Hang on.


        (18) Web Master (Ex): So you wanted to be Spiderman, did you? Well, Paizo went and spit on your childhood dreams, because you’ll only get this ability at the very end of your Pathfinder career. Let’s check out my rewritten arachnid specialization for something a little more spicy, shall we?

---

# Arachnid (Allerseelen’s Rewrite)

Arachnid (Allerseelen’s Rewrite)


        (2) Heightened Senses (Ex): At 2nd level, an arachnid wildsoul can anticipate physical danger and react astonishingly quickly. He gains the stalker sense vigilante talent, even if he is not a stalker.


                No changes here. It’s a good ability.


(6) Shoot Webs (Ex): At 6th level, an arachnid wildsoul’s wrists can produce thick, sticky webbing that he can shoot at his enemies. Shooting webbing is a ranged touch attack with a range increment of 10 feet. The webbing acts as a tanglefoot bag when it hits a creature, except the DC of the Reflex save to avoid being glued to the floor or unable to fly is equal to 10 + 1/2 the arachnid wildsoul’s vigilante level + his Constitution modifier. Like a tanglefoot bag, the webbing does not work underwater. The arachnid wildsoul can shoot webs a number of times per day equal to 5 + Constitution modifier.
Additionally, at 6th level, an arachnid wildsoul can coat his hands and feet in super-sticky webbing, even over equipment such as gloves and boots. This gives him a climb speed of 30 feet. As a move action that provokes attacks of opportunity, an arachnid wildsoul can use his webbing to create the equivalent of a 50-foot length of silk rope (hp 4, break DC 24) and can shoot it at any flat, unoccupied surface that is at least one 5-foot square in size. If the arachnid wildsoul succeeds at a ranged attack against AC 10, the rope sticks fast to the surface, can only be removed if it is destroyed, and can support the weight of the arachnid wildsoul plus one additional Medium or smaller creature. Alternatively, he can create numerous web-ropes as he is moving and swing from rope to rope, essentially allowing him to move at his base speed through the air as long as he always uses at least one hand to hold the webbing and continues to have tether points within 50 feet and above him. The web ropes he creates in this way dissolve as soon as he is done using them to swing.


        My rewrite essentially combines shoot webs, web specialist, and web master into one ability, and adds a few more uses per day to shoot webs. It’s strong, even for a vigilante talent, but I don’t think it’s anywhere close to broken. Aerokineticists, occultists, and arcane casters of nearly any variety can fly as often as they want at faster speeds and without any restrictions on having solid surfaces within 50 feet of them, so it doesn’t feel crazy to offer limited “flight” to this archetype. I bumped up the base uses per day of shoot webs, too, in light of the fact that not everyone will have a great Constitution modifier.


        (12) Web Sling (Ex): At 12th level, an arachnid wildsoul has become adept at using his webs to launch objects at foes. Treat this ability as an aetherkineticist’s telekinetic blast wild talent, except the arachnid wildsoul must have an unimpeded line of sight to the object being thrown. At 16th level, the range of the arachnid wildsoul’s web sling increases from 30 feet to 120 feet.


        This is another iconic Spiderman move, right? Grab a manhole cover, fling it at a bad guy. As strong as it may sound to give another class a kineticist’s simple blast, it’s actually not so bad. There aren’t any infusions, metakinesis, composite blasts, or elemental overflow to worry about, so even 20th-level arachnid wildsouls will only be able to deal 10d6 + 10 + Constitution modifier damage per hit. That’s, what, about 50 damage? It’s fun flavor, but CR 20 enemies have about 370 HP on average, so 50 damage isn’t gonna break the bank. You can consider giving it the empower metakinesis at 16th level, if you want average damage to jump to around 70.


        (16) Moment of Glory (Ex): At 16th level, an arachnid wildsoul is capable of inhuman feats of strength or speed when forced into desperate action. Once per day, he may treat his Strength or Dexterity score as 20 points higher for the purposes of one Reflex save, Strength-based skill or ability check, or Dexterity-based skill or ability check (including initiative rolls). The arachnid wildsoul is treated as possessing evasion and improved evasion, as the rogue class features, if he uses moment of glory to modify a Reflex save. If he has fewer than 20% of his maximum hit points and the GM agrees that the action is suitably legendary in scale, he may use this ability without expending his daily use of moment of glory, and may do so even if the daily use has already been expended. Hit point damage taken from allied, friendly, or helpful creatures does not qualify the arachnid wildsoul for this ability’s hit point threshold.


        Spiderman is defined not only by his physical powers, but by the mental grit, heroism, and will to persevere that he possesses. I felt like that aspect of him deserved some recognition! We all know the moments when he’s beaten, battered, broken, and somehow musters the strength to stop a moving train car, lift a mountain of steel, dodge a flying glider, or catch a hapless bystander as they fall to their death. If you want to crush an initiative check that your Spidey Sense warned you about, completely dodge an ancient dragon’s breath weapon, single handedly prevent a cave collapse, break down a door that stands between you and the love of your life, this is your ability. And the more beaten you are, the more able you are to pull these spectacular moments out of your cowl! The last line felt a little forced to me, but I wanted to make sure players didn’t simply beat down on their ally, force them into a bunch of manual labor, and then heal them back up with Signature Skill: Heal, heal, or other HP-restoring abilities. Because that’s not heroic, ya know?

---

# Falconine

Falconine


(2) Soft Landing (Ex): Yeah…perfect fall is a pretty bad vigilante talent, and it doesn’t make any sense to me that a bird would need a wall within arm’s reach to fall like this. I’ve rewritten the ability below to be closer to the tengu’s glide racial trait.


(6) Eagle Eye (Ex): Competence bonuses are widely replicated (see, e.g., the eyes of the eagle wondrous item) and, moreover, eagle eye only applies to visual Perception checks. I prefer the version presented by the cleric Feather subdomain, which grants a racial bonus to all Perception checks. Again, rewritten below.


(12) Take to the Air (Ex): Finally, at 12th level, the bird takes to the skies. Why did it take this long to get a 40-foot fly speed when casters have been zooming around with a 60-foot fly speed for 7 levels?


(18) Deadly Dive (Ex): Credit where it’s due: deadly dive is a fun and interesting ability. Assuming you have the vertical space to use it (not a guarantee!) you’ll typically use a withdraw action to pull 80 or so feet up in the air, hitting that DC 20 Fly check to do your vertical move, then dive on your next turn for an additional 16d6 damage on your attack. Whether this is worth it is a separate question: you’re essentially sacrificing two rounds of full-attack actions for as much damage as your dive allows, and the math simply doesn’t work out in favor of that option, especially for full-BAB avengers, and especially especially because enemies get to take attacks of opportunity against you as you move and attack. Probably only useful when you’re up against high DR or an enemy has such high AC that you’re unlikely to hit with any iterative attacks.

---

# Falconine (Allerseelen’s Rewrite)

Falconine (Allerseelen’s Rewrite)


        (2) Aquiline Instinct (Ex): At 2nd level, a falconine wildsoul has begun to emulate the grace and perceptual acuity of raptors. He is constantly under the effects of glide while in his vigilante identity; treat this as an extraordinary effect. Additionally, he gains a racial bonus on Perception checks equal to 1/2 his vigilante level (minimum +1) and a +2 racial bonus on initiative checks in any surprise round in which he can act.


        Perfect fall on its own is a poor excuse for an archetype ability, so I modified the original soft landing class feature and combined it with the cleric’s Feather domain ability eye of the hawk—the Perception bonuses are better, both in terms of breadth and bonus type, and a little initiative bonus doesn’t matter so much. Glide is mostly a quality of life upgrade, and mimics the tengu racial trait in terms of power.


        (6) Take to the Air (Ex): At 6th level, a falconine wildsoul can harness the flight inherent in his blood. While in his vigilante identity, he has feathery wings that grant him a fly speed of 40 feet (with good maneuverability).


        You’re turning into a literal bird. It shouldn’t take until 12th level to be able to fly. Enough said.


        (12) Aerial Supremacy (Ex): At 12th level, a falconine wildsoul has honed his skill in the air to an incredible degree. His fly speed increases to 60 feet (with perfect maneuverability) and he gains the Hover and Powerful Wings feats, ignoring their prerequisites.


        Again, a literal bird should be at least as good at flying as a creature using fly. Hover and Powerful Wings are fun little abilities for kicking up clouds of dust, staying in place without a Fly check, and qualifying for feats like Buffeting Wings or Snatch and Drop. This is another good ability, but nothing that full casters haven’t been doing for a bunch of levels by now.


        (18) Deadly Dive (Ex): At 18th level, a falconine wildsoul has learned how to mimic the swooping and diving of birds of prey. As a full-round action while he is flying at least 30 feet above the ground, a falconine wildsoul can dive onto a creature by moving up to twice his fly speed and making a full-attack action. The movement provokes attacks of opportunity as normal, and the attack provokes an attack of opportunity from the attack’s target. On the first successful attack of the full-attack action, the falconine wildsoul deals the attack’s damage plus 2d6 points of damage for every 10 feet he dove (to a maximum of 30d6 damage if he dove 150 feet or more). Even if each attack misses, the target still takes the extra damage for the distance of the dive, but it receives a Reflex save to halve the damage (DC = 10 + 1/2 the falconine wildsoul’s vigilante level + his Strength or Dexterity modifier, whichever is higher). A falconine vigilante can dive in this way a number of times per day equal to his Strength or Dexterity modifier, whichever is higher.


        Falconine wildsouls now get to make a full-attack action at the end of their dive, and the height cap is increased to 150 feet to accommodate the improved fly speed from aerial supremacy and haste. All these changes do is help make one round of ascension followed by one round of a dive more comparable to two rounds of full-attack actions. Depending on attack and damage bonuses, two rounds of full-attack actions may still come out ahead, so I don’t think this is too strong.

---

# Ursine

Ursine


        (2) Deadly Claws (Ex): It’s hard to get excited about any of the ursine wildsoul’s three natural attacks when morphic weaponry, available for the cost of two vigilante talents with seamless shapechanger to boot, would grant four. Heck, these natural attacks don’t even do much damage! Bite attacks are usually 1d6, at least.


        (6) Bear Jaws (Ex): Yeah, ditto. These just needed to be stronger.


        (12) Thick Hide (Ex): A whole +1 to natural armor? Golly jee, mister, you shouldn’t have! Seriously, though, this is a pathetic talent when compared with practically any other wildsoul’s 12th-level talent. Felines get mad rush, falconines get a permanent fly speed, and even arachnids can start web swinging at this level. You get…+1 AC? Nah.


        (18) Bear Form (Ex): Let’s see…bear form will get you a +4 bonus to Strength, a -2 penalty to Dexterity, a +4 natural armor bonus, low-light vision, scent, two claws, a bite, and grab. But you lose the ability to speak, use manufactured weaponry, and do basically anything else. You do realize that by the time you get beast shape II, full casters will have been casting beast shape IV for, like, seven levels, don’t you? This is just a bad deal.

---

# Ursine (Allerseelen’s Rewrite)

Ursine (Allerseelen’s Rewrite)


(2) Apex Predator (Ex): At 2nd level, an ursine wildsoul gains two claw attacks and a bite attack while in his vigilante identity. These are primary natural attacks. The bite attack deals 1d6 damage if he is Medium or 1d4 damage if he is Small; the claw attacks deal 1d4 points of damage if he is Medium or 1d3 if he is Small. Additionally, he gains a natural armor bonus of +1. This natural armor bonus increases by 1 at 6th level and every four levels thereafter, to a maximum of +5 at 18th level.


I’m sorry, but the ursine wildsoul just needed more oomph. This frontloads many of the necessary combat bonuses in the archetype, but isn’t fundamentally dissimilar to something like a ratfolk getting Sharpclaw and Sharptooth by 3rd level for three natural attacks.


        (6) Big Game (Ex): At 6th level, an ursine wildsoul has become incredibly difficult to kill. He gains the Endurance, Diehard, and Toughness feats, ignoring their prerequisites. Additionally, he may ignore the BAB and Constitution requirements of any feat that lists Endurance or Diehard as a prerequisite, and counts as an orc with Ironhide for the purposes of qualifying for the Tenacious Survivor, Deathless Initiate, Deathless Master, and Deathless Zealot feats.


“Three feats!” I hear you say. “Isn’t that a bit overboard?” Well…no. These aren’t fantastic feats. Endurance is generally quite poor, and Diehard can be dangerous to use without a very clear idea of what you’re doing. Only Toughness is unambiguously good. This is mostly here for the flavor of being an unstoppable bear, and opens the way for some more animal ferocity-esque feats like Heroic Recovery, Fast Healer, Tenacious Survivor, Stalwart, Survivor, and the Deathless line. 


(12) Bear Form (Su): At 12th level, an ursine wildsoul has learned how to take a bear’s form. He can change into bear form as a standard action, at will, and the transformation lasts until he changes back. While transformed, he becomes either a grizzly bear or a polar bear, as beast shape II. An ursine wildsoul loses his ability to speak while in bear form and is limited to the sounds that a normal bear can make, though he can communicate normally with other bears, as speak with animal. At 16th level, this ability grants +8 Strength, -4 Dexterity, +2 Constitution, +6 natural armor, and the rake (2 claws) and rend (2 claws) abilities, dealing damage as appropriate for the wildsoul’s Strength modifier and size. At 20th level, this ability grants +12 Strength, -4 Dexterity, +6 Constitution, +8 natural armor, and DR 10/—.


If you want to incentivize bear form and can’t afford to grant magical beast shapes, it’s got to come with better attribute bonuses. Essentially, this rewrite packages Huge- and then Gargantuan-sized attribute bonuses into your existing Large-sized bear form, plus adds a little more meat onto your grapples (through rake) and full-attack routine (through rend). See, now there’s actually a reason to abandon your vigilante talents and run around mauling people, Revenant style. I also handed out a polar bear form, because they’re objectively way scarier than grizzly bears. “If it’s black, fight back; if it’s brown, lie down; if it’s white, say good night.”


        (18) Ursine Paragon (Ex): At 18th level, an ursine wildsoul has become one with the bear’s ecosystem. He gains a combined favored terrain bonus of +12, as the ranger class feature, that he may apportion as he sees fit between plains, cold, and forest terrains. No terrain may exceed a +8 bonus. (For example, he may choose a +8 bonus in cold terrains and a +2 in both forest and plains, a +6 bonus in forest, a +4 in cold, and a +2 in plains, or a +4 in all terrains.) Additionally, the ursine wildsoul gains the corresponding terrain mastery and terrain dominance benefits of the horizon walker prestige class in each terrain to which he apportions a bonus of at least +4.


For an 18th-level ability, ursine paragon is frankly still a bit tame. In very specific campaigns like Reign of Winter, of course, that cold terrain ability is going to slap, but then again, rangers always slap when terrain and enemy conditions are in their favor. The horizon walker bonuses are mostly fun fluff: cold resistance 20, +10 to base move speed, a +4 competence bonus on Stealth checks—none of this stuff is going to break the bank when full casters are laying down wish and mass heal every other round.

---

# Feline

Feline


        (2) Feline Attributes (Ex): Low-light vision, scent, and Perception bonuses are all well and good, but probably the premiere ability here is the fulfillment of prerequisites for Aspect of the Beast. You’ll probably want to take the claws option—I don’t think the others are particularly worthwhile.


        (6) Feline Grace (Ex): I’ve rated the stalker sense vigilante talent a green, and that’s what I’ll do here. Feline wildsouls have huge incentives to be stalkers anyway, so you’re just taking a talent you probably would have taken anyway. Uncanny dodge and improved uncanny dodge are good class features to have.


        (12) Feline Pounce (Ex): This is why you’d choose to play a feline wildsoul, and more specifically why you’d choose to play one on a stalker chassis. Typically, rogues and rogue-like classes have a lot of trouble setting up damage: you can feint, Shatter Defenses, blind enemies with dirty tricks, Gang Up, have greater invisibility cast on you, etc., but ultimately your damage per round still depends on being within 5 feet of an enemy at the start of your turn so you can step and full-attack. Mad rush solves that. Sure, you take a big penalty to AC, but if you’ve got greater invisibility, haste, a weapon, and secondary claw attacks or morphic weaponry + Multiattack, then charge an enemy for a full-attack action, you might conceivably pull off five to seven, each dealing full hidden strike damage, before a creature has a chance to respond. Few will be able to withstand that kind of onslaught, especially if you open with it against a flat-footed or unaware enemy. Avenger feline wildsouls could simply purchase mad rush normally, so there’s no real incentive here.


        (18) Nine Lives (Su): If you’re being brought below 0 HP more than eight times per day by the time you take a character to 18th level, I frankly don’t know what to do for you. Git gud, I guess. Defensive roll is a neat rogue talent, but by its nature, it’s not going to work every time. Even with a +12 base Reflex save, a +5 cloak of resistance, +1 from haste, and a +7 Dexterity bonus, you could soak only 45 points of damage on a natural 20, and only 26 points on a natural 1. CR 18-20 enemies tend to hit on the harder end of that spectrum, for sure, and will often blow right past the outer limits of “Is this save even possible?”

---

# Feline (Allerseelen’s Rewrite)

Feline (Allerseelen’s Rewrite)


        I think there’s probably a more elegant way to balance the feline wildsoul than three middling abilities sandwiched around “POUNCE CAT GO BRRRRRR,” but I don’t much feel like writing it. Feline pounce is strong enough that the entire natural course rockets up the charts. Let it go, man.


Is It Worth It?


        The natural courses stratify themselves nicely into Feline > Falconine > Arachnid > Ursine. My rewrites bring them all into alignment with one another, somewhere in the high green, low blue zone. It’s a fun archetype, and I’d hate for it to be relegated to the trash heap, but as it stands, only feline and falconine are really worth it.
________________
The Chaff

---

# Agathiel

Agathiel


The Basics


        Vigilantes who transform themselves wholly or partly into animals as their vigilante identities. An interesting choice for natural attack builds, but it sacrifices a bit too much power for my tastes.


        Versatility: -1
        Power: -1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Immortal Commitment (Su): Although it makes complete sense that agathiels must be aligned with the standard neutral good of Nirvana and other agathions, it does stink that the archetype has more or less the same alignment restrictions as a cleric. Keep a scroll of atonement handy in case you get mind-controlled, unwillingly outfitted with a helm of opposite alignment, etc.


        (1) Bestial Identity (Su): Alters dual identity and replaces 4th-, 8th-, 12th-, and 16th-level vigilante talents. Ayayay. What a mish-mash of mechanics, eh? I’m going to drop some links, first to the beast shape suite of spells, and second to the polymorph subschool rules. Those will be necessary as we go on. Okay. Let’s dive in, blow by blow. The first stop along Bestial Identity Avenue is just being able to imitate an animal. You’re not an animal, although your seamless guise bonuses (+20) combined with the -10 penalty associated with this ability make it relatively easy for you to pass as an animal—a total +10 to your Disguise check. While in this shape, you won’t have any natural attacks or special abilities, so you’re pretty weak to start off, although you can still wield weapons at this point.
        By the time 4th level rolls around, you have access to a permanent version of beast shape I, with the exception that you get no ability adjustments and can pick only one of the special abilities mentioned by beast shape I. The former is less of a big deal than the latter; polymorph spells are powerful precisely because you can often pick up multiple special abilities, and the restrictions to this and later iterations of bestial identity do not inspire confidence. The upgrades at 8th, 12th, and 16th levels hand you more abilities from beast shape (pounce, poison, rend, and tremorsense/blindsense are the best available) but don’t change your form at all—you’re not going to be turning into a Warcat of Rull or other Large- or Huge-sized creature, as with beast shape II/III/IV, but rather just tacking on abilities to your base Small- or Medium-sized form. The final wrinkle with bestial identity is that your equipment does not meld with your body, as stated in the polymorph rules text! This is a net positive for the archetype, as it allows you to wear armor normally without having to worry about tanking your AC values into the basement. Overall, though, I think that bestial identity is a swing and a miss for agathiels. Vigilante talents are so powerful that you need an extremely compelling reason to give them up, and a few natural attacks and animal special abilities don’t truly compensate you for their loss. There aren’t a huge number of valuable forms in beast shape I anyway.


        (2) Agathion Blessing (Su): Replaces 2nd-level vigilante talents. Aspect of the Beast is a decent enough feat, I suppose, but the opportunity cost is again incredibly high. Claws of the beast is the only truly worthwhile option; don’t even bother with the others.


Is It Worth It?


        On balance, probably not unless you have a very specific character concept in mind. Beast shape I simply doesn’t pack a powerful enough punch to keep your combat efficacy on par with the base vigilante, and the special abilities you get are rationed out too stingily to compensate for the loss of most of your vigilante talents.




________________

---

# Anaphexia Thought-Killer

Anaphexia Thought-Killer


The Basics


        Norgorber-flavored vigilantes who cut out their own tongues in exchange for the ability to read minds and conceal their thoughts from others. Not a great archetype, as cool as it sounds.


        Versatility: -2
        Power: -2


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Tongue Sacrifice (Su): Tongue sacrifice has to be one of the most metal openings to any archetype I’ve ever read, but man, is it not worth it. Your social identity has to be a Pharasmin priest or priestess, and you cannot speak or cast spells with verbal components at all until you cast regenerate…which you only receive a whopping 1/day. What if you need your social identity to talk, and you’ve already regrown your one tongue for the day? And you deal damage to yourself, too. Ayayay. Probably the only upshot to tongue sacrifice is that it appears (RAW) to pave the way for a standard action transition from your social to vigilante identity, but that’s not much of a comfort when the quick change social talent can do much the same thing with a lot less hassle and hemorrhage.


        (2) Monastic Communication (Ex): Replaces 2nd-level vigilante talent. Monastic communication begins a long tradition in this archetype of mislabeling social and vigilante talents—someone at Paizo clearly got confused and didn’t read the class in depth before writing it. Monastic communication actually replaces a vigilante talent, and no, there’s no way in Phlegethon’s infernal foundries that getting bonuses to Bluff to pass messages through pantomime is an adequate replacement for a vigilante talent. This is as bad a trade as they come.


        (6) Silent to Magic (Su): Replaces 6th-level vigilante talent. Ugh. No, no, no! Bad archetype. Bad. Nondetection is already a little shrimpier than I’d like, but it earns its bones with an hours/level duration and fairly wide coverage over elementary divination magic. Make it only apply to mind-reading effects, and only apply to your social identity until 10th level, and it’s way, way undertuned. And for a vigilante talent? No. No, no, a thousand times no.


        (7) Thought-Scent (Su): Replaces 7th-level social talent. Credit where it’s due: thought-scent is a wicked cool ability, thematically speaking. Lack of credit where it’s also due: smelling whether someone knows a fact that you already know is incredibly niche, and certainly doesn’t portend well for 7th level, when any sane vigilante would be picking up quick change. I’ll grant it a yellow for pure rule of cool, but that’s it.


        (15) False Reading (Su): Replaces 15th-level social talent. Most opposed divination effects feature a save, and low-level magic like detect thoughts or detect anxieties should be a cinch for the Will-strong vigilante to plow through. Frankly, most Norgorberite PCs in this archetype would probably use false reading to guard against their allies more than their enemies, and that’s not usually a good sign for an ability. Just move on from this archetype already.


Is It Worth It?


        If it weren’t for the brute archetype, anaphexia thought-killer might easily take the cake for worst vigilante archetype of all time. Self-mutilation, a cumbersome and bloody transition into your vigilante identity, near-useless bonuses to pass hidden messages or conceal your thoughts from divination magic…there’s just so little to redeem the archetype. I want to love it, conceptually, but the mechanics are abysmal.




________________

---

# Avenging Beast

Avenging Beast


The Basics


        Vigilantes who cast spells as the hunter class and can wild shape (albeit somewhat poorly) into animals.


        Versatility: +1
        Power: -2


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Class Skills: Almost nothing but upshot here! Knowledge (Nature) and Spellcraft are both very useful skills (Spellcraft more so than Nature) and Knowledge (Engineering), Perform, and Sleight of Hand are almost never used. Certain builds might regret the loss of Disable Device, but those certain builds would also probably have a means of reacquiring Disable Device as a class skill through traits or something.


        (1) Skill Ranks: Boo, hiss. Well, avenging beasts aren’t going to be skill monkeys, that’s for sure. Plenty of classes survive with 4 + Intelligence skill ranks, though, and you can, too. Necessity, mothers, inventions—you know the saying.


        (1) Animal Mask (Ex): Alters dual identity. There are a lot of opportunities to get screwed in this archetype if your animal mask gets stolen, sundered, etc., but with the only class feature reliant on your vigilante identity being the undertuned version of wild shape you get at 5th level, there’s not much lost if you have to play an avenging beast in your social identity most of the time. Granted, that’s the iconic feature of this archetype, but it’s not essential to the playstyle.


        (1) Spellcasting: Replaces 4th-, 8th-, 10th-, 14th-, and 16th-level vigilante talents. I’m on record as believing that most Pathfinder players seriously underestimate the hunter spell list. First, you gain all the spells up to 6th level on the druid spell list, which is absolutely crammed full of valuable battlefield control spells. Perhaps more interestingly for a class that’s meant to be primarily combat-oriented, however, the hunter spell list grants you all the spells on the ranger spell list—with far more spell slots and access at far earlier levels than rangers themselves get! Allfood, bow spirit, hunter’s howl, protective spirit—there are lots of amazing ranger-exclusive spells, and hunters get ‘em, too. You’ll obviously have to pick your vigilante talents with care, but that’s not impossible to do, and the sacrifice is worth it in this instance.


        (1) Patron Spells: Replaces vigilante specialization. With so many of your vigilante talents gone, I couldn’t have gotten too excited about a specialization anyway, but patron spells still feels a little lackluster for my taste. In exchange for full BAB or hidden strike dice, avenging beasts get their patron’s spells added to their spell lists. You don’t automatically learn them as spells known, so choose wisely!


        (5) Wild Shape (Su): Replaces startling appearance, frightening appearance, and stunning appearance. I’m mostly okay with surrendering the appearance abilities, but I do wish that this version of wild shape weren’t so tragically limited. You can only ever assume the form of the animal represented by your mask, and when you do, it only acts as beast shape I. You’ll never get larger combat forms, better attribute bonuses, more exotic abilities, or wider versatility when wild shaped, taking much of the wind out of the ability’s sails before it even leaves harbor.


Is It Worth It?


        I’m not quite convinced, no. Gaining access to the hunter spell list is a great pickup, true, but wild shape, patron spells, and your notorious animal mask are all fairly steep downgrades from the base vigilante class features they replace. My advice would be just to play a hunter if you want this kind of playstyle.




________________

---

# Brute

Brute


The Basics


        Hulk-esque vigilantes who completely lose control of their ability to distinguish friends from foes during combat. Fun fact: the brute archetype is widely acknowledged to be one of the worst in the game. I’ve done a third-party rewrite of the archetype here, in case you’re interested in what that might look like.


        Versatility: -2
        Power: -2


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Chaotic Vigilante (Ex): Alters dual identity. Blah blah alignment restrictions blah blah. Sure.


(1) Chaotic Vigilante [3PP]: No changes needed. The chaotic alignment restriction is annoying, but not mechanically bad.


(1) Brutish Dualism [3PP]: Although I appreciate the Hulk-like changes that this archetype makes to the vigilante chassis, there’s more than one way to skin a cat, and I think a freakishly speedy, dexterous brute is every bit as interesting as a smashy-smashy brute. Brutish dualism essentially creates two “tracks” for the archetype, one of which will focus more on Dexterity-based combat, while the other is the traditional brute you know and love (to hate).
