        (1) Brutish Fortitude (Ex): Alters base saving throws. Ack! You lose two good saves (Reflex and Will) in exchange for one good save (Fortitude). Given that Will saves are far and away the most important save type in the game, and doubly so when you will continue to attack your allies after a fight has concluded unless you succeed at a high Will save, this is absolutely unacceptable. 


(1) Brutish Resilience [3PP]: My third-party rewrites give both wrath and freak brutes a strong Will save progression; the only difference is that wrath brutes get a strong Fortitude save progression, while freak brutes get a strong Reflex save progression. Flavor this however you want: brutes are too angry to be mind-controlled, whatever. They need strong Will save progression mechanically, or else they will start murdering team members once a fight has concluded.


        (1) Weapon and Armor Proficiencies: Alters weapon and armor proficiencies. Ayayay. Brutes already have huge AC problems, thanks to brute form’s -2 penalty, and then add on the loss of medium armor? No way. Martial weapons I’m fine surrendering, but you need your armor or a valid equivalent.


(1) Weapon and Armor Proficiencies [3PP]: My third-party rewrites go one step further than Paizo did and eliminate a brute’s armor proficiencies altogether. Nani…!? Yes, that’s right. Does the Hulk wear armor? No, he does not. So we’re going to slap some natural armor and dodge bonuses on this package in the next ability. You can use a wand of mage armor to supplement, like any other monk.


        (1) Brute Form (Ex): Replaces vigilante specialization. So you wanted to be the Hulk, did you? Well, I hope you’re happy, world, because this is the stinking pile of dog crap that Paizo squeezed out onto the lawn when you clamored for Hulk fan service. To get to my point, brute form is universally loathed, and for excellent reason. Why, I hear you ask? Let’s run through a list of brute form’s many fatal flaws in no particular order before you beg for my third-party rewrites:


      * A permanent -2 to AC. Wasn’t the -1 AC from the size change enough for you, Paizo? You had to kick us while we’re down?
      * Absolutely tanks your out-of-combat utility. Brute form prevents the vigilante from using Bluff, Diplomacy, Use Magic Device, Disguise, Handle Animal, Disable Device, Escape Artist, Sleight of Hand, Stealth, Appraise, Linguistics, Spellcraft, or any Knowledge check while in their vigilante identity. Not to mention the ability proscribes any activities that require “patience or concentration,” which GMs could interpret as liberally as their twisted minds allow. (I 🖤 you, GMs!) Vigilantes in their social identities can’t often participate in combat without risking discovery, but a saving grace of the class is that vigilantes in their vigilante identities can often do most things that their social identities could, including skill use, spellcasting, reading a children’s picturebook, etc. Without those aspects of the vigilante class, you are a weapon and nothing more—and that’s not a compliment.
      * Terrible entry into brute form. Brutes are renowned for their inability to control the shift into their vigilante identities, and it’s a doozy, even in an archetype that’s already a real doozfest. If you fail a Will save as combat starts, you immediately start the transition to your vigilante identity. Allies will know your secret double life, as will any enemy that happens to witness this. More than that, the process takes 1 full round and cannot be shortened with quick change, immediate, change, transformation sequence, or other social talents…plus you’re flat-footed the entire time, making you easy prey for sneak attack damage and bad on-hit rider effects. The worst.
      * You’ll end up attacking your friends. Classic Hulk, right? Yes, except The Avengers goes into great detail about why having an uncontrollable, easily provoked, infinitely powerful smash-monster on your team leads to such volatile situations. The Will save to stop mauling your allies starts at DC 21 at 1st level, when essentially nothing but a natural 20 will pass, and doesn’t get meaningfully better throughout your career, thanks to a poor Will save progression and little support in the class for a strong Wisdom stat. Folks in the Max the Min thread about this archetype have some ideas, but they almost always burn your teammates’ resources, and that’s a major party foul.
      * Your armor and weapons don’t resize. Again, classic Hulk: mild-mannered Bruce Banner wakes up in an impact crater, buck-naked because he hulked out of his clothes. Humorous to watch Mark Ruffalo cosplay; really freaking annoying to have to deal with as a Pathfinder PC. This forces you into the unenviable position of having to either slap magical enchantments on your equipment (a resizing weapon will set you back a cool 4,000 gp, while fitting armor costs 2,000 gp) or else burn a precious vigilante talent on sizing equipment.
      * You become fatigued or exhausted by your transitions. It’s like Paizo built a machine specially engineered to kick this archetype in the metaphorical fruitstand with all the force of Lionel Messi on steroids, over and over again. Yeesh. Immunity to fatigue or exhaustion (e.g., the horizon walker’s desert terrain dominance or the oracle’s lame curse) also make you immune to turning into a brute, and you can’t remove the fatigued or exhausted condition by any means except rest. I have no idea how to fix this except to burn the entire archetype down to the waterline and start over again.


(1) Brute Form [3PP]: Brute form is the ability that absolutely, 100% needs to get completely overhauled by third-party content, so let’s run through the changes I’ve made:


      * Your armor and weapons resize automatically. I have no idea why Paizo punished the archetype like this, but it’s gone now. Enjoy your extra vigilante talent or 6,000 gp.
      * You can use all skills in your vigilante identity, albeit at a penalty. Rather than penalize Dexterity, Intelligence, and Charisma, I penalized Intelligence, Wisdom, and Charisma, and rather than make you unable to use these skills entirely, I simply added a scaling penalty to some checks.
      * You can choose to transform out of sight, for a price, or transform in full view, for a benefit. I’m a fan of decision points that are meaningful, so I wrote one into the ability. You can choose to transform out of sight, but at the cost of dealing yourself nonlethal damage every round to suppress your murderous urges, or you can transform in full view of enemies, causing them to become shaken or frightened but risking the secrecy of your vigilante identity. At the very least, the brute needs a way not to get made unless they want to get made.
      * You become confused after combat is over, instead of dominated. In addition to changing the scaling of the Will save DC to transform (10 + vigilante level is infinitely more lenient than 20 + ½ vigilante level) I made it so that the brute simply becomes confused when there are no enemies left. You might roll “attack nearest creature,” but you might also harm yourself or babble incoherently. It still lends some urgency to your post-combat actions, but doesn’t make it so that you automatically start dismantling your loved ones when there aren’t any mooks left to be slaughtered.
      * You take mental ability damage for transforming, rather than the fatigued or exhausted conditions. The longer you stay in your brute form, the more ability damage you rack up to your Intelligence, Wisdom, and Charisma scores as your social identity recoils from the horrors of your vigilante identity. You can still heal it through natural or magical rest, but you can also heal it through murder. The social identity may fear and loathe killing; the vigilante identity revels in it. Anyway, this felt like a nice push-and-pull mechanic that lets you take some penalties for overusing brute form while not shooting you in both kneecaps absolutely every day.
      * Automatic enhancement bonuses to Strength or Dexterity. This should mostly be thought of as a means of compensating brutes (who will tend toward unarmed strike builds) for the amulet of mighty fists that they’re inevitably going to need. The bonuses are enhancement bonuses, rather than alchemical bonuses or somesuch, in order to avoid too steep a power curve.
      * Automatic dodge or natural armor bonuses to AC. Skipping the armor question entirely makes sense for this archetype, which feels like it shouldn’t narratively rely on armor anyway. Instead, wrath brutes get scaling natural armor bonuses to AC, while freak brutes get scaling dodge bonuses to AC. Add on a wand of mage armor, and a 2nd-level brute can have an AC of about 18 or 19. This scales up pretty well into the endgame; accounting for mage armor, the “replacement” AC values for wrath brutes will be about +11 (equivalent to a +5 breastplate) and +9 for freak brutes (equivalent to a +5 chain shirt). The rules text precludes any stat from being added to AC, so GMs fearful of monk dips should rest easy.


        (2) Brute Talents: Alters vigilante talents. Brute talents are actually pretty good! Well, some are, at least. Let’s review:


      * (2) Heavy Punches (Ex): Monk unarmed strike scaling immediately earns its blue rating with aplomb. Especially given that the brute’s size increase automatically increases your damage (to a maximum of 4d8 at 20th level, rather than the standard 2d10 for Medium-sized creatures) this talent is exceptionally worth it. More damage never goes out of style.
      * (8) Awesome Blow (Ex): Awesome Blow is, I would hazard, flirting with a yellow rating. It costs a standard action, forcing you to miss out on a full-attack action, and relies on a successful CMB check against a Medium or smaller creature. If you nail the CMB check, though, you get to damage the opponent, move them against their will, and cause them to fall prone—a tidy little package.
      * (4) Scale Surroundings (Ex): A climb speed is great if a fly speed isn’t available. I’m fine with this.
      * (2) Total Destruction (Ex): Total destruction would be an awesome talent but for one itsy-bitsy problem: it doesn’t allow you to use your Strength modifier on attack rolls with thrown weapons. This is still doable with a lesser belt of mighty hurling, but frankly, a talent needs to come functional out of the box if you intend to spend a precious resource investing in it. If your GM is willing to give you Strength to attack rolls, it becomes much, much better—a cheap and easy ranged option for a mostly melee archetype. This is the change I make in my third-party rewrite of the talent.
      * (2) Sizing Equipment (Ex): Sizing equipment is a talent tax, plain and simple. See my third-party rewrites to brute form if you’d rather not have to suffer through this horse manure.


(2) Brute Talents [3PP]: These should mostly be self-explanatory. The only changes I’ve made to Paizo’s talents are to eliminate sizing equipment and buff total destruction so that it allows you to use your Strength modifier on attack rolls.


        (20) Tear Them Apart (Ex): Replaces vengeance strike. I enjoy tear them apart, both for the undeniably metal name as well as for the fact that it’s more or less a passive damage buff. If you anticipate getting to 20th level, you should almost certainly take the morphic weaponry vigilante talent, as adding a bunch of natural attacks to your normal unarmed iteratives will increase the likelihood that you hit with enough attacks to proc your rend.


Is It Worth It?


        Oh, brute. You know you’re in trouble when you’ve been the subject of a Max the Min Reddit thread. It’s a pity, because I actually quite like some of the brute talents, and I love the idea of a Hulk/Mr. Hyde kind of vigilante. But brute form, the loss of a strong Will save, and the loss of your armor and weapon proficiencies are so hard in the red that there’s truly no way for the archetype to recover. Go with the third-party rewrites, or don’t go at all.




________________

---

# Darklantern

Darklantern


The Basics


        Elven vigilantes who transform into drow in order to infiltrate drow societies in the Darklands. Comes with a meddlesome Will save mechanic that deals Wisdom damage each time they try to resume their social identity.


        Versatility: -1
        Power: -2


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Elven Ancestry: Elves aren’t any great shakes as vigilantes, so you’ll want to go half-elf for the overlap with the human favored class bonus and floating stat bonus.


        (1) Dark Identity (Su): Alters dual identity. Dark identity turns your vigilante side into a drow, and while there are decent compensations for the form (+2 Dexterity and Charisma is nice, as is darkvision to augment the half-elf’s low-light vision) the disadvantages outweigh the advantages. First, there’s that nasty Will save mechanic to resume your social identity—reminiscent of the brute archetype—with Wisdom damage attached to every failure. Until you can reliably hit your Will save DC to transform back on everything but a natural 1, it’s best not to increase the time you spend in your dark identity beyond 1 hour—ability damage is hard to deal with at low levels, and every point of Wisdom damage reduces your ability to hit the DC the next time you try to break away from your drow form. Second is a thematic objection: absent everybody’s favorite drow Drizzt, anybody on Golarion who knows even a little bit about drow will despise and probably attack you in your vigilante identity. Is that something you want to deal with?


        (1) Drow Magic (Sp): Replaces 1st-level social identity. This is essentially one 0th-, 1st-, and 2nd-level spell per day, and while faerie fire is undoubtedly a great spell, dancing lights and darkness are considerably less useful as 1/day abilities. Plus, drow magic competes with owl’s sight, companion to the lonely, social grace, renown, and other good 1st-level social talents. My instinct is that this ain’t worth it.


        (4) Drow Paragon (Su): Replaces 4th-level vigilante talent. Unfortunately, SR 6 + level is about as weak as it gets. Against a caster of an even level, you have only about a 25% chance to avoid a spell—and that’s not even counting Spell Penetration or similar effects. Now, granted, a lot of monsters with class levels tend to have caster levels much lower than a PC’s level, but spell resistance will still prevent your allies’ spells from affecting you unless you take a standard action to lower your spell resistance. That makes in-combat buffing pretty difficult. I don’t know. I’m not enthused.


Is It Worth It?


        Between the lore complications of assuming the form of a drow, the hardships of transforming from your vigilante identity to your social identity, and the relative weakness of drow magic and drow paragon, I can’t recommend it. It might work for certain Darklands-themed campaigns, but otherwise, no.




________________

---

# Dragonscale Loyalist

Dragonscale Loyalist


The Basics


        Pro-monarchy Brevoyan vigilantes who pretend to be nobility. The archetype loses relatively little, but also doesn’t gain much.


        Versatility: -1
        Power: -1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Ruby Courtier (Ex): Alters social talents and replaces 1st-level social talent. Quite beyond the fact that I believe swapping Exotic Weapon Proficiency for a 1st-level social talent is a bad trade, ruby courtier locks you out of some of your most powerful social talents, including the many guises line. Why all the mundane identity hate, Brevoy?


        (3) Reflexive Reaction (Ex): Replaces unshakable. Reflexive reaction is better than unshakable, but frankly, that’s a laughably easy bar to clear. You don’t come out ahead in the action economy at all, since you’re still staggered on the first round of combat, but stalker vigilantes in particular would be able to take advantage of a full-attack while enemies are flat-footed.


        (5) False Allegiance (Ex): Replaces startling appearance. Oh boy, you know how I love class features that are actually seven class features standing on top of each other’s shoulders in a trenchcoat! We’ll get into the nitty gritty below, but the value of the class feature depends on whether you’re a stalker or avenger. Avengers care less about startling appearance than stalkers do, and even then, only about four of the seven possible houses are worth anything.


      * House Lebeda: One of the top houses for dragonscale loyalists. It’s sure as shootin’ not because of Skill Focus: Appraise, either, which is just as poor as it sounds. No, it’s for the extra 10% commission you get on all equipment you sell! Combine four PCs’ wealth by level, add 10% on top, and then tell me that this isn’t worth surrendering startling appearance for. Heck, if you have a crafter buddy, they can craft goods for 50% of their listed cost, and you can then sell them for 60%. Talk about a mercantile wombo combo.
      * House Surtova: Persuasive supplies some sound bonuses to interaction skills. Plus, it’s not like using Sense Motive to discern when someone is lying about being your friend or ally is uncommon. Pretty solid, overall.
      * House Garess: Garess isn’t terrible, but it’s a long way from good, too. Climbing should come up less and less as campaigns progress, making Sure Grasp dubious, and stonecunning (while nice for the automatic roll to notice unusual stonework) is often reliant on a GM remembering to roll it.
      * House Lodovka: Fair is fair: in an aquatic campaign, the Lodovka bonuses would be pretty great, giving you some well-rounded skill bonuses while also allowing you to ignore armor check penalties for most armor while swimming. Everywhere else? Yeah, not so much.
      * House Medvyed: Endurance is widely considered a weak feat used only to clear away the prereqs for Diehard. Resist nature’s lure, likewise, is very weak. Not a good package.
      * House Orlovsky: Call Truce is a bad feat with a ludicrously long “casting” time, a sky-high Diplomacy DC, and a decent chance of failing altogether due to Intelligence restrictions or enemy hostility. CMD bonuses, likewise, are worth almost nothing. Probably the weakest house bonuses of all.
      * House Rogarvia: Knowledge (History) rarely comes up in campaigns, and Diplomacy checks to gather information are pretty niche, too. I’m comfortable calling this red.


        (11) Dragonscale Vigilance (Su): Replaces frightening appearance. Dragonscale vigilance is an NPC ability, if it’s anything, and even then, perceive betrayal is a staggeringly non-specific spell for what it costs. Again, an easy red.


        (17) Conqueror’s Wrath (Ex): Replaces stunning appearance. Conqueror’s wrath is technically more versatile than the mad rush avenger vigilante talent, since you don’t have to charge to use it, but it comes with some hefty attack and damage bonuses that enemies get to leverage against you. Watch your opponents carefully for attacks of opportunity, because if they attack you, chances are excellent that they will hit, and hit hard. Still a great class feature.


Is It Worth It?


        Not truly, no. Conqueror’s wrath is the best argument for the archetype, but unless you’re playing at the very end of a six-book Paizo AP, you’ll never see that class feature. You’re left with a limited repertoire of social talents and some mediocre abilities otherwise. If you’re looking to trade away startling, frightening, and stunning appearance, experimenter is probably the superior archetype.




________________

---

# Gunmaster

Gunmaster


The Basics


        Vigilantes who draw inspiration from the gunslinger class. It’s as straightforward as it sounds, but loses most of what makes gunslingers, well, good.


        Versatility: -1
        Power: -1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Weapon and Armor Proficiencies: Alters weapon and armor proficiencies. Gummasters will be focused on Dexterity by default, so medium armor is no huge loss. And since the archetype is built around mastering, ya know, guns, I suppose it’s also no huge loss to give up martial weapons.


        (1) Gunmaster: Replaces vigilante specialization. Every gunslinger needs the ability to craft their own firearms and ammunition, since these items are so rare in Golarion canon. The second part of the ability is, unfortunately, quite a bit worse than what you would get from a 5-level dip into gunslinger. Firearms really don’t need any extra attack bonuses, since you’ll almost always be trying to hit touch AC; what you’re left with is a vastly inferior damage bonus than the one gunslingers get from gun training (gun training grants the full Dexterity bonus to damage immediately; gunmaster grants a +1 damage bonus every 4 levels). I don’t personally believe this is an upgrade from either stalker or avenger, but your mileage may vary.


        (2) Gunmaster Talents: Alters vigilante talents. The issue with gunmaster talents is that gunmasters don’t get a grit pool, and without a grit pool, all of your most important deeds (questionably deadeye, but certainly quick clear and lightning reload) are based off of maximum daily uses. That’s bad. Gunslingers get to use their deeds as often as they have grit, which they replenish throughout the day with kills, critical hits, etc. Gunmasters end up in the unenviable position of needing these to use deeds just as much as gunslingers do, but being far less able to use them than gunslingers are.


      * (2) Deadeye (Ex): Deadeye is always a good pick for gunslingers. Targeting touch AC is kinda the whole ballgame where firearms are concerned, making the deed particularly useful for picking off a target from long(er) range with a final iterative attack when you might have chosen to target regular AC for iteratives at higher BABs. The daily use cap sucks.
      * (4) Gunmaster Initiative (Ex): Gunmaster initiative is identical to gunslinger initiative, which is to say that it’s pretty good, allowing you a better chance of pulling off a full-attack action against enemies that ambush you at close range in the first round of combat. Since it doesn’t cost any grit, it has no daily use cap.
      * (20) Death’s Shot (Ex): A 20th-level save-or-die effect that only procs when you crit with a firearm? Nah. I mean, with a base x4 crit modifier, a firearm should still hurt like hell when you manage to confirm, and plenty of enemies at this level will be immune to death effects, Fortitude saves, critical hits, or all three.
      * (12) Lightning Reload (Ex): I always thought lightning reload was a bit of a bizarre deed for gunslingers. All firearm reload times go down in steps from full-round → standard → move → free, and both the Rapid Reload feat and alchemical cartridges reduce the loading time by one step. (Hint: there’s a reason they’re considered mandatory investments for gunslingers.) So for any one-handed firearm, you’d have been full-attacking with free actions by, what, 5th level at the very latest? That leaves only two uses for lightning reload: first, to avoid attacks of opportunity when reloading—issue is, enemies within threatened range will still ping you for ranged attacks, regardless of reloads. Second, to allow two-handed firearm users to reload as a free action. But oh look, where gunslingers can do this all day (as long as they have grit in their pool) gunmasters can do it only enough for one, maybe two combats per day. Horrid.
      * (2) Quick Clear (Ex): Once per day? Once per day? Look how they massacred my boy! Quick clear is among the most important deeds for gunslingers to have, because all it takes is an unlucky roll while your firearm is misfired for something to explode. This deed was supposed to be the fast, cheap answer to that problem. Instead, you can’t even clear as a standard action consistently, and you can’t clear as a move action at all. Ugh.


        (4) Nimble (Ex): Replaces unshakable. Sure, a few points of free AC. It’s not numerically dramatic, but it’s sure as shootin’ better than unshakable.


Is It Worth It?


        Gunmaster doesn’t force you to pick up gunmaster talents, so really all the archetype changes is your vigilante specialization and weapon/armor proficiencies. You’d be hard-pressed to make firearms work with just those core abilities, though, and when you do move into gunmaster talents, everywhere you turn you’ll be confronted by the fact that the gunslinger class stands head and shoulders above this archetype. Since the vigilante doesn’t have any Wisdom-based archetypes and the gunslinger doesn’t have any Charisma-based archetypes (apart from mysterious stranger, which is terrible as a dip since you don’t get gun training until 9th level) I can’t think of a great way to fuse the two classes apart from a traditional 5-level gunslinger dip that mostly ignores Charisma at point buy to prioritize a larger grit pool. That’s a clunky solution, though, and frankly I think you’re better off letting the two parent classes stay separate. They both still love you very much, and you’ll see each of them on alternating weekends.




________________

---

# Half-Elf Double Scion

Half-Elf Double Scion


The Basics


        Half-elf vigilantes who gain some optional vigilante talents, but lose essentially nothing else.


        Versatility: -1
        Power: -1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Dual Heritage: Alters dual identity. Sure. Human by day, elf by night. Fine.


        (2) Half-Elf Double Scion Talents: Alters vigilante talents. Nothing but options, babyyy! (Mediocre options, but still, options!)


      * (10) Arrogant Strike (Ex): It’s fun to be able to call your crits like this, but realistically, even crit-fishing builds have no idea whether they’ll threaten enough to make the ability worthwhile. Plus, you can only use it only 1/day, when you pick it up at 10th level, which seems excessively stingy. I’d say just take Critical Focus if you really want to get your confirmation rolls on point.
      * (2) Slick Step (Ex): Close the gap will be the superior vigilante talent in almost all circumstances, but slick step still isn’t terrible.
      * (2) Elven Weapon Command (Ex): In a world where the ancestral arms and weapon familiarity alternate racial traits exist, elven weapon command is essentially a trap that burns a vigilante talent on Weapon Focus, which is *checks notes* bad, bad, bad.


Is It Worth It?


        Not truly, no. Even though the archetype gives up essentially nothing, the fact that it alters dual identity and vigilante talents prevents you from taking other, better archetypes that also alter those class features. Save your squeeze for better juice.




________________

---

# Hidden Current

Hidden Current


The Basics


        Amphibious vigilantes who need to transition often between terrestrial and aquatic terrains. The only Paizo AP to which this archetype is even somewhat suited is Ruins of Azlant, so keep that in mind.


        Versatility: -1
        Power: -1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Guise of the Land Walker (Ex): Replaces 1st-level social talent. Don’t get me wrong, it’s awesome to pick up many guises several levels before you’d normally be able to, but restricting you to a non-aquatic humanoid is…cumbersome, to say the least. I could only see this working in a game like Ruins of Azlant, and even then not all of the time.


        (2) Stealthy Swimmer (Ex): Replaces 2nd-level vigilante talent. You know you’re looking at a severely undertuned class feature when even in the type of campaign it’s built for, it’s still weak. Such is the unfortunate case of stealthy swimmer. Bad even in aquatic campaigns, especially compared with the usual power level of vigilante talents.


        (11) Sea’s Return (Sp): Replaces frightening appearance. I’ll never say no to free dimension door, but the uses per day and land-sea targeting limitations don’t make it quite as versatile as the spell. Still pretty good!


Is It Worth It?


        Not on the whole, no. Even in campaigns centered around aquatic environments, sea’s return (which you don’t get until 11th level!) is the only class feature that earns its keep in comparison with the base vigilante. Plus, taking this archetype locks you out of other, better ones. Let it go.




________________

# Magical Child

Magical Child


The Basics


        Anime-inspired vigilantes who gain unchained summoner spellcasting, a familiar, and the ability to do Sailor Moon-esque transformation sequences.


        Versatility: +1
        Power: -2


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Class Skills: Alters class skills. The magical child archetype skews much more toward arcane caster than rogue-like—and that’s no trouble, it’s just that you have to get used to it. If you were planning on taking on trap spotting and scouting duties, this ain’t the archetype to do it with; if you were planning to be more of a knowledge jockey, then this is the archetype to do it with.


        (1) Skill Ranks per Level: Well, 4 + Intelligence is obviously worse than 6 + Intelligence, but it’s in keeping with the design decision to make the archetype more like arcane casters, which generally don’t get many skill ranks per level.


        (1) Weapon and Armor Proficiencies: Alters weapon and armor proficiencies. Again, totally par for the arcane caster course. You’ll prioritize CHA > DEX, CON > WIS, INT > STR at point buy and stick it out in light armor.


        (1) Spellcasting: Replaces 4th-, 8th-, 10th-, 14th-, and 16th-level vigilante talents. I’m torn on this one. On the one hand, spellcasting is always good! It’s good for the cabalist, good for the warlock, and good for pretty much any other archetype in the game that can learn to cast spells. On the other hand, the unchained summoner spell list is a pale shadow of the chained summoner spell list, and without an eidolon to cast some of its most powerful spells on, you end up with this strangely mediocre spell selection. (Don’t mistake me: you’ve got invisibility, greater invisibility, see invisibility, fly, haste, heroism, dispel magic, magic circle against alignment, dimension door, stoneskin, dismissal, teleport, true seeing, and a bevy of wall, pit, and fog spells to work with, so even with a limited selection, you’ll still have enough to work with. It’s just that it’s suboptimal, relative to other spellcasting classes. You can refer to my resource The Spell Codex 2.0, sort by Unchained Summoner (A to Z) → Rating (Z to A) → Name (A to Z) to see a well-organized list of how I would rate the entire spell list.) I’ve heard suggestions from other GMs to swap the unchained summoner spell list for the bard spell list, which seems like a sensible suggestion. The bard spell list has the same support flair that I think Paizo was going for, but with a more eidolon-agnostic approach.
Regarding what to do with your three vigilante talents, there isn’t any vigilante talent support for spellcasting, so magical children will need to take perhaps one offensive vigilante talent—lethal grace would be my recommendation—and then focus on defensive and mobility options to ensure you don’t get pinned down and beaten to death. Shadow’s speed, sure-footed, and steely resolve are good on this front.


        (1) Transformation Sequence (Su): Prepare for trouble! And make it double! Transformation sequence is another one of those abilities that makes it almost entirely impossible to keep your vigilante and social identities separate. After all, it’s not an easy task when you’re shooting light from every orifice and “Immigrant Song” is playing in the background. The best remedy for this problem is simply to loudly proclaim, “I’m a magical child!” and let the world know that your social identity is your vigilante identity. Let your familiar be the one who lurks in the shadows, defending justice. You’re just a kid…with magic.


        (1) Animal Guide (Ex): Replaces vigilante specialization. So…animal guide is a really weird ability. Awesome, but weird, weird, weird in terms of how it interacts with normal familiar rules. Things don’t start out weird: you get a familiar with DR/magic equal to your vigilante level, and staunch ally gives it the option to use startling appearance, etc. So it’s a combat familiar, right? Just slap the mauler archetype on there and call it quits. But wait! Your animal guide transforms into a creature from the Improved Familiar list at 3rd level, and some of the best familiar archetypes (valet, mauler, protector, etc.) don’t stack with Improved Familiar, since both options replace the speak with animals of its own kind ability. When your animal guide picks up two more forms at 5th and 7th level and then the ability to shapeshift between any of its four forms at 9th level, you can start to see what a headache this class feature becomes.
        Originally, people just applied different sets of archetypes to the different forms. The base familiar identity kept mauler, protector, or whatever familiar archetype you wanted to give it, and the Improved Familiar forms got either a compatible archetype (sage, e.g.) or else no archetype at all. Then along came Ultimate Wilderness, which quietly sent animal guides into the Big Sleep with the nerfhammer:


Shapechanging Familiars: Familiars that can take various forms, such as the magical child’s animal guide, imps, and quasits, must have the same archetype for each form, and it must be legal for all of those forms (meaning if any form is an improved familiar, it can’t take archetypes that don’t stack with improved familiar).


        So if you take a sage familiar, it always has to be a sage, regardless of current identity; if you take a mauler familiar, it always has to be a mauler—and can’t be, because mauler doesn’t stack with Improved Familiar. Personally, I think Paizo is vastly overrating the power level of familiars, even with archetypes. Especially since the next class feature grants your familiar some level of combat efficacy, you want it to be decent in a scrap. That means mauler is a must. If you want my recommendation, simply ignore the Ultimate Wilderness errata and allow different familiar forms to have different archetypes—the base form gets mauler, another form gets sage, a third gets emissary, etc. I always thought it was lunacy that “improved” familiars couldn’t use most of the archetypes that, ya know, improve familiars. RAW, however, animal guide is a bad ability that deserves a red rating. Gaining a familiar does not compare favorably with losing full BAB, scaling hidden strike dice, or the slew of vigilante talents available to the base specializations.


        (5) Staunch Ally (Ex): Alters startling appearance, frightening appearance, stunning appearance, and vengeance strike. Yeah, see, this is just one more reason why the magical child truly needs a familiar with the mauler archetype. Normally, familiars are pretty bad at combat, barring things like using wands or delivering touch spells, and if you want them to be actually fighting and using all of these class features, they definitely need more power. GMs, just let your players have their multiple familiar archetypes.


Is It Worth It?


        RAW? No. Magical child is an unfortunate errata victim that got done dirty by Paizo when it really didn’t need to be. Swap the spell list for the bard’s and allow multiple familiar archetypes as originally written, and you’ve got a pretty decent archetype, though.




________________

---

# Masked Maiden

Masked Maiden


The Basics


        Traumatized former Gray Maidens whose suits of armor are as much psychological prisons as they are physical protections. The archetype comes with some pretty stiff penalties (no morale bonuses or benefits of bardic performances while in the vigilante identity, e.g.) so make sure you know what you’re doing here.


        Versatility: -2
        Power: +1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Weapon and Armor Proficiency: Alters weapon and armor proficiencies. Gray Maiden plate armor for free? Sure! Saves you a vigilante talent spent on heavy training.


        (1) Imperfect Control (Ex): Alters dual identity. Imperfect control + scars of the past kind of work together to create a strange, strange vigilante experience. Imperfect control forces involuntary changes (pending failed Will saves) between your social and vigilante identities, so you won’t be able to go the Iron Man route and simply conduct your business fully in your social identity. On the other hand, scars of the past eliminates seamless guise, which is the core mechanical barrier separating your social and vigilante identities. So you have all the drawbacks of people knowing that you’re a former Gray Maiden who still can’t shake the compulsions while also having all the drawbacks of having to switch between identities, often involuntarily and often at the worst possible moment. It’s rough, I’ll be honest. Probably your best bet is to remain as a vigilante for the entire day; you’ve lost a majority of your social talents, so it’s not like you’ll be missing much power there. Staying as a vigilante ensures that you don’t get forced into that identity against your will.


        (1) Scars of the Past (Ex): Replaces seamless guise. As above. Losing seamless guise means that people will associate your social and vigilante identities.


        (3) Armor Training (Ex): Replaces 3rd-, 7th-, 11th-, 15th-, and 19th-level social talents. If we were just trading out social talents for armor training, I’d say no way, but fully scaling armor training opens the way for advanced armor training, and some of these options are amazing. Armor specialization is an easy pick, since you’ll only ever wear Gray Maiden plate; armored juggernaut is a simple source of DR/—; adaptable training and critical deflection have their places, but probably not as your first picks. At any rate, this is a straight power for versatility trade.


Is It Worth It?


        Honestly, the masked maiden doesn’t really play all that much like a vigilante. You’ve lost most of your social talents and seamless guise, and dual identity has been replaced by the strictly poorer imperfect control—so what’s left? You’re an avenger, so you still have full BAB, and you still have all your vigilante talents. It might honestly be best to think of this one as a fighter archetype that grants a few nifty tricks from vigilante. I’d recommend you just stay in your vigilante identity all day; you won’t have the Disguise bonuses to navigate the switch between identities well, and a single failed Will save will lock you into that identity all day anyway.




________________

---

# Mutated Defender

Mutated Defender


The Basics


        Obviously a nod to the X-Men, mutated defenders gain mutant powers in their vigilante identities that they can hide while in their social identities.


        Versatility: -1
        Power: -1


        Stacks With: 


The Abilities


        (1) Mutant Specialization: Replaces vigilante specialization. Full BAB is good, although I’m less enthused about the lack of stalker or vigilante talents on tap. As for mutant deformities, oh boy, it’s time for some good, old-fashioned Pathfinder cheese! Wherever there’s a loophole, we’re gonna find it. Deformities are rated by how easy they are to bypass, as well as how detrimental they are before they’re bypassed.


      * Lame: Super easy! Barely an inconvenience! You love to see it. Reducing your base speed by 10 feet isn’t much of a hit at all, especially if you’re playing a ranged vigilante. Boots of striding and springing (5,500 gp) will solve your problem permanently, or else you can use a wand of longstrider if you’re even more strapped for cash. A couple thousand gold to solve your deformity is way better than the five or six feats it would take to solve blindness.
      * Light Blindness: Another deformity that’s super easy to solve with the 2nd-level spell protective penumbra. Have your resident prepared caster prep it for the day, use a lesser extend metamagic rod, and you’re off to the races. Heck, your GM might even let you have the cantrip version, penumbra, which doesn’t cost any spell resources at all.
      * Misshapen: I’m knocking this one down a rung only because it would require a dip into scaled fist unchained monk to easily bypass it. Still, scaled fist unchained monk is a great dip for vigilantes anyway: pump Dexterity and Charisma, grab the lethal grace vigilante talent and a wand of mage armor, and ignore manufactured armor entirely. Your GM might also consider something like flowing fortress armor, which is literally just water turned into armor. How could that not reasonably fit any body type?
      * Deaf: While not quite as crippling as blindness, deafness is still pretty bad. You don’t have any verbal spellcasting components to worry about—not an issue—but communication with teammates, social interaction, and auditory Perception checks will all become impossible. Still too challenging.
      * Poor Ability: Intelligence would probably be your best choice—or Strength, perhaps—but even then, penalties to your ability scores are gnarly. I’d pass.
      * Useless Arm: I don’t think this is the roughest deformity in the world, but it’s a far cry from easy to work around. You’d have to play a one-handed Dexterity build for optimal mechanics, and even then, you wouldn’t be able to draw and drink potions, use wands, etc.
      * Blind: Blindness is really, really hard to cope with in Pathfinder. Oh, sure, you could worship Vildeis, take the Blind Zeal trait, dip master of many styles monk to pick up Blinded Blade Style and Improved/Greater Blind Fight, and eventually arrive at 30-foot blindsight, but is it really worth five feats and a trait to overcome? Cool concept—it just requires more squeeze than I’m willing to give it for the juice.
      * Fragile: Vigilantes have poor Fortitude saves, so you might end up staggered a surprisingly high percentage of the time. Let’s not make failed saves worse than they already are, mkay?
      * Fractured Mind: As fragile, but with even worse consequences.
      * Spasms: As fragile and fractured mind. Failed saves are bad enough.


        (2) Mutant Talents: Alters vigilante talents. Given that you entered here, abandoned all hope of vigilante or stalker talents, and took a mutant deformity to boot, we’d better hope it was worth it, huh? Well, I hate to spoil the ending with that color rating, but let’s take a look at the individual talents:


      * (2) Mutant Evolution: Eidolon evolutions are typically great when applied to PCs, but the issue here is that many of the best evolutions are denied to you—whether explicitly by the ability text or implicitly because you don’t meet the eidolon’s form requirements (lookin’ at you, pounce). You can turn yourself into a Big Ball O’ Jaws ‘N’ Claws, as is the time-honored summoner tradition, but honestly, vigilantes can already do that with significantly less investment via morphic weaponry. Reach and resistance are the only non-natural attack evolutions I’d want to take out of those available to you, and that doesn’t make a great argument for the archetype.
      * (9) Mutant Blast (Sp): Man, this is…as bad as they come. If you got a limited version of kineticist’s simple blast or some variant of the warlock vigilante’s mystic bolts, I might consider it, but the maximum scaling at 20th level for elemental ray is 1d6+10. Does that seem worthwhile to you, especially when resistances and immunities are a thing and you get so few uses per day? It sure doesn’t to me.
      * (4) Mutated Lobe (Sp): I don’t like to say no, so I’ll just say…nah, bro. Detect thoughts isn’t great anyway, as every enemy will eventually be able to crush the Will save DC for a 2nd-level spell. Any serious enemy will have abjuration magic in place to prevent this kind of mental intrusion, too. So what’s the point?


Is It Worth It?


        No. Some mutant deformities are easily worked around, but why would you bother when the mutant talents being offered range from mediocre to downright bad?




________________






Zealot


The Basics


        Zealots are divine spellcasters who gain the ability to smite alignments, detect lies, channel energy, pronounce judgments, and other inquisitor/cleric/paladin chestnuts. A fun archetype, but it mostly struggles to keep pace with the classes that inspired it.


        Versatility: +1
        Power: -2


        Stacks With:

---

# The Abilities

The Abilities


        (1) Class Skills: Alters class skills. Pretty much nothing but upside here. Knowledge (Religion) and Spellcraft are way more useful than any of the four replaced skills.


        (1) Skill Ranks Per Level: Alters skill ranks per level. Boo, hiss. I like my skill ranks. Don’t take my skill ranks from me.


        (1) Weapon Proficiencies: Alters weapon proficiencies. Vigilantes are already proficient with all simple and martial weapons, so this will only make a difference if your deity has an exotic weapon as their favored implement of murder. It’s nice that Irori worshipers get Improved Unarmed Strike for free, but you’d probably want a scaled fist unchained monk dip anyway.


        (1) Spellcasting: Replaces 4th-, 8th-, 10th-, 14th-, and 16th-level vigilante talents. I’m going to go out on a limb and say that this actually isn’t so great for the zealot archetype. I’ll be the first to sing the praises of the inquisitor class, but what I won’t do is lie to you and tell you that its spell list is superior to other options on the market. Its only competition among ⅔ divine casters are the warpriest spell list (which mimics the cleric spell list and is therefore quite comprehensive) and the hunter spell list (which poaches every spell from both the druid and ranger list, for a pretty unbeatable selection). You’ll find great recommendations for spells in the linked guide, but my advice would be to stick with self-buffs and leave the rest of it alone.


        (1) Aligned Spells, Alignment, and Aura: Fairly boilerplate cleric/inquisitor stuff. Don’t worry about it too much.


        (1) Inquisition (Ex): Replaces vigilante specialization. This isn’t a fair trade; hidden strike dice and full BAB outmatch inquisitions, hands down. I’ve outlined some good inquisitions here, but you’ll have to figure out which one works best for you. Check with your GM to see whether they’ll permit you a domain as a normal inquisitor would get, because that would open up your options considerably.


        (2) Zealot Talents: Alters vigilante talents.


      * (2) Zealot Smite (Su): If you pick no other zealot talent, pick zealot smite. It’s essentially a paladin’s smite evil class feature, minus the AC bonus and minus a few niche cases where zealot smite won’t penetrate DR that smite evil would (DR/damage type or DR/—, notably, which can’t be pierced by weapon enhancement bonuses). To compensate, zealot smite permits you to smite a wider variety of creatures—not just evil or good, but chaos and law, too! This incentivizes you to pick an alignment at the extremes (lawful evil, lawful good, chaotic good, chaotic evil) so that you can smite a wider spectrum of alignments. Excellent, at any rate.
      * (2) Harsh Judgment (Su): I’m not sure why Archives of Nethys lists this talent on the vigilante talents page, but not on the zealot archetype page. *Shrug.* At any rate, it’s a fantastic talent. Even without second/third judgment, and even without the full spectrum of judgments available to inquisitors, you should still be salivating at the thought of judgments of destruction and purity. Easily worth a purchase.
      * (12) Stalwart (Ex): Part of the reason stalwart works pretty well for inquisitors is that inquisitors have strong Fortitude and Will saves. Vigilantes have strong Will saves, but not strong Fortitude saves—and when the ability relies on a successful save to work, that might be a bigger deal than you think. Stalwart will always be limited by the number of Fortitude or Will half/partial effects in the game, too: there are only about 220 spells in the game that have those save configurations, out of over 3000 spells overall. So…nice to have, but not essential by any stretch.
      * (2) Stern Gaze (Ex): Vigilantes specialize in Intimidate quite often, and especially with Signature Skill: Intimidate, it’s certainly not unreasonable to think that you might want to pump that skill bonus as high as possible so you can escalate fear conditions directly to frightened, panicked, or cowering. Even here, though, a skill bonus is a mighty thin rationale for burning one of only three vigilante talents on.
      * (2) Channel Energy (Su): As a zealot, you’ll get three vigilante talents (2nd, 6th, 12th) before most Pathfinder campaigns end. Do you really want to spend one of those talents on channel energy (a dubious class feature even for the cleric) when it will take you until 7th level to go up to 2d6 healing? I sure wouldn’t.
      * (6) Discern Lies (Sp): Discern lies earns a yellow rating in my inquisitor guide when it’s given to you for free. When you have to burn one of your three vigilante talents on it? Nah.
      * (6) Empower Symbol (Su): The addition of a permanent consecrate/desecrate effect to your holy symbol is going to be useful only for necromancers, who can get much more out of animate dead in a desecrated area than they can elsewhere. Unfortunately, animate dead isn’t on the inquisitor spell list, so…that’s a bummer. You could make do with a staff of animate dead, I suppose, but that’s a lot of trouble to go to.

---

# Is It Worth It?

Is It Worth It?


        Man, what a tricky question. Look: y’all know that I’m an inquisitor guy, through and through. It’s my bread and butter class. But I look at this and I think: “Yes, you’ve got social talents, but absent so many vigilante talents, how is this distinct or better than the inquisitor?” You’d have to spend four talents (stalwart, harsh judgment, stern gaze, discern lies) just buying back inquisitor powers, and you’d still have poorer skill ranks and domain choices, no monster lore, no cunning initiative, no teamwork feats, no bane, no second or third judgments…it just seems like you’re far better off playing an inquisitor if you want to be an inquisitor. All you’d be missing out on is zealot smite and the infiltration abilities you could get from many guises.




________________
VIG 605: Dips and VMC

---

# Dips

Dips


        For new players, “dipping” describes the process of taking some small number of class levels in a different class than the class the player intends to end up in eventually. One-level dips are the easiest, and most players agree that after about three levels you’re not really “dipping” anymore, but going for a truer multiclass. Dipping usually accomplishes a few things—the more things, the better the case for the dip.


      * Gaining class features that aren’t level-dependent. The paladin’s divine grace class feature, for example, simply adds your Charisma modifier to all your saving throws. You don’t need any further levels in paladin to enable the combo, which makes this particular dip attractive for Charisma-based classes.
      * Smoothing away feat prerequisites. Thanks to the presence of vigilante talents, which often replace feats or even improve on them, vigilantes aren’t terribly pressed for build space. Some dips throw a number of bonus feats at you in the first few levels, though, which helps you become combat-effective faster.
      * Weapon or armor proficiencies. Vigilantes have fairly good weapon and armor proficiencies already, but dipping for heavy armor or exotic weapon proficiencies isn’t out of the question.
      * BAB or saves. Stalker vigilantes have ¾ BAB progression, and all vigilantes suffer from a weak Fortitude save. Common dips like scaled fist unchained monk or paladin can help on both counts.




Dipping From Vigilante

---

# Melee (Strength)

Melee (Strength)


      * Paladin 2 or Paladin 3 / Antipaladin 2 or Antipaladin 3: Paladin and antipaladin have quite a lot to offer the aspiring vigilante: heavy armor proficiency, full BAB, a good Fortitude save progression, and a 1/day smite evil or good. Divine grace or unholy resilience are the true keys to the kingdom, adding your Charisma modifier to all saves. Vigilantes, especially Strength-based vigilantes, tend not to have the greatest saves: Wisdom usually can’t be boosted beyond 10 or so at point buy, Dexterity remains low due to heavy armor, and it’s difficult to pump Constitution hard enough to compensate for a weak Fortitude save progression. Two levels of paladin will get you +3 Fortitude and Will from the dip alone, plus an additional 6 or 7 points to every save over the course of your career. Antipaladin 3 also gets a shout-out for aura of cowardice, which makes enemies much more susceptible to effects like twisting fear and frightening appearance. Do note that alignment restrictions don’t often play nicely with vigilantes, though: you’ve got to stay lawful good or chaotic evil in both identities, social and vigilante, to retain your dipped abilities.
      * Fighter 1: A one-level fighter dip snags you a bonus feat, a +2 Fortitude bonus, and heavy armor proficiency. There are worse dips, and there are better dips.
      * Iroran Paladin 2 / Scaled Fist UnMonk 1 or 2: Unarmed vigilante builds are quite common, and there are few dips for Charisma-based unarmed builds as worthwhile as scaled fist unchained monk 1. A single level gets you +1 BAB, +2 Fortitude and Reflex, flurry of blows, Improved Unarmed Strike, Stunning Fist, a bonus feat that should probably be Dragon Style, and, crucially, Charisma to AC. A second level gets you another bonus feat and evasion. Iroran paladin, while not an exceptional dip, can fill some of the same roles as scaled fist unmonk while also giving you divine grace.


Melee (Dexterity)


      * None. Unlike other classes, which might consider a swashbuckler or unchained rogue dip to kickstart a career in Dexterity-based melee combat, the teisatsu vigilante already kinda out-rogues the rogue, if you take my meaning. The lethal grace vigilante talent is all you should need to make a Dexterity fighter work in this class.


Ranged (Dexterity)


      * Musketeer Swashbuckler 1: “Call an ambulance…but not for me.” Yes, a vigilante with a gun is about as iconic as it can get, but beware, Punisher, because Paizo built basically zero support for ranged vigilantes into the system. You’re on your own with this one.
      * Divine Hunter Paladin 2 / Arrow Champion Swashbuckler 1 / Bolt Ace Gunslinger 1 or 5: Probably some of the better options for bow-wielding vigilantes.

---

# Dipping To Vigilante

Dipping To Vigilante


        Dips to vigilante don’t really work, unfortunately. Vigilante and social talents are obviously the most valuable resources the class has to offer, but since there’s no “Extra Vigilante Talent” or “Extra Social Talent” feat, you can’t jump in, grab the class feature, then jump out again and continue purchasing vigilante talents with feats. Moreover, many of the best vigilante and social talents are gated behind vigilante levels, which makes dipping a non-starter. If you do dip, you have to be prepared to play the Iron Man angle, not concealing that your social and vigilante identities are one and the same—you simply won’t have the seamless guise bonuses to keep that secret. The level-gating in the class is real, though, and poses the larger problem.

---

# Variant Multiclassing

Variant Multiclassing


        Variant multiclassing can be a good option for classes with many bonus feats to spare, usually full martial classes looking to add some versatility, spellcasting, etc. Vigilantes exist in a strange space where they have lots of vigilante talents that grant feats (strike the unseen, shield of blades, vigilante’s reflexes, vital punishment, favored maneuver, etc.) but may not be the exact right feats you need to progress down a feat line. Thus, vigilante variant multiclassing builds will typically sit with only the bare of needed feats and let their vigilante talents “do the talking.” That said, there are some interesting options out there:

---

# Core Classes

Core Classes


      * Bard: I really, really like the bard variant multiclass. Bardic knowledge is great, versatile performance is great, and inspire courage is obviously aces. For vigilantes who want more support through knowledge checks, face skills, and party buffing, this is the right play. Don’t forget that certain wondrous items like the banner of the ancient kings,the dervish sikke, and three reasons to live can offset the penalties to inspire courage or bardic knowledge!
      * Rogue: A stalker vigilante with a rogue variant multiclass is kind of like a “rogue plus”: you’ve got hidden strike, but also sneak attack; you get evasion without needing to burn a vigilante talent on it; and most of your combat efficacy can be handled through vigilante talents like lethal grace or twisting fear, rendering the loss of feats minimally painful. Definitely one of the best variant multiclass options available. Check out The Murderer build for an example of how this might work in practice.
      * Cleric: A polarizing variant multiclass option. Most Pathfinder players acknowledge that channeling energy is a weak option in combat, and even outside of combat, your effective cleric level is too low to do much healing; moreover, there aren’t any easily purchasable wondrous items that increase your effective cleric level, like there are for the bard’s class features. Domain abilities, however, are a different story: touch of chaos, fury of the abyss, touch of good, liberation, bit of luck, vision of madness, inspiring word…there are some incredibly powerful abilities granted to clerics at 1st level, and the variant multiclass suffers no penalty to your effective level or uses per day. If you have a specific domain ability that is absolutely essential to your playstyle, this is an option worth considering.
      * Sorcerer: There are a stupid number of sorcerer bloodlines, and even though I’m not all that familiar with the class, I know enough to know that some of those bloodlines are exceptionally powerful. Toss out any bloodlines that improve spellcasting, though, because, well, you’re not a spellcasting class.
      * Barbarian: The barbarian variant multiclass is decent, but hindered somewhat by the fact that barbarians need rage powers to offset the weaknesses of rage—weaknesses like your inability to use most skills while raging, or the sudden decrease in HP (Sudden Barbarian Death Syndrome) that occurs when a chained barbarian’s rage drops as they fall unconscious. You don’t gain your first rage power until 11th level in the variant multiclass, and although you could theoretically use feats after that to take Extra Rage Power, you don’t have many feats available with which to do so. Combine that with the smaller number of rage rounds per day that you receive, and I can’t strongly justify this choice for vigilantes.
      * Wizard: Some of the arcane schools are particularly tasty for vigilantes (aether, transmutation, etc.) but you’re somewhat limited by what’s likely to be a low Intelligence score. If for some reason you’ve pumped Intelligence, there really are great options available for combat classes when they add in some wizard tricks.
      * Druid: I’ve always felt like the druid variant multiclass was a cleverly disguised trap. The main thing you’d want from it is a fully scaling animal companion, but you can acquire that with the classic three-feat combo of Nature Soul → Animal Ally → Boon Companion. Wild shape, wild empathy, and the druidic code of conduct don’t do anything good for vigilantes.
      * Fighter: Ugh. What a bad variant multiclass. The only thing you might conceivably want out of it is weapon training to qualify for weapon mastery feats. If that’s the case, the Martial Focus feat is what you want, not whatever this travesty is.
      * Monk: It’s nice to be able to pick up scaling unarmed strike damage without Monastic Legacy or a monk’s robe, but those feats/items plus a dip in scaled fist unchained monk are ultimately much better for vigilantes than a full variant multiclass. Most of the monk’s goodies are frontloaded; you don’t truly need a ki pool, evasion, etc.
      * Paladin: Another instance where the 2-level dip is infinitely more powerful than the variant multiclass. Divine health, a 1/day smite evil, heavy armor proficiency, etc. should all be good enough for you, and they’re available with no loss of feats.
      * Ranger: Ranger is on the weaker side even as a fully fledged class, for crying out loud, much less a variant multiclass that gives you nerfed versions of every class feature. No.

---

# Base Classes

Base Classes


      * Cavalier: I’m not all that familiar with the various cavalier orders, but you’ve got a good enough Charisma modifier that this variant multiclass isn’t out of the question. Note, however, that variant multiclassed cavaliers don’t receive any bonus teamwork feats, which limits the utility of tactician when you’re already trading away half of your feats.
      * Inquisitor: I suppose I’m biased, but I think the inquisitor variant multiclass is pretty solid. Judgment is strong and flexible, even 1/day or 2/day, and stern gaze synergizes naturally with many of the demoralization tactics that vigilantes are fond of. Solo tactics feels like a bit of an odd inclusion, as you run into the same problem as the cavalier variant multiclass: no bonus teamwork feats with which to fuel the class feature.
      * Oracle: It should be a mark of how powerful oracle revelations are that even the oracle variant multiclass, which is incredibly stingy with revelations, gets rated green. I’ve got a whole guide to the oracle class, so go and peruse at your leisure.
      * Alchemist: An interesting choice for experimenters, although the overlap between mutagen class features is unfortunately. Talk to your GM to see whether they’d let you get another variant multiclassing goodie at 11th level. You’ve got enormous bonuses to Craft (Alchemy) from this crossover, so prestige classes like Daggermark poisoner become more appealing. The alchemist variant multiclass is always a bit of an odd duck on Charisma-based classes, though.
      * Magus: A difficult dip for vigilantes, mostly because magus arcana, arcane pool, and spellstrike fall on deaf ears with classes that have no innate spellcasting. If you take a spellcasting archetype, though, especially one like cabalist that grants full access to the magus spell list, you’re very, very close to a magus with vigilante abilities, which is pretty cool.
      * Summoner: The summoner variant multiclass strips away most of the things that make summoners cool (standard action summoning, minutes/level duration, eidolons with buckets of evolution points) so I can’t recommend it too highly. Magical child might be the better bet.
      * Gunslinger: Bad, bad, bad. Just dip 5 levels of gunslinger if you’re so horny for firearms.
      * Witch: Oof. You’re stuck with one 1st-level hex until 15th level. Bad variant multiclass: change my mind.
________________

---

# VIG 660: Magic Items

VIG 660: Magic Items


        I used to go into much greater depth on wondrous items, unique weapons, unique armors, etc. in my guides, but that all changed when I finished The Armamentarium. Simply put, this is a set of three documents for each of the types of magical item listed above. In addition to giving a mechanics-only shorthand with consistent formatting for every item in the game (well, slotted item, anyway) The Armamentarium also adds tags to each item so that you can filter it by use case. Some tags that vigilantes are particularly interested in:


      * [Vigilante]: Duh.
      * [Charisma], [Strength], [Dexterity]: Your main attack and save DC stats should need no explanation.
      * [Cleave], [Crit], [Finesse], [Improvised], [Melee], [Mounted], [Natural], [Nonlethal], [Ranged], [Reach], [Toxin], [TWF], [Unarmed]: Since there’s no such thing as a “standard” vigilante combat build, it seems reasonable to throw just about every tag into the mix here.
      * [ResDivination], [Anticaster]: Remember how annoying spellcasters are at figuring out that you’re not what you appear to be? Items with these tags will help alleviate that annoyance.
      * [Face], [Interrogation], [Bluff], [Diplomacy], [Intimidate], [Sense Motive]: Great tags for anyone who needs to deal with social interaction regularly. You have a Charisma focus and tons of social talents that lend themselves to this role, so prepare for that “anyone” to be you.
      * [Infiltration], [Scouting], [Mobility], [Acrobatics], [Disable Device], [Escape Artist], [Perception], [Stealth]: For moving around unseen or getting into places you’re not supposed to be.
      * [Intrigue], [Disguise], [Sleight of Hand]: For all-around ways to mess with enemies’ minds and spring clever traps.
      * [Ambush]: A key tag for stalker vigilantes, since it usually deals with how to get sneak attack or hidden strike damage more effectively.
      * [Disease], [Poison], [Tank], [Fortitude]: Generally good defensive tags for the vigilante, with their weak Fortitude save.
      * [Feint], [Grapple], [Steal], etc.: Many vigilantes go in for combat maneuver builds; these are your tags.

---

# Weapon Enchantments

Weapon Enchantments


Gold Cost


Sniping / Improved / Greater (CL 5/10/15, 1,875/7,500/16,875 gp): Even though vigilantes aren’t set up at all for ranged builds, stalkers can still derive great benefit from sniping weapons. Remember that the 5-rank benefit of Signature Skill: Stealth and the Expert Sniper feat will each reduce the penalties for sniping by 10, as well. If you’re going to snipe, you can’t be taking any penalties on your Stealth checks.


Lesser Concealed / Concealed (CL 5/10, 3,000/7,500 gp): Whereas shrinking weapons remain identifiable as weapons when “concealed,” lesser concealed and concealed weapons actually turn into other things: lesser concealed weapons into things of approximately the same size as the original weapon, and concealed weapons into things much smaller than the original weapon. You’re free to pick among these three enchantments which you need/want, but I’d recommend at least one.
Glamered (CL 10, 4,000 gp): Glamered weapons only appear to be something different than what they are, but since true seeing is the only way of penetrating that illusion, you’re probably safe from most enemies. I still think I’d rather go lesser concealed than glamered. Do note that glamered is the only way to conceal a ranged weapon, though.
Shrinking (CL 5, 1,000 gp): Because of their talents for infiltration and ambush, vigilantes will often need to conceal weapons. Shrinking is an exceptionally cheap weapon that can turn a naginata into a dinner fork, a greatsword into a dagger, or a greataxe into a small camping hatchet. Should make it much easier to conceal via Sleight of Hand, even if it’s clearly identifiable as a weapon of some kind. Keep in mind that the hidden magic social talent will conceal the magical aura of a shrinking weapon, making it look exactly like a mundane piece of cutlery under even magical scrutiny.




+1 Bonus


Agile (CL 7): Don’t want to burn a vigilante talent on lethal grace? An agile weapon + Weapon Finesse is your best bet, since Weapon Focus + [Weapon] Grace is two feats that feat-strapped vigilantes don’t particularly want to take.
Bane (CL 8): In certain campaigns, a bane weapon is practically required. Who’s going to play Giantslayer without taking bane (humanoid [giant])?
Cruel (CL 5): Vigilantes, especially stalkers with twisting fear, can often make a lot of hay with demoralization tactics; a cruel weapon simply amplifies the debuffing that you get from that process.
Keen (CL 10): A classic. Stalkers may not value crits quite as much as avengers do, but they can always move into Butterfly’s Sting to pass them off to more martially minded colleagues, if that’s appealing.
Training (CL 3): How many places can you buy feats for gold? Getting a training weapon is one way. Useful if you need to squeeze just a little more out of your build to bring some giant, complicated feat chain to fruition.
Veering (CL 5): A seeking veering ranged weapon is, like, 90% of the way to Improved Precise Shot, and that’s a dang good benefit for ranged vigilantes. Highly recommended.


Distracting / Greater Distracting (CL 5 / 8): Vigilantes don’t have access to the full complement of fighter feats (Spellbreaker, predominantly) that would be required to really chew up spellcasters, but distracting is still a great investment with Disruptive to force difficult concentration checks on enemies.
Fortuitous (CL 8): Some vigilantes, especially those with vital punishment, vigilante’s reflexes, or the snake animal patron specialize in taking attacks of opportunity. The fortuitous enchantment will grant you a second attack of opportunity 1/round at a -5 penalty. Great stuff!
Merciful (CL 5): Enforcer + a merciful weapon is a classic combo for debuffing with Intimidate. Assuming your GM is okay with the cognitive dissonance of having a cruel merciful weapon, you can do even more debuffing with that setup.


Benevolent (CL 5): A special call-out for bellflower harvesters is necessary, since this is an incredible—mandatory, even—enchantment for them.
PSFG Dueling / Leveraging (CL 7 / 6): Hey! Do you want insane bonuses to your CMB for certain combat maneuvers? Yeah, I thought you might. If you’re bull rushing, dragging, disarming, tripping, repositioning, or dirty tricking, you need these enchantments.
Ghost Touch (CL 9): Stalker vigilantes are rogue-likes, and like all rogue-likes, they depend on precision damage to win combats. Any guesses what type of damage incorporeal creatures are immune to? That’s right, precision damage from non-ghost touch weapons. I wouldn’t try to slap ghost touch on your main weapon—get a +1 ghost touch dagger or something similar and use it as a backup when you need it.




+2 Bonus


Truthful (CL 11): A choice anticaster enchantment. Blur? Displacement? Mirror image? Nuh-uh.


Distracting, Greater (CL 8): As distracting. A great anticaster enchantment.
Phase Locking (CL 7): Prevents outsiders and spellcasters from teleporting away when they get tired of a fight. Stay here, dammit!


Silencing (CL 8): Probably a trap for most vigilantes, since silent dispatch is so demonstrably superior.




Armor Enchantments

---

# Gold Cost

Gold Cost


Glamered (CL 10, 2,700 gp): As with glamered weaponry, glamered armor appears to be entirely mundane on casual (or even fairly motivated) inspection. Especially with the many guises social talent line, where you could conceivably be walking around as a common menial much of the time, you don’t want to forego the protection of your armor. Mandatory for any infiltration specialist.
Shadow / Slick (CL 5/4, 3,750 gp): Shadow and slick armor provide cheap (and, in the case of the improved and greater versions) numerically huge competence bonuses to Stealth and Escape Artist, two key skills for stalker vigilantes in particular. If you’re Dexterity-based, you need these, 100%.


Deceiving (CL 7, 5,000 gp): Yet another excellent infiltration enchantment. Want to be a chaotic evil character who always detects as lawful good? With deceiving armor, you can.


Hosteling (CL 9, 7,500 gp): Mounted furies can’t take their equine amigos everywhere; hosteling armor gives you free room and board while you squeeze through doorways and tight passages.
Malevolent (CL 7, 5,000 gp): Staggeringly effective for Shatter Defenses builds, which regularly make enemies flat-footed. Worth considering also for stalker vigilantes focusing on ambush tactics—those big attack bonuses are tasty.




+1 Bonus


Bolstering (CL 5): Damage an enemy, gain a +2 competence bonus to all saves against their abilities for a round. An easy way to keep your defenses in tip-top shape.
Defiant (CL 8): The armor equivalent of a bane weapon, and similarly good in campaigns that feature one enemy type to the exclusion of all others.
________________
VIG 801: Sample Builds
